The fortune she would inherit struck him as a\r\nvery sufficient provision for two reasonable persons, and if a penniless\r\nswain who could give a good account of himself should enter the lists, he\r\nshould be judged quite upon his personal merits.  There were other things\r\nbesides.  The Doctor thought it very vulgar to be precipitate in accusing\r\npeople of mercenary motives, inasmuch as his door had as yet not been in\r\nthe least besieged by fortune-hunters; and, lastly, he was very curious\r\nto see whether Catherine might really be loved for her moral worth.  He\r\nsmiled as he reflected that poor Mr. Townsend had been only twice to the\r\nhouse, and he said to Mrs. Penniman that the next time he should come she\r\nmust ask him to dinner.\r\n\r\nHe came very soon again, and Mrs. Penniman had of course great pleasure\r\nin executing this mission.  Morris Townsend accepted her invitation with\r\nequal good grace, and the dinner took place a few days later.  The Doctor\r\nhad said to himself, justly enough, that they must not have the young man\r\nalone; this would partake too much of the nature of encouragement.  So\r\ntwo or three other persons were invited; but Morris Townsend, though he\r\nwas by no means the ostensible, was the real, occasion of the feast.\r\nThere is every reason to suppose that he desired to make a good\r\nimpression; and if he fell short of this result, it was not for want of a\r\ngood deal of intelligent effort.  The Doctor talked to him very little\r\nduring dinner; but he observed him attentively, and after the ladies had\r\ngone out he pushed him the wine and asked him several questions.  Morris\r\nwas not a young man who needed to be pressed, and he found quite enough\r\nencouragement in the superior quality of the claret.  The Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s wine\r\nwas admirable, and it may be communicated to the reader that while he\r\nsipped it Morris reflected that a cellar-full of good liquor\xe2\x80\x94there was\r\nevidently a cellar-full here\xe2\x80\x94would be a most attractive idiosyncrasy in a\r\nfather-in-law.  The Doctor was struck with his appreciative guest; he saw\r\nthat he was not a commonplace young man.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe has ability,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s father, \xe2\x80\x9cdecided ability; he has a very good head if he\r\nchooses to use it.  And he is uncommonly well turned out; quite the sort\r\nof figure that pleases the ladies.  But I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I like him.\xe2\x80\x9d  The\r\nDoctor, however, kept his reflexions to himself, and talked to his\r\nvisitors about foreign lands, concerning which Morris offered him more\r\ninformation than he was ready, as he mentally phrased it, to swallow.\r\nDr. Sloper had travelled but little, and he took the liberty of not\r\nbelieving everything this anecdotical idler narrated.  He prided himself\r\non being something of a physiognomist, and while the young man, chatting\r\nwith easy assurance, puffed his cigar and filled his glass again, the\r\nDoctor sat with his eyes quietly fixed on his bright, expressive face.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has the assurance of the devil himself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris\xe2\x80\x99s host; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I ever saw such assurance.  And his powers of invention are most\r\nremarkable.  He is very knowing; they were not so knowing as that in my\r\ntime.  And a good head, did I say?  I should think so\xe2\x80\x94after a bottle of\r\nMadeira and a bottle and a half of claret!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter dinner Morris Townsend went and stood before Catherine, who was\r\nstanding before the fire in her red satin gown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like me\xe2\x80\x94he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like me at all!\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour father; extraordinary man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, blushing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel; I am very quick to feel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you are mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well; you ask him and you will see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather not ask him, if there is any danger of his saying what\r\nyou think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris looked at her with an air of mock melancholy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give you any pleasure to contradict him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never contradict him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you hear me abused without opening your lips in my defence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father won\xe2\x80\x99t abuse you.  He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know you enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris Townsend gave a loud laugh, and Catherine began to blush again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never mention you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, to take refuge from her confusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very well; but it is not quite what I should have liked you to\r\nsay.  I should have liked you to say: \xe2\x80\x98If my father doesn\xe2\x80\x99t think well of\r\nyou, what does it matter?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but it would matter; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t say that!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl exclaimed.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her for a moment, smiling a little; and the Doctor, if he\r\nhad been watching him just then, would have seen a gleam of fine\r\nimpatience in the sociable softness of his eye.  But there was no\r\nimpatience in his rejoinder\xe2\x80\x94none, at least, save what was expressed in a\r\nlittle appealing sigh.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, well, then, I must not give up the hope of\r\nbringing him round!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe expressed it more frankly to Mrs. Penniman later in the evening.  But\r\nbefore that he sang two or three songs at Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s timid request; not\r\nthat he flattered himself that this would help to bring her father round.\r\nHe had a sweet, light tenor voice, and when he had finished every one\r\nmade some exclamation\xe2\x80\x94every one, that is, save Catherine, who remained\r\nintensely silent.  Mrs. Penniman declared that his manner of singing was\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmost artistic,\xe2\x80\x9d and Dr. Sloper said it was \xe2\x80\x9cvery taking\xe2\x80\x94very taking\r\nindeed\xe2\x80\x9d; speaking loudly and distinctly, but with a certain dryness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like me\xe2\x80\x94he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like me at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris Townsend,\r\naddressing the aunt in the same manner as he had done the niece.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nthinks I\xe2\x80\x99m all wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnlike her niece, Mrs. Penniman asked for no explanation.  She only\r\nsmiled very sweetly, as if she understood everything; and, unlike\r\nCatherine too, she made no attempt to contradict him.  \xe2\x80\x9cPray, what does\r\nit matter?\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you say the right thing!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, greatly to the gratification\r\nof Mrs. Penniman, who prided herself on always saying the right thing.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor, the next time he saw his sister Elizabeth, let her know that\r\nhe had made the acquaintance of Lavinia\xe2\x80\x99s _prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhysically,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s uncommonly well set up.  As an anatomist, it\r\nis really a pleasure to me to see such a beautiful structure; although,\r\nif people were all like him, I suppose there would be very little need\r\nfor doctors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see anything in people but their bones?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond rejoined.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of him as a father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a father?  Thank Heaven I am not his father!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but you are Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s.  Lavinia tells me she is in love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must get over it.  He is not a gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, take care!  Remember that he is a branch of the Townsends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not what I call a gentleman.  He has not the soul of one.  He is\r\nextremely insinuating; but it\xe2\x80\x99s a vulgar nature.  I saw through it in a\r\nminute.  He is altogether too familiar\xe2\x80\x94I hate familiarity.  He is a\r\nplausible coxcomb.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond; \xe2\x80\x9cif you make up your mind so easily, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngreat advantage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t make up my mind easily.  What I tell you is the result of thirty\r\nyears of observation; and in order to be able to form that judgement in a\r\nsingle evening, I have had to spend a lifetime in study.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery possibly you are right.  But the thing is for Catherine to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will present her with a pair of spectacles!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nIF it were true that she was in love, she was certainly very quiet about\r\nit; but the Doctor was of course prepared to admit that her quietness\r\nmight mean volumes.  She had told Morris Townsend that she would not\r\nmention him to her father, and she saw no reason to retract this vow of\r\ndiscretion.  It was no more than decently civil, of course, that after\r\nhaving dined in Washington Square, Morris should call there again; and it\r\nwas no more than natural that, having been kindly received on this\r\noccasion, he should continue to present himself.  He had had plenty of\r\nleisure on his hands; and thirty years ago, in New York, a young man of\r\nleisure had reason to be thankful for aids to self-oblivion.  Catherine\r\nsaid nothing to her father about these visits, though they had rapidly\r\nbecome the most important, the most absorbing thing in her life.  The\r\ngirl was very happy.  She knew not as yet what would come of it; but the\r\npresent had suddenly grown rich and solemn.  If she had been told she was\r\nin love, she would have been a good deal surprised; for she had an idea\r\nthat love was an eager and exacting passion, and her own heart was filled\r\nin these days with the impulse of self-effacement and sacrifice.\r\nWhenever Morris Townsend had left the house, her imagination projected\r\nitself, with all its strength, into the idea of his soon coming back; but\r\nif she had been told at such a moment that he would not return for a\r\nyear, or even that he would never return, she would not have complained\r\nnor rebelled, but would have humbly accepted the decree, and sought for\r\nconsolation in thinking over the times she had already seen him, the\r\nwords he had spoken, the sound of his voice, of his tread, the expression\r\nof his face.  Love demands certain things as a right; but Catherine had\r\nno sense of her rights; she had only a consciousness of immense and\r\nunexpected favours.  Her very gratitude for these things had hushed\r\nitself; for it seemed to her that there would be something of impudence\r\nin making a festival of her secret.  Her father suspected Morris\r\nTownsend\xe2\x80\x99s visits, and noted her reserve.  She seemed to beg pardon for\r\nit; she looked at him constantly in silence, as if she meant to say that\r\nshe said nothing because she was afraid of irritating him.  But the poor\r\ngirl\xe2\x80\x99s dumb eloquence irritated him more than anything else would have\r\ndone, and he caught himself murmuring more than once that it was a\r\ngrievous pity his only child was a simpleton.  His murmurs, however, were\r\ninaudible; and for a while he said nothing to any one.  He would have\r\nliked to know exactly how often young Townsend came; but he had\r\ndetermined to ask no questions of the girl herself\xe2\x80\x94to say nothing more to\r\nher that would show that he watched her.  The Doctor had a great idea of\r\nbeing largely just: he wished to leave his daughter her liberty, and\r\ninterfere only when the danger should be proved.  It was not in his\r\nmanner to obtain information by indirect methods, and it never even\r\noccurred to him to question the servants.  As for Lavinia, he hated to\r\ntalk to her about the matter; she annoyed him with her mock romanticism.\r\nBut he had to come to this.  Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s convictions as regards the\r\nrelations of her niece and the clever young visitor who saved appearances\r\nby coming ostensibly for both the ladies\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s convictions had\r\npassed into a riper and richer phase.  There was to be no crudity in Mrs.\r\nPenniman\xe2\x80\x99s treatment of the situation; she had become as uncommunicative\r\nas Catherine herself.  She was tasting of the sweets of concealment; she\r\nhad taken up the line of mystery.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe would be enchanted to be able to\r\nprove to herself that she is persecuted,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor; and when at\r\nlast he questioned her, he was sure she would contrive to extract from\r\nhis words a pretext for this belief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe so good as to let me know what is going on in the house,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to\r\nher, in a tone which, under the circumstances, he himself deemed genial.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing on, Austin?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman exclaimed.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I am sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow!  I believe that last night the old grey cat had kittens!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt her age?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe idea is startling\xe2\x80\x94almost shocking.\r\nBe so good as to see that they are all drowned.  But what else has\r\nhappened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, the dear little kittens!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nthem drowned for the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer brother puffed his cigar a few moments in silence.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour sympathy\r\nwith kittens, Lavinia,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently resumed, \xe2\x80\x9carises from a feline\r\nelement in your own character.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCats are very graceful, and very clean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd very stealthy.  You are the embodiment both of grace and of\r\nneatness; but you are wanting in frankness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly are not, dear brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to be graceful, though I try to be neat.  Why haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou let me know that Mr. Morris Townsend is coming to the house four\r\ntimes a week?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman lifted her eyebrows.  \xe2\x80\x9cFour times a week?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive times, if you prefer it.  I am away all day, and I see nothing.\r\nBut when such things happen, you should let me know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, with her eyebrows still raised, reflected intently.  \xe2\x80\x9cDear\r\nAustin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cI am incapable of betraying a confidence.  I\r\nwould rather suffer anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever fear; you shall not suffer.  To whose confidence is it you allude?\r\nHas Catherine made you take a vow of eternal secrecy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy no means.  Catherine has not told me as much as she might.  She has\r\nnot been very trustful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the young man, then, who has made you his confidante?  Allow me to\r\nsay that it is extremely indiscreet of you to form secret alliances with\r\nyoung men.  You don\xe2\x80\x99t know where they may lead you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by an alliance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cI take\r\na great interest in Mr. Townsend; I won\xe2\x80\x99t conceal that.  But that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the circumstances, that is quite enough.  What is the source of\r\nyour interest in Mr. Townsend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, musing, and then breaking into her smile,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat he is so interesting!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor felt that he had need of his patience.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what makes him\r\ninteresting?\xe2\x80\x94his good looks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis misfortunes, Austin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, he has had misfortunes?  That, of course, is always interesting.\r\nAre you at liberty to mention a few of Mr. Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that he would like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe has told\r\nme a great deal about himself\xe2\x80\x94he has told me, in fact, his whole history.\r\nBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ought to repeat those things.  He would tell them to\r\nyou, I am sure, if he thought you would listen to him kindly.  With\r\nkindness you may do anything with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor gave a laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall request him very kindly, then, to\r\nleave Catherine alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, shaking her forefinger at her brother, with her\r\nlittle finger turned out, \xe2\x80\x9cCatherine had probably said something to him\r\nkinder than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaid that she loved him?  Do you mean that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman fixed her eyes on the floor.  \xe2\x80\x9cAs I tell you, Austin, she\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t confide in me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have an opinion, I suppose, all the same.  It is that I ask you for;\r\nthough I don\xe2\x80\x99t conceal from you that I shall not regard it as\r\nconclusive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s gaze continued to rest on the carpet; but at last she\r\nlifted it, and then her brother thought it very expressive.  \xe2\x80\x9cI think\r\nCatherine is very happy; that is all I can say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTownsend is trying to marry her\xe2\x80\x94is that what you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is greatly interested in her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe finds her such an attractive girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine has a lovely nature, Austin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, \xe2\x80\x9cand Mr.\r\nTownsend has had the intelligence to discover that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a little help from you, I suppose.  My dear Lavinia,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the\r\nDoctor, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are an admirable aunt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Mr. Townsend says,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Lavinia, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he is sincere?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn saying that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; that\xe2\x80\x99s of course.  But in his admiration for Catherine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeeply sincere.  He has said to me the most appreciative, the most\r\ncharming things about her.  He would say them to you, if he were sure you\r\nwould listen to him\xe2\x80\x94gently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI doubt whether I can undertake it.  He appears to require a great deal\r\nof gentleness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a sympathetic, sensitive nature,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\nHer brother puffed his cigar again in silence.  \xe2\x80\x9cThese delicate qualities\r\nhave survived his vicissitudes, eh?  All this while you haven\xe2\x80\x99t told me\r\nabout his misfortunes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a long story,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I regard it as a sacred\r\ntrust.  But I suppose there is no objection to my saying that he has been\r\nwild\xe2\x80\x94he frankly confesses that.  But he has paid for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what has impoverished him, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean simply in money.  He is very much alone in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that he has behaved so badly that his friends have given him\r\nup?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has had false friends, who have deceived and betrayed him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seems to have some good ones too.  He has a devoted sister, and\r\nhalf-a-dozen nephews and nieces.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was silent a minute.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe nephews and nieces are children,\r\nand the sister is not a very attractive person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t abuse her to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I am told he\r\nlives upon her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLives upon her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLives with her, and does nothing for himself; it is about the same\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is looking for a position\xe2\x80\x94most earnestly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nhopes every day to find one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely.  He is looking for it here\xe2\x80\x94over there in the front parlour.\r\nThe position of husband of a weak-minded woman with a large fortune would\r\nsuit him to perfection!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was truly amiable, but she now gave signs of temper.  She\r\nrose with much animation, and stood for a moment looking at her brother.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Austin,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cif you regard Catherine as a weak-minded\r\nwoman, you are particularly mistaken!\xe2\x80\x9d  And with this she moved\r\nmajestically away.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was a regular custom with the family in Washington Square to go and\r\nspend Sunday evening at Mrs. Almond\xe2\x80\x99s.  On the Sunday after the\r\nconversation I have just narrated, this custom was not intermitted and on\r\nthis occasion, towards the middle of the evening, Dr. Sloper found reason\r\nto withdraw to the library, with his brother-in-law, to talk over a\r\nmatter of business.  He was absent some twenty minutes, and when he came\r\nback into the circle, which was enlivened by the presence of several\r\nfriends of the family, he saw that Morris Townsend had come in and had\r\nlost as little time as possible in seating himself on a small sofa,\r\nbeside Catherine.  In the large room, where several different groups had\r\nbeen formed, and the hum of voices and of laughter was loud, these two\r\nyoung persons might confabulate, as the Doctor phrased it to himself,\r\nwithout attracting attention.  He saw in a moment, however, that his\r\ndaughter was painfully conscious of his own observation.  She sat\r\nmotionless, with her eyes bent down, staring at her open fan, deeply\r\nflushed, shrinking together as if to minimise the indiscretion of which\r\nshe confessed herself guilty.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor almost pitied her.  Poor Catherine was not defiant; she had no\r\ngenius for bravado; and as she felt that her father viewed her\r\ncompanion\xe2\x80\x99s attentions with an unsympathising eye, there was nothing but\r\ndiscomfort for her in the accident of seeming to challenge him.  The\r\nDoctor felt, indeed, so sorry for her that he turned away, to spare her\r\nthe sense of being watched; and he was so intelligent a man that, in his\r\nthoughts, he rendered a sort of poetic justice to her situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be deucedly pleasant for a plain inanimate girl like that to\r\nhave a beautiful young fellow come and sit down beside her and whisper to\r\nher that he is her slave\xe2\x80\x94if that is what this one whispers.  No wonder\r\nshe likes it, and that she thinks me a cruel tyrant; which of course she\r\ndoes, though she is afraid\xe2\x80\x94she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t the animation necessary\xe2\x80\x94to admit it\r\nto herself.  Poor old Catherine!\xe2\x80\x9d mused the Doctor; \xe2\x80\x9cI verily believe she\r\nis capable of defending me when Townsend abuses me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the force of this reflexion, for the moment, was such in making him\r\nfeel the natural opposition between his point of view and that of an\r\ninfatuated child, that he said to himself that he was perhaps, after all,\r\ntaking things too hard and crying out before he was hurt.  He must not\r\ncondemn Morris Townsend unheard.  He had a great aversion to taking\r\nthings too hard; he thought that half the discomfort and many of the\r\ndisappointments of life come from it; and for an instant he asked himself\r\nwhether, possibly, he did not appear ridiculous to this intelligent young\r\nman, whose private perception of incongruities he suspected of being\r\nkeen.  At the end of a quarter of an hour Catherine had got rid of him,\r\nand Townsend was now standing before the fireplace in conversation with\r\nMrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will try him again,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  And he crossed the room and\r\njoined his sister and her companion, making her a sign that she should\r\nleave the young man to him.  She presently did so, while Morris looked at\r\nhim, smiling, without a sign of evasiveness in his affable eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s amazingly conceited!\xe2\x80\x9d thought the Doctor; and then he said aloud:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am told you are looking out for a position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a position is more than I should presume to call it,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris\r\nTownsend answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat sounds so fine.  I should like some quiet\r\nwork\xe2\x80\x94something to turn an honest penny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of thing should you prefer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean what am I fit for?  Very little, I am afraid.  I have\r\nnothing but my good right arm, as they say in the melodramas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too modest,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cIn addition to your good right\r\narm, you have your subtle brain.  I know nothing of you but what I see;\r\nbut I see by your physiognomy that you are extremely intelligent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d Townsend murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to answer when you say that!\r\nYou advise me, then, not to despair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he looked at his interlocutor as if the question might have a double\r\nmeaning.  The Doctor caught the look and weighed it a moment before he\r\nreplied.  \xe2\x80\x9cI should be very sorry to admit that a robust and\r\nwell-disposed young man need ever despair.  If he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t succeed in one\r\nthing, he can try another.  Only, I should add, he should choose his line\r\nwith discretion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, with discretion,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris Townsend repeated sympathetically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I have been indiscreet, formerly; but I think I have got over it.\r\nI am very steady now.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he stood a moment, looking down at his\r\nremarkably neat shoes.  Then at last, \xe2\x80\x9cWere you kindly intending to\r\npropose something for my advantage?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, looking up and smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn his impudence!\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor exclaimed privately.  But in a moment he\r\nreflected that he himself had, after all, touched first upon this\r\ndelicate point, and that his words might have been construed as an offer\r\nof assistance.  \xe2\x80\x9cI have no particular proposal to make,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it occurred to me to let you know that I have you in my mind.\r\nSometimes one hears of opportunities.  For instance\xe2\x80\x94should you object to\r\nleaving New York\xe2\x80\x94to going to a distance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be able to manage that.  I must seek my fortune\r\nhere or nowhere.  You see,\xe2\x80\x9d added Morris Townsend, \xe2\x80\x9cI have ties\xe2\x80\x94I have\r\nresponsibilities here.  I have a sister, a widow, from whom I have been\r\nseparated for a long time, and to whom I am almost everything.  I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to say to her that I must leave her.  She rather depends\r\nupon me, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that\xe2\x80\x99s very proper; family feeling is very proper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Sloper.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI often think there is not enough of it in our city.  I think I have\r\nheard of your sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is possible, but I rather doubt it; she lives so very quietly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs quietly, you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor went on, with a short laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cas a\r\nlady may do who has several young children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my little nephews and nieces\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the very point!  I am helping to\r\nbring them up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris Townsend.  \xe2\x80\x9cI am a kind of amateur tutor; I\r\ngive them lessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s very proper, as I say; but it is hardly a career.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t make my fortune!\xe2\x80\x9d the young man confessed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not be too much bent on a fortune,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nassure you I will keep you in mind; I won\xe2\x80\x99t lose sight of you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf my situation becomes desperate I shall perhaps take the liberty of\r\nreminding you!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris rejoined, raising his voice a little, with a\r\nbrighter smile, as his interlocutor turned away.\r\n\r\nBefore he left the house the Doctor had a few words with Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see his sister,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call her?  Mrs.\r\nMontgomery.  I should like to have a little talk with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try and manage it,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond responded.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will take the\r\nfirst opportunity of inviting her, and you shall come and meet her.\r\nUnless, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond added, \xe2\x80\x9cshe first takes it into her head to\r\nbe sick and to send for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh no, not that; she must have trouble enough without that.  But it\r\nwould have its advantages, for then I should see the children.  I should\r\nlike very much to see the children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very thorough.  Do you want to catechise them about their\r\nuncle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely.  Their uncle tells me he has charge of their education, that\r\nhe saves their mother the expense of school-bills.  I should like to ask\r\nthem a few questions in the commoner branches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe certainly has not the cut of a schoolmaster!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond said to\r\nherself a short time afterwards, as she saw Morris Townsend in a corner\r\nbending over her niece, who was seated.\r\n\r\nAnd there was, indeed, nothing in the young man\xe2\x80\x99s discourse at this\r\nmoment that savoured of the pedagogue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you meet me somewhere to-morrow or next day?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a low\r\ntone, to Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeet you?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, lifting her frightened eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have something particular to say to you\xe2\x80\x94very particular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you come to the house?  Can\xe2\x80\x99t you say it there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTownsend shook his head gloomily.  \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t enter your doors again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Townsend!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Catherine.  She trembled as she wondered\r\nwhat had happened, whether her father had forbidden it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t in self-respect,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour father has insulted\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInsulted you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has taunted me with my poverty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are mistaken\xe2\x80\x94you misunderstood him!\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine spoke with\r\nenergy, getting up from her chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I am too proud\xe2\x80\x94too sensitive.  But would you have me otherwise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe asked tenderly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere my father is concerned, you must not be sure.  He is full of\r\ngoodness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe laughed at me for having no position!  I took it quietly; but only\r\nbecause he belongs to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what he thinks.  I am sure\r\nhe means to be kind.  You must not be too proud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will be proud only of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cWill you meet me in the\r\nSquare in the afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA great blush on Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s part had been the answer to the declaration\r\nI have just quoted.  She turned away, heedless of his question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you meet me?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very quiet there; no one need\r\nsee us\xe2\x80\x94toward dusk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is you who are unkind, it is you who laugh, when you say such things\r\nas that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear girl!\xe2\x80\x9d the young man murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know how little there is in me to be proud of.  I am ugly and\r\nstupid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris greeted this remark with an ardent murmur, in which she recognised\r\nnothing articulate but an assurance that she was his own dearest.\r\n\r\nBut she went on.  \xe2\x80\x9cI am not even\xe2\x80\x94I am not even\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d  And she paused a\r\nmoment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not even brave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, then, if you are afraid, what shall we do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated a while; then at last\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cYou must come to the house,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cI am not afraid of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather it were in the Square,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man urged.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou know\r\nhow empty it is, often.  No one will see us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care who sees us!  But leave me now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe left her resignedly; he had got what he wanted.  Fortunately he was\r\nignorant that half an hour later, going home with her father and feeling\r\nhim near, the poor girl, in spite of her sudden declaration of courage,\r\nbegan to tremble again.  Her father said nothing; but she had an idea his\r\neyes were fixed upon her in the darkness.  Mrs. Penniman also was silent;\r\nMorris Townsend had told her that her niece preferred, unromantically, an\r\ninterview in a chintz-covered parlour to a sentimental tryst beside a\r\nfountain sheeted with dead leaves, and she was lost in wonderment at the\r\noddity\xe2\x80\x94almost the perversity\xe2\x80\x94of the choice.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nCATHERINE received the young man the next day on the ground she had\r\nchosen\xe2\x80\x94amid the chaste upholstery of a New York drawing-room furnished in\r\nthe fashion of fifty years ago.  Morris had swallowed his pride and made\r\nthe effort necessary to cross the threshold of her too derisive parent\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nact of magnanimity which could not fail to render him doubly interesting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must settle something\xe2\x80\x94we must take a line,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared, passing his\r\nhand through his hair and giving a glance at the long narrow mirror which\r\nadorned the space between the two windows, and which had at its base a\r\nlittle gilded bracket covered by a thin slab of white marble, supporting\r\nin its turn a backgammon board folded together in the shape of two\r\nvolumes, two shining folios inscribed in letters of greenish gilt,\r\n_History of England_.  If Morris had been pleased to describe the master\r\nof the house as a heartless scoffer, it is because he thought him too\r\nmuch on his guard, and this was the easiest way to express his own\r\ndissatisfaction\xe2\x80\x94a dissatisfaction which he had made a point of concealing\r\nfrom the Doctor.  It will probably seem to the reader, however, that the\r\nDoctor\xe2\x80\x99s vigilance was by no means excessive, and that these two young\r\npeople had an open field.  Their intimacy was now considerable, and it\r\nmay appear that for a shrinking and retiring person our heroine had been\r\nliberal of her favours.  The young man, within a few days, had made her\r\nlisten to things for which she had not supposed that she was prepared;\r\nhaving a lively foreboding of difficulties, he proceeded to gain as much\r\nground as possible in the present.  He remembered that fortune favours\r\nthe brave, and even if he had forgotten it, Mrs. Penniman would have\r\nremembered it for him.  Mrs. Penniman delighted of all things in a drama,\r\nand she flattered herself that a drama would now be enacted.  Combining\r\nas she did the zeal of the prompter with the impatience of the spectator,\r\nshe had long since done her utmost to pull up the curtain.  She too\r\nexpected to figure in the performance\xe2\x80\x94to be the confidante, the Chorus,\r\nto speak the epilogue.  It may even be said that there were times when\r\nshe lost sight altogether of the modest heroine of the play, in the\r\ncontemplation of certain great passages which would naturally occur\r\nbetween the hero and herself.\r\n\r\nWhat Morris had told Catherine at last was simply that he loved her, or\r\nrather adored her.  Virtually, he had made known as much already\xe2\x80\x94his\r\nvisits had been a series of eloquent intimations of it.  But now he had\r\naffirmed it in lover\xe2\x80\x99s vows, and, as a memorable sign of it, he had\r\npassed his arm round the girl\xe2\x80\x99s waist and taken a kiss.  This happy\r\ncertitude had come sooner than Catherine expected, and she had regarded\r\nit, very naturally, as a priceless treasure.  It may even be doubted\r\nwhether she had ever definitely expected to possess it; she had not been\r\nwaiting for it, and she had never said to herself that at a given moment\r\nit must come.  As I have tried to explain, she was not eager and\r\nexacting; she took what was given her from day to day; and if the\r\ndelightful custom of her lover\xe2\x80\x99s visits, which yielded her a happiness in\r\nwhich confidence and timidity were strangely blended, had suddenly come\r\nto an end, she would not only not have spoken of herself as one of the\r\nforsaken, but she would not have thought of herself as one of the\r\ndisappointed.  After Morris had kissed her, the last time he was with\r\nher, as a ripe assurance of his devotion, she begged him to go away, to\r\nleave her alone, to let her think.  Morris went away, taking another kiss\r\nfirst.  But Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s meditations had lacked a certain coherence.  She\r\nfelt his kisses on her lips and on her cheeks for a long time afterwards;\r\nthe sensation was rather an obstacle than an aid to reflexion.  She would\r\nhave liked to see her situation all clearly before her, to make up her\r\nmind what she should do if, as she feared, her father should tell her\r\nthat he disapproved of Morris Townsend.  But all that she could see with\r\nany vividness was that it was terribly strange that anyone should\r\ndisapprove of him; that there must in that case be some mistake, some\r\nmystery, which in a little while would be set at rest.  She put off\r\ndeciding and choosing; before the vision of a conflict with her father\r\nshe dropped her eyes and sat motionless, holding her breath and waiting.\r\nIt made her heart beat, it was intensely painful.  When Morris kissed her\r\nand said these things\xe2\x80\x94that also made her heart beat; but this was worse,\r\nand it frightened her.  Nevertheless, to-day, when the young man spoke of\r\nsettling something, taking a line, she felt that it was the truth, and\r\nshe answered very simply and without hesitating.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must do our duty,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cwe must speak to my father.  I will do\r\nit to-night; you must do it to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very good of you to do it first,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe young\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94the happy lover\xe2\x80\x94generally does that.  But just as you please!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt pleased Catherine to think that she should be brave for his sake, and\r\nin her satisfaction she even gave a little smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cWomen have more\r\ntact,\xe2\x80\x9d she said \xe2\x80\x9cthey ought to do it first.  They are more conciliating;\r\nthey can persuade better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will need all your powers of persuasion.  But, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris\r\nadded, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are irresistible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t speak that way\xe2\x80\x94and promise me this.  To-morrow, when you\r\ntalk with father, you will be very gentle and respectful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs much so as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris promised.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t be much use, but I\r\nshall try.  I certainly would rather have you easily than have to fight\r\nfor you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk about fighting; we shall not fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, we must be prepared,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris rejoined; \xe2\x80\x9cyou especially, because for\r\nyou it must come hardest.  Do you know the first thing your father will\r\nsay to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Morris; please tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will tell you I am mercenary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercenary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a big word; but it means a low thing.  It means that I am after\r\nyour money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Catherine softly.\r\n\r\nThe exclamation was so deprecating and touching that Morris indulged in\r\nanother little demonstration of affection.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut he will be sure to say\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be easy to be prepared for that,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nsimply say that he is mistaken\xe2\x80\x94that other men may be that way, but that\r\nyou are not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must make a great point of that, for it will be his own great\r\npoint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine looked at her lover a minute, and then she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\npersuade him.  But I am glad we shall be rich,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\nMorris turned away, looking into the crown of his hat.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nmisfortune,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is from that our difficulty will\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if it is the worst misfortune, we are not so unhappy.  Many people\r\nwould not think it so bad.  I will persuade him, and after that we shall\r\nbe very glad we have money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris Townsend listened to this robust logic in silence.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will leave\r\nmy defence to you; it\xe2\x80\x99s a charge that a man has to stoop to defend\r\nhimself from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine on her side was silent for a while; she was looking at him\r\nwhile he looked, with a good deal of fixedness, out of the window.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMorris,\xe2\x80\x9d she said abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9care you very sure you love me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned round, and in a moment he was bending over her.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy own\r\ndearest, can you doubt it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only known it five days,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut now it seems to me as if\r\nI could never do without it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will never be called upon to try!\xe2\x80\x9d  And he gave a little tender,\r\nreassuring laugh.  Then, in a moment, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cThere is something you\r\nmust tell me, too.\xe2\x80\x9d  She had closed her eyes after the last word she\r\nuttered, and kept them closed; and at this she nodded her head, without\r\nopening them.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou must tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if your father is\r\ndead against me, if he absolutely forbids our marriage, you will still be\r\nfaithful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine opened her eyes, gazing at him, and she could give no better\r\npromise than what he read there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will cleave to me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou know you are your own\r\nmistress\xe2\x80\x94you are of age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Morris!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, for all answer.  Or rather not for all; for\r\nshe put her hand into his own.  He kept it a while, and presently he\r\nkissed her again.  This is all that need be recorded of their\r\nconversation; but Mrs. Penniman, if she had been present, would probably\r\nhave admitted that it was as well it had not taken place beside the\r\nfountain in Washington Square.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\n\r\nCATHERINE listened for her father when he came in that evening, and she\r\nheard him go to his study.  She sat quiet, though her heart was beating\r\nfast, for nearly half an hour; then she went and knocked at his door\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nceremony without which she never crossed the threshold of this apartment.\r\nOn entering it now she found him in his chair beside the fire,\r\nentertaining himself with a cigar and the evening paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have something to say to you,\xe2\x80\x9d she began very gently; and she sat down\r\nin the first place that offered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be very happy to hear it, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father.  He\r\nwaited\xe2\x80\x94waited, looking at her, while she stared, in a long silence, at\r\nthe fire.  He was curious and impatient, for he was sure she was going to\r\nspeak of Morris Townsend; but he let her take her own time, for he was\r\ndetermined to be very mild.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am engaged to be married!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine announced at last, still staring\r\nat the fire.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor was startled; the accomplished fact was more than he had\r\nexpected.  But he betrayed no surprise.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou do right to tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsimply said.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is the happy mortal whom you have honoured with\r\nyour choice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Morris Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d  And as she pronounced her lover\xe2\x80\x99s name, Catherine\r\nlooked at him.  What she saw was her father\xe2\x80\x99s still grey eye and his\r\nclear-cut, definite smile.  She contemplated these objects for a moment,\r\nand then she looked back at the fire; it was much warmer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen was this arrangement made?\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis afternoon\xe2\x80\x94two hours ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas Mr. Townsend here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, father; in the front parlour.\xe2\x80\x9d  She was very glad that she was not\r\nobliged to tell him that the ceremony of their betrothal had taken place\r\nout there under the bare ailantus-trees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it serious?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery serious, father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer father was silent a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Townsend ought to have told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe means to tell you to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter I know all about it from you?  He ought to have told me before.\r\nDoes he think I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care\xe2\x80\x94because I left you so much liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9che knew you would care.  And we have been so\r\nmuch obliged to you for\xe2\x80\x94for the liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor gave a short laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou might have made a better use of it,\r\nCatherine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say that, father,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl urged softly, fixing her dull\r\nand gentle eyes upon him.\r\n\r\nHe puffed his cigar awhile, meditatively.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou have gone very fast,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered simply; \xe2\x80\x9cI think we have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer father glanced at her an instant, removing his eyes from the fire.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder Mr. Townsend likes you.  You are so simple and so good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why it is\xe2\x80\x94but he _does_ like me.  I am sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you very fond of Mr. Townsend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like him very much, of course\xe2\x80\x94or I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t consent to marry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have known him a very short time, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, with some eagerness, \xe2\x80\x9cit doesn\xe2\x80\x99t take long to like\r\na person\xe2\x80\x94when once you begin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have begun very quickly.  Was it the first time you saw\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94that night at your aunt\xe2\x80\x99s party?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, father,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you about\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course; that\xe2\x80\x99s your own affair.  You will have observed that I have\r\nacted on that principle.  I have not interfered, I have left you your\r\nliberty, I have remembered that you are no longer a little girl\xe2\x80\x94that you\r\nhave arrived at years of discretion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel very old\xe2\x80\x94and very wise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, smiling faintly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid that before long you will feel older and wiser yet.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike your engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine exclaimed softly, getting up from her chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my dear.  I am sorry to give you pain; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it.  You\r\nshould have consulted me before you settled it.  I have been too easy\r\nwith you, and I feel as if you had taken advantage of my indulgence.\r\nMost decidedly, you should have spoken to me first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine hesitated a moment, and then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cIt was because I was afraid you\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it!\xe2\x80\x9d she confessed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there it is!  You had a bad conscience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I have not a bad conscience, father!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl cried out, with\r\nconsiderable energy.  \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t accuse me of anything so dreadful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThese words, in fact, represented to her imagination something very\r\nterrible indeed, something base and cruel, which she associated with\r\nmalefactors and prisoners.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt was because I was afraid\xe2\x80\x94afraid\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwent on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you were afraid, it was because you had been foolish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid you didn\xe2\x80\x99t like Mr. Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were quite right.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t like him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear father, you don\xe2\x80\x99t know him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, in a voice so timidly\r\nargumentative that it might have touched him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know him intimately.  But I know him enough.  I have\r\nmy impression of him.  You don\xe2\x80\x99t know him either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stood before the fire, with her hands lightly clasped in front of\r\nher; and her father, leaning back in his chair and looking up at her,\r\nmade this remark with a placidity that might have been irritating.\r\n\r\nI doubt, however, whether Catherine was irritated, though she broke into\r\na vehement protest.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know him?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I know\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94better than I have ever known any one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know a part of him\xe2\x80\x94what he has chosen to show you.  But you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rest?  What is the rest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever it may be.  There is sure to be plenty of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, remembering how Morris had\r\nforewarned her.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that he is mercenary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer father looked up at her still, with his cold, quiet reasonable eye.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I meant it, my dear, I should say it!  But there is an error I wish\r\nparticularly to avoid\xe2\x80\x94that of rendering Mr. Townsend more interesting to\r\nyou by saying hard things about him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t think them hard if they are true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t, you will be a remarkably sensible young woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will be your reasons, at any rate, and you will want me to hear\r\nyour reasons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor smiled a little.  \xe2\x80\x9cVery true.  You have a perfect right to ask\r\nfor them.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he puffed his cigar a few moments.  \xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then,\r\nwithout accusing Mr. Townsend of being in love only with your fortune\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nwith the fortune that you justly expect\xe2\x80\x94I will say that there is every\r\nreason to suppose that these good things have entered into his\r\ncalculation more largely than a tender solicitude for your happiness\r\nstrictly requires.  There is, of course, nothing impossible in an\r\nintelligent young man entertaining a disinterested affection for you.\r\nYou are an honest, amiable girl, and an intelligent young man might\r\neasily find it out.  But the principal thing that we know about this\r\nyoung man\xe2\x80\x94who is, indeed, very intelligent\xe2\x80\x94leads us to suppose that,\r\nhowever much he may value your personal merits, he values your money\r\nmore.  The principal thing we know about him is that he has led a life of\r\ndissipation, and has spent a fortune of his own in doing so.  That is\r\nenough for me, my dear.  I wish you to marry a young man with other\r\nantecedents\xe2\x80\x94a young man who could give positive guarantees.  If Morris\r\nTownsend has spent his own fortune in amusing himself, there is every\r\nreason to believe that he would spend yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor delivered himself of these remarks slowly, deliberately, with\r\noccasional pauses and prolongations of accent, which made no great\r\nallowance for poor Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s suspense as to his conclusion.  She sat\r\ndown at last, with her head bent and her eyes still fixed upon him; and\r\nstrangely enough\xe2\x80\x94I hardly know how to tell it\xe2\x80\x94even while she felt that\r\nwhat he said went so terribly against her, she admired his neatness and\r\nnobleness of expression.  There was something hopeless and oppressive in\r\nhaving to argue with her father; but she too, on her side, must try to be\r\nclear.  He was so quiet; he was not at all angry; and she too must be\r\nquiet.  But her very effort to be quiet made her tremble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not the principal thing we know about him,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and there\r\nwas a touch of her tremor in her voice.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere are other things\xe2\x80\x94many\r\nother things.  He has very high abilities\xe2\x80\x94he wants so much to do\r\nsomething.  He is kind, and generous, and true,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Catherine, who\r\nhad not suspected hitherto the resources of her eloquence.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd his\r\nfortune\xe2\x80\x94his fortune that he spent\xe2\x80\x94was very small!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more reason he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have spent it,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Doctor,\r\ngetting up, with a laugh.  Then as Catherine, who had also risen to her\r\nfeet again, stood there in her rather angular earnestness, wishing so\r\nmuch and expressing so little, he drew her towards him and kissed her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t think me cruel?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, holding her a moment.\r\n\r\nThis question was not reassuring; it seemed to Catherine, on the\r\ncontrary, to suggest possibilities which made her feel sick.  But she\r\nanswered coherently enough\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear father; because if you knew how I\r\nfeel\xe2\x80\x94and you must know, you know everything\xe2\x80\x94you would be so kind, so\r\ngentle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think I know how you feel,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will be very\r\nkind\xe2\x80\x94be sure of that.  And I will see Mr. Townsend to-morrow.  Meanwhile,\r\nand for the present, be so good as to mention to no one that you are\r\nengaged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXII\r\n\r\n\r\nON the morrow, in the afternoon, he stayed at home, awaiting Mr.\r\nTownsend\xe2\x80\x99s call\xe2\x80\x94a proceeding by which it appeared to him (justly perhaps,\r\nfor he was a very busy man) that he paid Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s suitor great honour,\r\nand gave both these young people so much the less to complain of.  Morris\r\npresented himself with a countenance sufficiently serene\xe2\x80\x94he appeared to\r\nhave forgotten the \xe2\x80\x9cinsult\xe2\x80\x9d for which he had solicited Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsympathy two evenings before, and Dr. Sloper lost no time in letting him\r\nknow that he had been prepared for his visit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine told me yesterday what has been going on between you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou must allow me to say that it would have been becoming of you\r\nto give me notice of your intentions before they had gone so far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have done so,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris answered, \xe2\x80\x9cif you had not had so much the\r\nappearance of leaving your daughter at liberty.  She seems to me quite\r\nher own mistress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiterally, she is.  But she has not emancipated herself morally quite so\r\nfar, I trust, as to choose a husband without consulting me.  I have left\r\nher at liberty, but I have not been in the least indifferent.  The truth\r\nis that your little affair has come to a head with a rapidity that\r\nsurprises me.  It was only the other day that Catherine made your\r\nacquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not long ago, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, with great gravity.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nadmit that we have not been slow to\xe2\x80\x94to arrive at an understanding.  But\r\nthat was very natural, from the moment we were sure of ourselves\xe2\x80\x94and of\r\neach other.  My interest in Miss Sloper began the first time I saw her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid it not by chance precede your first meeting?\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor asked.\r\n\r\nMorris looked at him an instant.  \xe2\x80\x9cI certainly had already heard that she\r\nwas a charming girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA charming girl\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what you think her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly.  Otherwise I should not be sitting here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor meditated a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear young man,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou must be very susceptible.  As Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s father, I have, I trust, a\r\njust and tender appreciation of her many good qualities; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\ntelling you that I have never thought of her as a charming girl, and\r\nnever expected any one else to do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris Townsend received this statement with a smile that was not wholly\r\ndevoid of deference.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I might think of her if I were\r\nher father.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t put myself in that place.  I speak from my own point\r\nof view.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor; \xe2\x80\x9cbut that is not all that is\r\nnecessary.  I told Catherine yesterday that I disapproved of her\r\nengagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe let me know as much, and I was very sorry to hear it.  I am greatly\r\ndisappointed.\xe2\x80\x9d  And Morris sat in silence awhile, looking at the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you really expect I would say I was delighted, and throw my daughter\r\ninto your arms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; I had an idea you didn\xe2\x80\x99t like me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat gave you the idea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact that I am poor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat has a harsh sound,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is about the\r\ntruth\xe2\x80\x94speaking of you strictly as a son-in-law.  Your absence of means,\r\nof a profession, of visible resources or prospects, places you in a\r\ncategory from which it would be imprudent for me to select a husband for\r\nmy daughter, who is a weak young woman with a large fortune.  In any\r\nother capacity I am perfectly prepared to like you.  As a son-in-law, I\r\nabominate you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris Townsend listened respectfully.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think Miss Sloper is a\r\nweak woman,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you must defend her\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s the least you can do.  But I have\r\nknown my child twenty years, and you have known her six weeks.  Even if\r\nshe were not weak, however, you would still be a penniless man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes; that is _my_ weakness!  And therefore, you mean, I am\r\nmercenary\xe2\x80\x94I only want your daughter\xe2\x80\x99s money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t say that.  I am not obliged to say it; and to say it, save under\r\nstress of compulsion, would be very bad taste.  I say simply that you\r\nbelong to the wrong category.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your daughter doesn\xe2\x80\x99t marry a category,\xe2\x80\x9d Townsend urged, with his\r\nhandsome smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe marries an individual\xe2\x80\x94an individual whom she is so\r\ngood as to say she loves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn individual who offers so little in return!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible to offer more than the most tender affection and a\r\nlifelong devotion?\xe2\x80\x9d the young man demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends how we take it.  It is possible to offer a few other things\r\nbesides; and not only is it possible, but it\xe2\x80\x99s usual.  A lifelong\r\ndevotion is measured after the fact; and meanwhile it is customary in\r\nthese cases to give a few material securities.  What are yours?  A very\r\nhandsome face and figure, and a very good manner.  They are excellent as\r\nfar as they go, but they don\xe2\x80\x99t go far enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is one thing you should add to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris; \xe2\x80\x9cthe word of a\r\ngentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe word of a gentleman that you will always love Catherine?  You must\r\nbe a very fine gentleman to be sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe word of a gentleman that I am not mercenary; that my affection for\r\nMiss Sloper is as pure and disinterested a sentiment as was ever lodged\r\nin a human breast!  I care no more for her fortune than for the ashes in\r\nthat grate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take note\xe2\x80\x94I take note,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut having done so, I turn\r\nto our category again.  Even with that solemn vow on your lips, you take\r\nyour place in it.  There is nothing against you but an accident, if you\r\nwill; but with my thirty years\xe2\x80\x99 medical practice, I have seen that\r\naccidents may have far-reaching consequences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris smoothed his hat\xe2\x80\x94it was already remarkably glossy\xe2\x80\x94and continued to\r\ndisplay a self-control which, as the Doctor was obliged to admit, was\r\nextremely creditable to him.  But his disappointment was evidently keen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there nothing I can do to make you believe in me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there were I should be sorry to suggest it, for\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you see?\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want to believe in you!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would go and dig in the fields.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be foolish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take the first work that offers, to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo so by all means\xe2\x80\x94but for your own sake, not for mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see; you think I am an idler!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris exclaimed, a little too much in\r\nthe tone of a man who has made a discovery.  But he saw his error\r\nimmediately, and blushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter what I think, when once I have told you I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nof you as a son-in-law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Morris persisted.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou think I would squander her money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor smiled.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, as I say; but I plead guilty to\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s because I spent my own, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cI frankly\r\nconfess that.  I have been wild.  I have been foolish.  I will tell you\r\nevery crazy thing I ever did, if you like.  There were some great follies\r\namong the number\xe2\x80\x94I have never concealed that.  But I have sown my wild\r\noats.  Isn\xe2\x80\x99t there some proverb about a reformed rake?  I was not a rake,\r\nbut I assure you I have reformed.  It is better to have amused oneself\r\nfor a while and have done with it.  Your daughter would never care for a\r\nmilksop; and I will take the liberty of saying that you would like one\r\nquite as little.  Besides, between my money and hers there is a great\r\ndifference.  I spent my own; it was because it was my own that I spent\r\nit.  And I made no debts; when it was gone I stopped.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t owe a\r\npenny in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to inquire what you are living on now\xe2\x80\x94though I admit,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nDoctor added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the question, on my part, is inconsistent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am living on the remnants of my property,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris Townsend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you!\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor gravely replied.\r\n\r\nYes, certainly, Morris\xe2\x80\x99s self-control was laudable.  \xe2\x80\x9cEven admitting I\r\nattach an undue importance to Miss Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s fortune,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cwould\r\nnot that be in itself an assurance that I should take much care of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you should take too much care would be quite as bad as that you\r\nshould take too little.  Catherine might suffer as much by your economy\r\nas by your extravagance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are very unjust!\xe2\x80\x9d  The young man made this declaration\r\ndecently, civilly, without violence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is your privilege to think so, and I surrender my reputation to you!\r\nI certainly don\xe2\x80\x99t flatter myself I gratify you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you care a little to gratify your daughter?  Do you enjoy the idea\r\nof making her miserable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am perfectly resigned to her thinking me a tyrant for a twelvemonth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a twelvemonth!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Morris, with a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a lifetime, then!  She may as well be miserable in that way as in\r\nthe other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere at last Morris lost his temper.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are not polite, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou push me to it\xe2\x80\x94you argue too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a great deal at stake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, whatever it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have lost it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure of that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Morris; \xe2\x80\x9care you sure your daughter will\r\ngive me up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, of course, you have lost it as far as I am concerned.  As for\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s giving you up\xe2\x80\x94no, I am not sure of it.  But as I shall\r\nstrongly recommend it, as I have a great fund of respect and affection in\r\nmy daughter\xe2\x80\x99s mind to draw upon, and as she has the sentiment of duty\r\ndeveloped in a very high degree, I think it extremely possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris Townsend began to smooth his hat again.  \xe2\x80\x9cI too have a fund of\r\naffection to draw upon!\xe2\x80\x9d he observed at last.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor at this point showed his own first symptoms of irritation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to defy me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall it what you please, sir!  I mean not to give your daughter up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor shook his head.  \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the least fear of your pining away\r\nyour life.  You are made to enjoy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris gave a laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour opposition to my marriage is all the more\r\ncruel, then!  Do you intend to forbid your daughter to see me again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is past the age at which people are forbidden, and I am not a father\r\nin an old-fashioned novel.  But I shall strongly urge her to break with\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think she will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris Townsend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not.  But I shall have done what I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone too far,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo retreat?  Then let her stop where she is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo far to stop, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor looked at him a moment; Morris had his hand on the door.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a great deal of impertinence in your saying it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will say no more, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris answered; and, making his bow, he left\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nIT may be thought the Doctor was too positive, and Mrs. Almond intimated\r\nas much.  But, as he said, he had his impression; it seemed to him\r\nsufficient, and he had no wish to modify it.  He had passed his life in\r\nestimating people (it was part of the medical trade), and in nineteen\r\ncases out of twenty he was right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps Mr. Townsend is the twentieth case,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he is, though he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look to me at all like a twentieth\r\ncase.  But I will give him the benefit of the doubt, and, to make sure, I\r\nwill go and talk with Mrs. Montgomery.  She will almost certainly tell me\r\nI have done right; but it is just possible that she will prove to me that\r\nI have made the greatest mistake of my life.  If she does, I will beg Mr.\r\nTownsend\xe2\x80\x99s pardon.  You needn\xe2\x80\x99t invite her to meet me, as you kindly\r\nproposed; I will write her a frank letter, telling her how matters stand,\r\nand asking leave to come and see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid the frankness will be chiefly on your side.  The poor little\r\nwoman will stand up for her brother, whatever he may be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever he may be?  I doubt that.  People are not always so fond of\r\ntheir brothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen it\xe2\x80\x99s a question of thirty thousand a year\r\ncoming into a family\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she stands up for him on account of the money, she will be a humbug.\r\nIf she is a humbug I shall see it.  If I see it, I won\xe2\x80\x99t waste time with\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not a humbug\xe2\x80\x94she is an exemplary woman.  She will not wish to\r\nplay her brother a trick simply because he is selfish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she is worth talking to, she will sooner play him a trick than that\r\nhe should play Catherine one.  Has she seen Catherine, by the way\xe2\x80\x94does\r\nshe know her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to my knowledge.  Mr. Townsend can have had no particular interest\r\nin bringing them together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she is an exemplary woman, no.  But we shall see to what extent she\r\nanswers your description.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be curious to hear her description of you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond,\r\nwith a laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, meanwhile, how is Catherine taking it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs she takes everything\xe2\x80\x94as a matter of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t she make a noise?  Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t she made a scene?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not scenic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought a love-lorn maiden was always scenic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fantastic widow is more so.  Lavinia has made me a speech; she thinks\r\nme very arbitrary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has a talent for being in the wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am\r\nvery sorry for Catherine, all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I.  But she will get over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou believe she will give him up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI count upon it.  She has such an admiration for her father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we know all about that!  But it only makes me pity her the more.  It\r\nmakes her dilemma the more painful, and the effort of choosing between\r\nyou and her lover almost impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she can\xe2\x80\x99t choose, all the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he will stand there entreating her to choose, and Lavinia will\r\npull on that side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad she is not on my side; she is capable of ruining an excellent\r\ncause.  The day Lavinia gets into your boat it capsizes.  But she had\r\nbetter be careful,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will have no treason in my\r\nhouse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suspect she will be careful; for she is at bottom very much afraid of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are both afraid of me\xe2\x80\x94harmless as I am!\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nit is on that that I build\xe2\x80\x94on the salutary terror I inspire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nHE wrote his frank letter to Mrs. Montgomery, who punctually answered it,\r\nmentioning an hour at which he might present himself in the Second\r\nAvenue.  She lived in a neat little house of red brick, which had been\r\nfreshly painted, with the edges of the bricks very sharply marked out in\r\nwhite.  It has now disappeared, with its companions, to make room for a\r\nrow of structures more majestic.  There were green shutters upon the\r\nwindows, without slats, but pierced with little holes, arranged in\r\ngroups; and before the house was a diminutive yard, ornamented with a\r\nbush of mysterious character, and surrounded by a low wooden paling,\r\npainted in the same green as the shutters.  The place looked like a\r\nmagnified baby-house, and might have been taken down from a shelf in a\r\ntoy-shop.  Dr. Sloper, when he went to call, said to himself, as he\r\nglanced at the objects I have enumerated, that Mrs. Montgomery was\r\nevidently a thrifty and self-respecting little person\xe2\x80\x94the modest\r\nproportions of her dwelling seemed to indicate that she was of small\r\nstature\xe2\x80\x94who took a virtuous satisfaction in keeping herself tidy, and had\r\nresolved that, since she might not be splendid, she would at least be\r\nimmaculate.  She received him in a little parlour, which was precisely\r\nthe parlour he had expected: a small unspeckled bower, ornamented with a\r\ndesultory foliage of tissue-paper, and with clusters of glass drops, amid\r\nwhich\xe2\x80\x94to carry out the analogy\xe2\x80\x94the temperature of the leafy season was\r\nmaintained by means of a cast-iron stove, emitting a dry blue flame, and\r\nsmelling strongly of varnish.  The walls were embellished with engravings\r\nswathed in pink gauze, and the tables ornamented with volumes of extracts\r\nfrom the poets, usually bound in black cloth stamped with florid designs\r\nin jaundiced gilt.  The Doctor had time to take cognisance of these\r\ndetails, for Mrs. Montgomery, whose conduct he pronounced under the\r\ncircumstances inexcusable, kept him waiting some ten minutes before she\r\nappeared.  At last, however, she rustled in, smoothing down a stiff\r\npoplin dress, with a little frightened flush in a gracefully-rounded\r\ncheek.\r\n\r\nShe was a small, plump, fair woman, with a bright, clear eye, and an\r\nextraordinary air of neatness and briskness.  But these qualities were\r\nevidently combined with an unaffected humility, and the Doctor gave her\r\nhis esteem as soon as he had looked at her.  A brave little person, with\r\nlively perceptions, and yet a disbelief in her own talent for social, as\r\ndistinguished from practical, affairs\xe2\x80\x94this was his rapid mental _r\xc3\xa9sum\xc3\xa9_\r\nof Mrs. Montgomery, who, as he saw, was flattered by what she regarded as\r\nthe honour of his visit.  Mrs. Montgomery, in her little red house in the\r\nSecond Avenue, was a person for whom Dr. Sloper was one of the great men,\r\none of the fine gentlemen of New York; and while she fixed her agitated\r\neyes upon him, while she clasped her mittened hands together in her\r\nglossy poplin lap, she had the appearance of saying to herself that he\r\nquite answered her idea of what a distinguished guest would naturally be.\r\nShe apologised for being late; but he interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cfor while I sat here I had time to think\r\nover what I wish to say to you, and to make up my mind how to begin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do begin!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not so easy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, smiling.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou will have gathered\r\nfrom my letter that I wish to ask you a few questions, and you may not\r\nfind it very comfortable to answer them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I have thought what I should say.  It is not very easy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must understand my situation\xe2\x80\x94my state of mind.  Your brother\r\nwishes to marry my daughter, and I wish to find out what sort of a young\r\nman he is.  A good way to do so seemed to be to come and ask you; which I\r\nhave proceeded to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery evidently took the situation very seriously; she was in a\r\nstate of extreme moral concentration.  She kept her pretty eyes, which\r\nwere illumined by a sort of brilliant modesty, attached to his own\r\ncountenance, and evidently paid the most earnest attention to each of his\r\nwords.  Her expression indicated that she thought his idea of coming to\r\nsee her a very superior conception, but that she was really afraid to\r\nhave opinions on strange subjects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am extremely glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a tone which seemed to\r\nadmit, at the same time, that this had nothing to do with the question.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor took advantage of this admission.  \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t come to see you\r\nfor your pleasure; I came to make you say disagreeable things\xe2\x80\x94and you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t like that.  What sort of a gentleman is your brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery\xe2\x80\x99s illuminated gaze grew vague, and began to wander.
