She\r\nsmiled a little, and for some time made no answer, so that the Doctor at\r\nlast became impatient.  And her answer, when it came, was not\r\nsatisfactory.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is difficult to talk about one\xe2\x80\x99s brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when one is fond of him, and when one has plenty of good to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, even then, when a good deal depends on it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing depends on it, for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean for\xe2\x80\x94for\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and she hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor your brother himself.  I see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean for Miss Sloper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.  The Doctor liked this;\r\nit had the accent of sincerity.  \xe2\x80\x9cExactly; that\xe2\x80\x99s the point.  If my poor\r\ngirl should marry your brother, everything\xe2\x80\x94as regards her happiness\xe2\x80\x94would\r\ndepend on his being a good fellow.  She is the best creature in the\r\nworld, and she could never do him a grain of injury.  He, on the other\r\nhand, if he should not be all that we desire, might make her very\r\nmiserable.  That is why I want you to throw some light upon his\r\ncharacter, you know.  Of course you are not bound to do it.  My daughter,\r\nwhom you have never seen, is nothing to you; and I, possibly, am only an\r\nindiscreet and impertinent old man.  It is perfectly open to you to tell\r\nme that my visit is in very bad taste and that I had better go about my\r\nbusiness.  But I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you will do this; because I think we shall\r\ninterest you, my poor girl and I.  I am sure that if you were to see\r\nCatherine, she would interest you very much.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean because she is\r\ninteresting in the usual sense of the word, but because you would feel\r\nsorry for her.  She is so soft, so simple-minded, she would be such an\r\neasy victim!  A bad husband would have remarkable facilities for making\r\nher miserable; for she would have neither the intelligence nor the\r\nresolution to get the better of him, and yet she would have an\r\nexaggerated power of suffering.  I see,\xe2\x80\x9d added the Doctor, with his most\r\ninsinuating, his most professional laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are already interested!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been interested from the moment he told me he was engaged,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! he says that\xe2\x80\x94he calls it an engagement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he has told me you didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he tell you that I don\xe2\x80\x99t like _him_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he told me that too.  I said I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it!\xe2\x80\x9d added Mrs.\r\nMontgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you can\xe2\x80\x99t.  But what you can do is to tell me I am right\xe2\x80\x94to\r\ngive me an attestation, as it were.\xe2\x80\x9d  And the Doctor accompanied this\r\nremark with another professional smile.\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery, however, smiled not at all; it was obvious that she\r\ncould not take the humorous view of his appeal.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat is a good deal to\r\nask,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere can be no doubt of that; and I must, in conscience, remind you of\r\nthe advantages a young man marrying my daughter would enjoy.  She has an\r\nincome of ten thousand dollars in her own right, left her by her mother;\r\nif she marries a husband I approve, she will come into almost twice as\r\nmuch more at my death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery listened in great earnestness to this splendid financial\r\nstatement; she had never heard thousands of dollars so familiarly talked\r\nabout.  She flushed a little with excitement.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour daughter will be\r\nimmensely rich,\xe2\x80\x9d she said softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the bother of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if Morris should marry her, he\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d  And she hesitated timidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would be master of all that money?  By no means.  He would be master\r\nof the ten thousand a year that she has from her mother; but I should\r\nleave every penny of my own fortune, earned in the laborious exercise of\r\nmy profession, to public institutions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery dropped her eyes at this, and sat for some time gazing at\r\nthe straw matting which covered her floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it seems to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cthat in so doing\r\nI should play your brother a very shabby trick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all.  That is too much money to get possession of so easily, by\r\nmarrying.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it would be right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s right to get all one can.  But in this case your brother wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe able.  If Catherine marries without my consent, she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get a\r\npenny from my own pocket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that certain?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Montgomery, looking up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs certain as that I sit here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven if she should pine away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven if she should pine to a shadow, which isn\xe2\x80\x99t probable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Morris know this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be most happy to inform him!\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor exclaimed.\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery resumed her meditations, and her visitor, who was\r\nprepared to give time to the affair, asked himself whether, in spite of\r\nher little conscientious air, she was not playing into her brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands.  At the same time he was half ashamed of the ordeal to which he\r\nhad subjected her, and was touched by the gentleness with which she bore\r\nit.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf she were a humbug,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cshe would get angry; unless she be\r\nvery deep indeed.  It is not probable that she is as deep as that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you dislike Morris so much?\xe2\x80\x9d she presently asked, emerging\r\nfrom her reflexions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t dislike him in the least as a friend, as a companion.  He seems\r\nto me a charming fellow, and I should think he would be excellent\r\ncompany.  I dislike him, exclusively, as a son-in-law.  If the only\r\noffice of a son-in-law were to dine at the paternal table, I should set a\r\nhigh value upon your brother.  He dines capitally.  But that is a small\r\npart of his function, which, in general, is to be a protector and\r\ncaretaker of my child, who is singularly ill-adapted to take care of\r\nherself.  It is there that he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t satisfy me.  I confess I have\r\nnothing but my impression to go by; but I am in the habit of trusting my\r\nimpression.  Of course you are at liberty to contradict it flat.  He\r\nstrikes me as selfish and shallow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery\xe2\x80\x99s eyes expanded a little, and the Doctor fancied he saw\r\nthe light of admiration in them.  \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder you have discovered he is\r\nselfish!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he hides it so well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I think we are all rather\r\nselfish,\xe2\x80\x9d she added quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so too; but I have seen people hide it better than he.  You see\r\nI am helped by a habit I have of dividing people into classes, into\r\ntypes.  I may easily be mistaken about your brother as an individual, but\r\nhis type is written on his whole person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very good-looking,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor eyed her a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou women are all the same!  But the type\r\nto which your brother belongs was made to be the ruin of you, and you\r\nwere made to be its handmaids and victims.  The sign of the type in\r\nquestion is the determination\xe2\x80\x94sometimes terrible in its quiet\r\nintensity\xe2\x80\x94to accept nothing of life but its pleasures, and to secure\r\nthese pleasures chiefly by the aid of your complaisant sex.  Young men of\r\nthis class never do anything for themselves that they can get other\r\npeople to do for them, and it is the infatuation, the devotion, the\r\nsuperstition of others that keeps them going.  These others in\r\nninety-nine cases out of a hundred are women.  What our young friends\r\nchiefly insist upon is that some one else shall suffer for them; and\r\nwomen do that sort of thing, as you must know, wonderfully well.\xe2\x80\x9d  The\r\nDoctor paused a moment, and then he added abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9cYou have suffered\r\nimmensely for your brother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis exclamation was abrupt, as I say, but it was also perfectly\r\ncalculated.  The Doctor had been rather disappointed at not finding his\r\ncompact and comfortable little hostess surrounded in a more visible\r\ndegree by the ravages of Morris Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s immorality; but he had said to\r\nhimself that this was not because the young man had spared her, but\r\nbecause she had contrived to plaster up her wounds.  They were aching\r\nthere, behind the varnished stove, the festooned engravings, beneath her\r\nown neat little poplin bosom; and if he could only touch the tender spot,\r\nshe would make a movement that would betray her.  The words I have just\r\nquoted were an attempt to put his finger suddenly upon the place; and\r\nthey had some of the success that he looked for.  The tears sprang for a\r\nmoment to Mrs. Montgomery\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and she indulged in a proud little jerk\r\nof the head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how you have found that out!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy a philosophic trick\xe2\x80\x94by what they call induction.  You know you have\r\nalways your option of contradicting me.  But kindly answer me a question.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you give your brother money?  I think you ought to answer that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have given him money,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have not had much to give him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was silent a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you ask me for a confession of poverty,\r\nthat is easily made.  I am very poor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne would never suppose it from your\xe2\x80\x94your charming house,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nDoctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cI learned from my sister that your income was moderate, and\r\nyour family numerous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have five children,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Montgomery observed; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am happy to say\r\nI can bring them up decently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you can\xe2\x80\x94accomplished and devoted as you are!  But your brother\r\nhas counted them over, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCounted them over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows there are five, I mean.  He tells me it is he that brings them\r\nup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery stared a moment, and then quickly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes; he teaches\r\nthem Spanish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor laughed out.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat must take a great deal off your hands!\r\nYour brother also knows, of course, that you have very little money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have often told him so!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Montgomery exclaimed, more unreservedly\r\nthan she had yet spoken.  She was apparently taking some comfort in the\r\nDoctor\xe2\x80\x99s clairvoyancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich means that you have often occasion to, and that he often sponges\r\non you.  Excuse the crudity of my language; I simply express a fact.  I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t ask you how much of your money he has had, it is none of my\r\nbusiness.  I have ascertained what I suspected\xe2\x80\x94what I wished.\xe2\x80\x9d  And the\r\nDoctor got up, gently smoothing his hat.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour brother lives on you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid as he stood there.\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery quickly rose from her chair, following her visitor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmovements with a look of fascination.  But then, with a certain\r\ninconsequence\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI have never complained of him!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t protest\xe2\x80\x94you have not betrayed him.  But I advise you not to\r\ngive him any more money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see it is in my interest that he should marry a rich person?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf, as you say, he lives on me, I can only wish to get rid\r\nof him, and to put obstacles in the way of his marrying is to increase my\r\nown difficulties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish very much you would come to me with your difficulties,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nDoctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, if I throw him back on your hands, the least I can\r\ndo is to help you to bear the burden.  If you will allow me to say so,\r\nthen, I shall take the liberty of placing in your hands, for the present,\r\na certain fund for your brother\xe2\x80\x99s support.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery stared; she evidently thought he was jesting; but she\r\npresently saw that he was not, and the complication of her feelings\r\nbecame painful.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that I ought to be very much offended\r\nwith you,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I have offered you money?  That\xe2\x80\x99s a superstition,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nDoctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou must let me come and see you again, and we will talk about\r\nthese things.  I suppose that some of your children are girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have two little girls,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Montgomery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, when they grow up, and begin to think of taking husbands, you will\r\nsee how anxious you will be about the moral character of these gentlemen.\r\nThen you will understand this visit of mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are not to believe that Morris\xe2\x80\x99s moral character is bad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor looked at her a little, with folded arms.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere is something\r\nI should greatly like\xe2\x80\x94as a moral satisfaction.  I should like to hear you\r\nsay\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98He is abominably selfish!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words came out with the grave distinctness of his voice, and they\r\nseemed for an instant to create, to poor Mrs. Montgomery\xe2\x80\x99s troubled\r\nvision, a material image.  She gazed at it an instant, and then she\r\nturned away.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou distress me, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is, after all,\r\nmy brother, and his talents, his talents\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d  On these last words her voice\r\nquavered, and before he knew it she had burst into tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis talents are first-rate!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cWe must find a proper\r\nfield for them!\xe2\x80\x9d  And he assured her most respectfully of his regret at\r\nhaving so greatly discomposed her.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all for my poor Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwent on.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou must know her, and you will see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Montgomery brushed away her tears, and blushed at having shed them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know your daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered; and then, in an\r\ninstant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let her marry him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Sloper went away with the words gently humming in his ears\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nher marry him!\xe2\x80\x9d  They gave him the moral satisfaction of which he had\r\njust spoken, and their value was the greater that they had evidently cost\r\na pang to poor little Mrs. Montgomery\xe2\x80\x99s family pride.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXV\r\n\r\n\r\nHE had been puzzled by the way that Catherine carried herself; her\r\nattitude at this sentimental crisis seemed to him unnaturally passive.\r\nShe had not spoken to him again after that scene in the library, the day\r\nbefore his interview with Morris; and a week had elapsed without making\r\nany change in her manner.  There was nothing in it that appealed for\r\npity, and he was even a little disappointed at her not giving him an\r\nopportunity to make up for his harshness by some manifestation of\r\nliberality which should operate as a compensation.  He thought a little\r\nof offering to take her for a tour in Europe; but he was determined to do\r\nthis only in case she should seem mutely to reproach him.  He had an idea\r\nthat she would display a talent for mute reproaches, and he was surprised\r\nat not finding himself exposed to these silent batteries.  She said\r\nnothing, either tacitly or explicitly, and as she was never very\r\ntalkative, there was now no especial eloquence in her reserve.  And poor\r\nCatherine was not sulky\xe2\x80\x94a style of behaviour for which she had too little\r\nhistrionic talent; she was simply very patient.  Of course she was\r\nthinking over her situation, and she was apparently doing so in a\r\ndeliberate and unimpassioned manner, with a view of making the best of\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will do as I have bidden her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, and he made the\r\nfurther reflexion that his daughter was not a woman of a great spirit.  I\r\nknow not whether he had hoped for a little more resistance for the sake\r\nof a little more entertainment; but he said to himself, as he had said\r\nbefore, that though it might have its momentary alarms, paternity was,\r\nafter all, not an exciting vocation.\r\n\r\nCatherine, meanwhile, had made a discovery of a very different sort; it\r\nhad become vivid to her that there was a great excitement in trying to be\r\na good daughter.  She had an entirely new feeling, which may be described\r\nas a state of expectant suspense about her own actions.  She watched\r\nherself as she would have watched another person, and wondered what she\r\nwould do.  It was as if this other person, who was both herself and not\r\nherself, had suddenly sprung into being, inspiring her with a natural\r\ncuriosity as to the performance of untested functions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad I have such a good daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father, kissing her,\r\nafter the lapse of several days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am trying to be good,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, turning away, with a conscience\r\nnot altogether clear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there is anything you would like to say to me, you know you must not\r\nhesitate.  You needn\xe2\x80\x99t feel obliged to be so quiet.  I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nthat Mr. Townsend should be a frequent topic of conversation, but\r\nwhenever you have anything particular to say about him I shall be very\r\nglad to hear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing particular at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe never asked her whether she had seen Morris again, because he was sure\r\nthat if this had been the case she would tell him.  She had, in fact, not\r\nseen him, she had only written him a long letter.  The letter at least\r\nwas long for her; and, it may be added, that it was long for Morris; it\r\nconsisted of five pages, in a remarkably neat and handsome hand.\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s handwriting was beautiful, and she was even a little proud of\r\nit; she was extremely fond of copying, and possessed volumes of extracts\r\nwhich testified to this accomplishment; volumes which she had exhibited\r\none day to her lover, when the bliss of feeling that she was important in\r\nhis eyes was exceptionally keen.  She told Morris in writing that her\r\nfather had expressed the wish that she should not see him again, and that\r\nshe begged he would not come to the house until she should have \xe2\x80\x9cmade up\r\nher mind.\xe2\x80\x9d  Morris replied with a passionate epistle, in which he asked\r\nto what, in Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s name, she wished to make up her mind.  Had not her\r\nmind been made up two weeks before, and could it be possible that she\r\nentertained the idea of throwing him off?  Did she mean to break down at\r\nthe very beginning of their ordeal, after all the promises of fidelity\r\nshe had both given and extracted?  And he gave an account of his own\r\ninterview with her father\xe2\x80\x94an account not identical at all points with\r\nthat offered in these pages.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe was terribly violent,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris wrote;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut you know my self-control.  I have need of it all when I remember\r\nthat I have it in my power to break in upon your cruel captivity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nCatherine sent him, in answer to this, a note of three lines.  \xe2\x80\x9cI am in\r\ngreat trouble; do not doubt of my affection, but let me wait a little and\r\nthink.\xe2\x80\x9d  The idea of a struggle with her father, of setting up her will\r\nagainst his own, was heavy on her soul, and it kept her formally\r\nsubmissive, as a great physical weight keeps us motionless.  It never\r\nentered into her mind to throw her lover off; but from the first she\r\ntried to assure herself that there would be a peaceful way out of their\r\ndifficulty.  The assurance was vague, for it contained no element of\r\npositive conviction that her father would change his mind.  She only had\r\nan idea that if she should be very good, the situation would in some\r\nmysterious manner improve.  To be good, she must be patient, respectful,\r\nabstain from judging her father too harshly, and from committing any act\r\nof open defiance.  He was perhaps right, after all, to think as he did;\r\nby which Catherine meant not in the least that his judgement of Morris\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmotives in seeking to marry her was perhaps a just one, but that it was\r\nprobably natural and proper that conscientious parents should be\r\nsuspicious and even unjust.  There were probably people in the world as\r\nbad as her father supposed Morris to be, and if there were the slightest\r\nchance of Morris being one of these sinister persons, the Doctor was\r\nright in taking it into account.  Of course he could not know what she\r\nknew, how the purest love and truth were seated in the young man\xe2\x80\x99s eyes;\r\nbut Heaven, in its time, might appoint a way of bringing him to such\r\nknowledge.  Catherine expected a good deal of Heaven, and referred to the\r\nskies the initiative, as the French say, in dealing with her dilemma.\r\nShe could not imagine herself imparting any kind of knowledge to her\r\nfather, there was something superior even in his injustice and absolute\r\nin his mistakes.  But she could at least be good, and if she were only\r\ngood enough, Heaven would invent some way of reconciling all things\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndignity of her father\xe2\x80\x99s errors and the sweetness of her own confidence,\r\nthe strict performance of her filial duties and the enjoyment of Morris\r\nTownsend\xe2\x80\x99s affection.  Poor Catherine would have been glad to regard Mrs.\r\nPenniman as an illuminating agent, a part which this lady herself indeed\r\nwas but imperfectly prepared to play.  Mrs. Penniman took too much\r\nsatisfaction in the sentimental shadows of this little drama to have, for\r\nthe moment, any great interest in dissipating them.  She wished the plot\r\nto thicken, and the advice that she gave her niece tended, in her own\r\nimagination, to produce this result.  It was rather incoherent counsel,\r\nand from one day to another it contradicted itself; but it was pervaded\r\nby an earnest desire that Catherine should do something striking.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmust _act_, my dear; in your situation the great thing is to act,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMrs. Penniman, who found her niece altogether beneath her opportunities.\r\nMrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s real hope was that the girl would make a secret marriage,\r\nat which she should officiate as brideswoman or duenna.  She had a vision\r\nof this ceremony being performed in some subterranean chapel\xe2\x80\x94subterranean\r\nchapels in New York were not frequent, but Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s imagination\r\nwas not chilled by trifles\xe2\x80\x94and of the guilty couple\xe2\x80\x94she liked to think of\r\npoor Catherine and her suitor as the guilty couple\xe2\x80\x94being shuffled away in\r\na fast-whirling vehicle to some obscure lodging in the suburbs, where she\r\nwould pay them (in a thick veil) clandestine visits, where they would\r\nendure a period of romantic privation, and where ultimately, after she\r\nshould have been their earthly providence, their intercessor, their\r\nadvocate, and their medium of communication with the world, they should\r\nbe reconciled to her brother in an artistic tableau, in which she herself\r\nshould be somehow the central figure.  She hesitated as yet to recommend\r\nthis course to Catherine, but she attempted to draw an attractive picture\r\nof it to Morris Townsend.  She was in daily communication with the young\r\nman, whom she kept informed by letters of the state of affairs in\r\nWashington Square.  As he had been banished, as she said, from the house,\r\nshe no longer saw him; but she ended by writing to him that she longed\r\nfor an interview.  This interview could take place only on neutral\r\nground, and she bethought herself greatly before selecting a place of\r\nmeeting.  She had an inclination for Greenwood Cemetery, but she gave it\r\nup as too distant; she could not absent herself for so long, as she said,\r\nwithout exciting suspicion.  Then she thought of the Battery, but that\r\nwas rather cold and windy, besides one\xe2\x80\x99s being exposed to intrusion from\r\nthe Irish emigrants who at this point alight, with large appetites, in\r\nthe New World and at last she fixed upon an oyster saloon in the Seventh\r\nAvenue, kept by a negro\xe2\x80\x94an establishment of which she knew nothing save\r\nthat she had noticed it in passing.  She made an appointment with Morris\r\nTownsend to meet him there, and she went to the tryst at dusk, enveloped\r\nin an impenetrable veil.  He kept her waiting for half an hour\xe2\x80\x94he had\r\nalmost the whole width of the city to traverse\xe2\x80\x94but she liked to wait, it\r\nseemed to intensify the situation.  She ordered a cup of tea, which\r\nproved excessively bad, and this gave her a sense that she was suffering\r\nin a romantic cause.  When Morris at last arrived, they sat together for\r\nhalf an hour in the duskiest corner of a back shop; and it is hardly too\r\nmuch to say that this was the happiest half-hour that Mrs. Penniman had\r\nknown for years.  The situation was really thrilling, and it scarcely\r\nseemed to her a false note when her companion asked for an oyster stew,\r\nand proceeded to consume it before her eyes.  Morris, indeed, needed all\r\nthe satisfaction that stewed oysters could give him, for it may be\r\nintimated to the reader that he regarded Mrs. Penniman in the light of a\r\nfifth wheel to his coach.  He was in a state of irritation natural to a\r\ngentleman of fine parts who had been snubbed in a benevolent attempt to\r\nconfer a distinction upon a young woman of inferior characteristics, and\r\nthe insinuating sympathy of this somewhat desiccated matron appeared to\r\noffer him no practical relief.  He thought her a humbug, and he judged of\r\nhumbugs with a good deal of confidence.  He had listened and made himself\r\nagreeable to her at first, in order to get a footing in Washington\r\nSquare; and at present he needed all his self-command to be decently\r\ncivil.  It would have gratified him to tell her that she was a fantastic\r\nold woman, and that he should like to put her into an omnibus and send\r\nher home.  We know, however, that Morris possessed the virtue of\r\nself-control, and he had, moreover, the constant habit of seeking to be\r\nagreeable; so that, although Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s demeanour only exasperated\r\nhis already unquiet nerves, he listened to her with a sombre deference in\r\nwhich she found much to admire.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nTHEY had of course immediately spoken of Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cDid she send me a\r\nmessage, or\xe2\x80\x94or anything?\xe2\x80\x9d Morris asked.  He appeared to think that she\r\nmight have sent him a trinket or a lock of her hair.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was slightly embarrassed, for she had not told her niece of\r\nher intended expedition.  \xe2\x80\x9cNot exactly a message,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nask her for one, because I was afraid to\xe2\x80\x94to excite her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid she is not very excitable!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Morris gave a smile of some\r\nbitterness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is better than that.  She is steadfast\xe2\x80\x94she is true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think she will hold fast, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I hope it won\xe2\x80\x99t come to that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must be prepared for the worst, and that is what I wish to speak to\r\nyou about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call the worst?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, \xe2\x80\x9cmy brother\xe2\x80\x99s hard, intellectual nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the devil!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is impervious to pity,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman added, by way of explanation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that he won\xe2\x80\x99t come round?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will never be vanquished by argument.  I have studied him.  He will\r\nbe vanquished only by the accomplished fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe accomplished fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will come round afterwards,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with extreme\r\nsignificance.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe cares for nothing but facts; he must be met by facts!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Morris, \xe2\x80\x9cit is a fact that I wish to marry his daughter.\r\nI met him with that the other day, but he was not at all vanquished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was silent a little, and her smile beneath the shadow of\r\nher capacious bonnet, on the edge of which her black veil was arranged\r\ncurtain-wise, fixed itself upon Morris\xe2\x80\x99s face with a still more tender\r\nbrilliancy.  \xe2\x80\x9cMarry Catherine first and meet him afterwards!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you recommend that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young man, frowning heavily.\r\n\r\nShe was a little frightened, but she went on with considerable boldness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the way I see it: a private marriage\xe2\x80\x94a private marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d  She\r\nrepeated the phrase because she liked it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that I should carry Catherine off?  What do they call\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94elope with her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a crime when you are driven to it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\nhusband, as I have told you, was a distinguished clergyman; one of the\r\nmost eloquent men of his day.  He once married a young couple that had\r\nfled from the house of the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s father.  He was so interested in\r\ntheir story.  He had no hesitation, and everything came out beautifully.\r\nThe father was afterwards reconciled, and thought everything of the young\r\nman.  Mr. Penniman married them in the evening, about seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock.  The\r\nchurch was so dark, you could scarcely see; and Mr. Penniman was\r\nintensely agitated; he was so sympathetic.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe he could have\r\ndone it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately Catherine and I have not Mr. Penniman to marry us,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMorris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but you have me!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Mrs. Penniman expressively.  \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nperform the ceremony, but I can help you.  I can watch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe woman\xe2\x80\x99s an idiot,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Morris; but he was obliged to say\r\nsomething different.  It was not, however, materially more civil.  \xe2\x80\x9cWas\r\nit in order to tell me this that you requested I would meet you here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman had been conscious of a certain vagueness in her errand,\r\nand of not being able to offer him any very tangible reward for his long\r\nwalk.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought perhaps you would like to see one who is so near to\r\nCatherine,\xe2\x80\x9d she observed, with considerable majesty.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd also,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would value an opportunity of sending her something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris extended his empty hands with a melancholy smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cI am greatly\r\nobliged to you, but I have nothing to send.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you a _word_?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his companion, with her suggestive smile\r\ncoming back.\r\n\r\nMorris frowned again.  \xe2\x80\x9cTell her to hold fast,\xe2\x80\x9d he said rather curtly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a good word\xe2\x80\x94a noble word.  It will make her happy for many days.\r\nShe is very touching, very brave,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman went on, arranging her\r\nmantle and preparing to depart.  While she was so engaged she had an\r\ninspiration.  She found the phrase that she could boldly offer as a\r\nvindication of the step she had taken.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you marry Catherine at all\r\nrisks\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will give my brother a proof of your being what he\r\npretends to doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat he pretends to doubt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know what that is?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman asked almost playfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not concern me to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris grandly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it makes you angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI despise it,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you know what it is, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, shaking her finger\r\nat him.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe pretends that you like\xe2\x80\x94you like the money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris hesitated a moment; and then, as if he spoke advisedly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI _do_\r\nlike the money!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but not\xe2\x80\x94but not as he means it.  You don\xe2\x80\x99t like it more than\r\nCatherine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe leaned his elbows on the table and buried his head in his hands.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ntorture me!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.  And, indeed, this was almost the effect of the\r\npoor lady\xe2\x80\x99s too importunate interest in his situation.\r\n\r\nBut she insisted on making her point.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you marry her in spite of him,\r\nhe will take for granted that you expect nothing of him, and are prepared\r\nto do without it.  And so he will see that you are disinterested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris raised his head a little, following this argument, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what shall\r\nI gain by that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that he will see that he has been wrong in thinking that you wished\r\nto get his money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd seeing that I wish he would go to the deuce with it, he will leave\r\nit to a hospital.  Is that what you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that; though that would be very grand!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman\r\nquickly added.  \xe2\x80\x9cI mean that having done you such an injustice, he will\r\nthink it his duty, at the end, to make some amends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris shook his head, though it must be confessed he was a little struck\r\nwith this idea.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he is so sentimental?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not sentimental,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, to be perfectly fair\r\nto him, I think he has, in his own narrow way, a certain sense of duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere passed through Morris Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s mind a rapid wonder as to what he\r\nmight, even under a remote contingency, be indebted to from the action of\r\nthis principle in Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s breast, and the inquiry exhausted itself\r\nin his sense of the ludicrous.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour brother has no duties to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid presently, \xe2\x80\x9cand I none to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but he has duties to Catherine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but you see that on that principle Catherine has duties to him as\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman got up, with a melancholy sigh, as if she thought him very\r\nunimaginative.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe has always performed them faithfully; and now, do\r\nyou think she has no duties to _you_?\xe2\x80\x9d  Mrs. Penniman always, even in\r\nconversation, italicised her personal pronouns.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would sound harsh to say so!  I am so grateful for her love,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris\r\nadded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell her you said that!  And now, remember that if you need me, I\r\nam there.\xe2\x80\x9d  And Mrs. Penniman, who could think of nothing more to say,\r\nnodded vaguely in the direction of Washington Square.\r\n\r\nMorris looked some moments at the sanded floor of the shop; he seemed to\r\nbe disposed to linger a moment.  At last, looking up with a certain\r\nabruptness, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is your belief that if she marries me he will cut her\r\noff?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman stared a little, and smiled.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I have explained to you\r\nwhat I think would happen\xe2\x80\x94that in the end it would be the best thing to\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that, whatever she does, in the long run she will get the\r\nmoney?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t depend upon her, but upon you.  Venture to appear as\r\ndisinterested as you are!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman ingeniously.  Morris\r\ndropped his eyes on the sanded floor again, pondering this; and she\r\npursued.  \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Penniman and I had nothing, and we were very happy.\r\nCatherine, moreover, has her mother\xe2\x80\x99s fortune, which, at the time my\r\nsister-in-law married, was considered a very handsome one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris; and, indeed, it was quite\r\nsuperfluous, for he had contemplated the fact in all its lights.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAustin married a wife with money\xe2\x80\x94why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but your brother was a doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris objected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all young men can\xe2\x80\x99t be doctors!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think it an extremely loathsome profession,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, with\r\nan air of intellectual independence.  Then in a moment, he went on rather\r\ninconsequently, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose there is a will already made in\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s favour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so\xe2\x80\x94even doctors must die; and perhaps a little in mine,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nPenniman frankly added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you believe he would certainly change it\xe2\x80\x94as regards Catherine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and then change it back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but one can\xe2\x80\x99t depend on that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to _depend_ on it?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman asked.\r\n\r\nMorris blushed a little.  \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am certainly afraid of being the cause\r\nof an injury to Catherine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you must not be afraid.  Be afraid of nothing, and everything will\r\ngo well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then Mrs. Penniman paid for her cup of tea, and Morris paid for his\r\noyster stew, and they went out together into the dimly-lighted wilderness\r\nof the Seventh Avenue.  The dusk had closed in completely and the street\r\nlamps were separated by wide intervals of a pavement in which cavities\r\nand fissures played a disproportionate part.  An omnibus, emblazoned with\r\nstrange pictures, went tumbling over the dislocated cobble-stones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow will you go home?\xe2\x80\x9d Morris asked, following this vehicle with an\r\ninterested eye.  Mrs. Penniman had taken his arm.\r\n\r\nShe hesitated a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cI think this manner would be pleasant,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid; and she continued to let him feel the value of his support.\r\n\r\nSo he walked with her through the devious ways of the west side of the\r\ntown, and through the bustle of gathering nightfall in populous streets,\r\nto the quiet precinct of Washington Square.  They lingered a moment at\r\nthe foot of Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s white marble steps, above which a spotless white\r\ndoor, adorned with a glittering silver plate, seemed to figure, for\r\nMorris, the closed portal of happiness; and then Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncompanion rested a melancholy eye upon a lighted window in the upper part\r\nof the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my room\xe2\x80\x94my dear little room!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman remarked.\r\n\r\nMorris started.  \xe2\x80\x9cThen I needn\xe2\x80\x99t come walking round the Square to gaze at\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s as you please.  But Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s is behind; two noble windows on\r\nthe second floor.  I think you can see them from the other street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see them, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Morris turned his back to the\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell her you have been _here_, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman,\r\npointing to the spot where they stood; \xe2\x80\x9cand I will give her your\r\nmessage\xe2\x80\x94that she is to hold fast!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes! of course.  You know I write her all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to say more when it is spoken!  And remember, if you need me,\r\nthat I am _there_\xe2\x80\x9d; and Mrs. Penniman glanced at the third floor.\r\n\r\nOn this they separated, and Morris, left to himself, stood looking at the\r\nhouse a moment; after which he turned away, and took a gloomy walk round\r\nthe Square, on the opposite side, close to the wooden fence.  Then he\r\ncame back, and paused for a minute in front of Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s dwelling.\r\nHis eyes travelled over it; they even rested on the ruddy windows of Mrs.\r\nPenniman\xe2\x80\x99s apartment.  He thought it a devilish comfortable house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nMRS. PENNIMAN told Catherine that evening\xe2\x80\x94the two ladies were sitting in\r\nthe back parlour\xe2\x80\x94that she had had an interview with Morris Townsend; and\r\non receiving this news the girl started with a sense of pain.  She felt\r\nangry for the moment; it was almost the first time she had ever felt\r\nangry.  It seemed to her that her aunt was meddlesome; and from this came\r\na vague apprehension that she would spoil something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you should have seen him.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was right,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nCatherine said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was so sorry for him\xe2\x80\x94it seemed to me some one ought to see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one but I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, who felt as if she were making the most\r\npresumptuous speech of her life, and yet at the same time had an instinct\r\nthat she was right in doing so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d Aunt Lavinia rejoined; \xe2\x80\x9cand I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat might have become of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not seen him, because my father has forbidden it,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine said\r\nvery simply.\r\n\r\nThere was a simplicity in this, indeed, which fairly vexed Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf your father forbade you to go to sleep, I suppose you would keep\r\nawake!\xe2\x80\x9d she commented.\r\n\r\nCatherine looked at her.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you.  You seem to be very\r\nstrange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear, you will understand me some day!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Mrs. Penniman, who\r\nwas reading the evening paper, which she perused daily from the first\r\nline to the last, resumed her occupation.  She wrapped herself in\r\nsilence; she was determined Catherine should ask her for an account of\r\nher interview with Morris.  But Catherine was silent for so long, that\r\nshe almost lost patience; and she was on the point of remarking to her\r\nthat she was very heartless, when the girl at last spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he say?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said he is ready to marry you any day, in spite of everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine made no answer to this, and Mrs. Penniman almost lost patience\r\nagain; owing to which she at last volunteered the information that Morris\r\nlooked very handsome, but terribly haggard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he seem sad?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her niece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was dark under the eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cSo different from\r\nwhen I first saw him; though I am not sure that if I had seen him in this\r\ncondition the first time, I should not have been even more struck with\r\nhim.  There is something brilliant in his very misery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was, to Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s sense, a vivid picture, and though she\r\ndisapproved, she felt herself gazing at it.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you see him?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked presently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn\xe2\x80\x94in the Bowery; at a confectioner\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, who had a\r\ngeneral idea that she ought to dissemble a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhereabouts is the place?\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine inquired, after another pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish to go there, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d said her aunt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Catherine got up from her seat and went to the fire, where\r\nshe stood looking a while at the glowing coals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so dry, Catherine?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo dry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo cold\xe2\x80\x94so irresponsive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl turned very quickly.  \xe2\x80\x9cDid _he_ say that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman hesitated a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you what he said.  He\r\nsaid he feared only one thing\xe2\x80\x94that you would be afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid of what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid of your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine turned back to the fire again, and then, after a pause, she\r\nsaid\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI _am_ afraid of my father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman got quickly up from her chair and approached her niece.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to give him up, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine for some time never moved; she kept her eyes on the coals.  At\r\nlast she raised her head and looked at her aunt.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you push me\r\nso?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t push you.  When have I spoken to you before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that you have spoken to me several times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid it is necessary, then, Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with\r\na good deal of solemnity.  \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you don\xe2\x80\x99t feel the importance\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe paused a little; Catherine was looking at her.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe importance of\r\nnot disappointing that gallant young heart!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Mrs. Penniman went back\r\nto her chair, by the lamp, and, with a little jerk, picked up the evening\r\npaper again.\r\n\r\nCatherine stood there before the fire, with her hands behind her, looking\r\nat her aunt, to whom it seemed that the girl had never had just this dark\r\nfixedness in her gaze.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you understand\xe2\x80\x94or that you know\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I don\xe2\x80\x99t, it is not wonderful; you trust me so little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine made no attempt to deny this charge, and for some time more\r\nnothing was said.  But Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s imagination was restless, and the\r\nevening paper failed on this occasion to enchain it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you succumb to the dread of your father\xe2\x80\x99s wrath,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what will become of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid _he_ tell you to say these things to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe told me to use my influence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must be mistaken,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe trusts me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope he may never repent of it!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Mrs. Penniman gave a little\r\nsharp slap to her newspaper.  She knew not what to make of her niece, who\r\nhad suddenly become stern and contradictious.\r\n\r\nThis tendency on Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s part was presently even more apparent.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nhad much better not make any more appointments with Mr. Townsend,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman rose with considerable majesty.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor child, are you\r\njealous of me?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Aunt Lavinia!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Catherine, blushing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is your place to teach me what is right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn this point Catherine made no concession.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt can\xe2\x80\x99t be right to\r\ndeceive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly have not deceived _you_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but I promised my father\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt you promised your father.  But I have promised him\r\nnothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine had to admit this, and she did so in silence.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe\r\nMr. Townsend himself likes it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t like meeting me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not in secret; the place was full of people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it was a secret place\xe2\x80\x94away off in the Bowery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman flinched a little.  \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen enjoy such things,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nremarked presently.  \xe2\x80\x9cI know what gentlemen like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it, if he knew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, do you propose to inform him?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Aunt Lavinia.  But please don\xe2\x80\x99t do it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I do it again, you will inform him: is that what you mean?  I do not\r\nshare your dread of my brother; I have always known how to defend my own\r\nposition.  But I shall certainly never again take any step on your\r\nbehalf; you are much too thankless.  I knew you were not a spontaneous\r\nnature, but I believed you were firm, and I told your father that he\r\nwould find you so.  I am disappointed\xe2\x80\x94but your father will not be!\xe2\x80\x9d  And\r\nwith this, Mrs. Penniman offered her niece a brief good-night, and\r\nwithdrew to her own apartment.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nCATHERINE sat alone by the parlour fire\xe2\x80\x94sat there for more than an hour,\r\nlost in her meditations.  Her aunt seemed to her aggressive and foolish,\r\nand to see it so clearly\xe2\x80\x94to judge Mrs. Penniman so positively\xe2\x80\x94made her\r\nfeel old and grave.  She did not resent the imputation of weakness; it\r\nmade no impression on her, for she had not the sense of weakness, and she\r\nwas not hurt at not being appreciated.  She had an immense respect for\r\nher father, and she felt that to displease him would be a misdemeanour\r\nanalogous to an act of profanity in a great temple; but her purpose had\r\nslowly ripened, and she believed that her prayers had purified it of its\r\nviolence.  The evening advanced, and the lamp burned dim without her\r\nnoticing it; her eyes were fixed upon her terrible plan.  She knew her\r\nfather was in his study\xe2\x80\x94that he had been there all the evening; from time\r\nto time she expected to hear him move.  She thought he would perhaps\r\ncome, as he sometimes came, into the parlour.  At last the clock struck\r\neleven, and the house was wrapped in silence; the servants had gone to\r\nbed.  Catherine got up and went slowly to the door of the library, where\r\nshe waited a moment, motionless.  Then she knocked, and then she waited\r\nagain.  Her father had answered her, but she had not the courage to turn\r\nthe latch.  What she had said to her aunt was true enough\xe2\x80\x94she was afraid\r\nof him; and in saying that she had no sense of weakness she meant that\r\nshe was not afraid of herself.  She heard him move within, and he came\r\nand opened the door for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou are standing there like a\r\nghost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went into the room, but it was some time before she contrived to say\r\nwhat she had come to say.  Her father, who was in his dressing-gown and\r\nslippers, had been busy at his writing-table, and after looking at her\r\nfor some moments, and waiting for her to speak, he went and seated\r\nhimself at his papers again.  His back was turned to her\xe2\x80\x94she began to\r\nhear the scratching of his pen.  She remained near the door, with her\r\nheart thumping beneath her bodice; and she was very glad that his back\r\nwas turned, for it seemed to her that she could more easily address\r\nherself to this portion of his person than to his face.  At last she\r\nbegan, watching it while she spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou told me that if I should have anything more to say about Mr.\r\nTownsend you would be glad to listen to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, not turning round, but stopping his\r\npen.\r\n\r\nCatherine wished it would go on, but she herself continued.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I\r\nwould tell you that I have not seen him again, but that I should like to\r\ndo so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo bid him good-bye?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor.\r\n\r\nThe girl hesitated a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is not going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor wheeled slowly round in his chair, with a smile that seemed to\r\naccuse her of an epigram; but extremes meet, and Catherine had not\r\nintended one.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not to bid him good-bye, then?\xe2\x80\x9d her father said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, father, not that; at least, not for ever.  I have not seen him\r\nagain, but I should like to see him,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine repeated.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor slowly rubbed his under lip with the feather of his quill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you written to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, four times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not dismissed him, then.  Once would have done that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cI have asked him\xe2\x80\x94asked him to wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer father sat looking at her, and she was afraid he was going to break\r\nout into wrath; his eyes were so fine and cold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a dear, faithful child,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.  \xe2\x80\x9cCome here to your\r\nfather.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he got up, holding out his hands toward her.\r\n\r\nThe words were a surprise, and they gave her an exquisite joy.  She went\r\nto him, and he put his arm round her tenderly, soothingly; and then he\r\nkissed her.  After this he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish to make me very happy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to\xe2\x80\x94but I am afraid I can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can if you will.  It all depends on your will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it to give him up?\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is to give him up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he held her still, with the same tenderness, looking into her face\r\nand resting his eyes on her averted eyes.  There was a long silence; she\r\nwished he would release her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are happier than I, father,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt you are unhappy just now.  But it is better to be\r\nunhappy for three months and get over it, than for many years and never\r\nget over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if that were so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be so; I am sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d  She answered nothing, and he went\r\non.  \xe2\x80\x9cHave you no faith in my wisdom, in my tenderness, in my solicitude\r\nfor your future?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you suppose that I know something of men: their vices, their\r\nfollies, their falsities?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe detached herself, and turned upon him.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is not vicious\xe2\x80\x94he is not\r\nfalse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer father kept looking at her with his sharp, pure eye.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou make\r\nnothing of my judgement, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t believe that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to believe it, but to take it on trust.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine was far from saying to herself that this was an ingenious\r\nsophism; but she met the appeal none the less squarely.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has he\r\ndone\xe2\x80\x94what do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has never done anything\xe2\x80\x94he is a selfish idler.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father, don\xe2\x80\x99t abuse him!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed pleadingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to abuse him; it would be a great mistake.  You may do as\r\nyou choose,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may see him again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as you choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you forgive me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy no means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will only be for once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by once.  You must either give him up or\r\ncontinue the acquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to explain\xe2\x80\x94to tell him to wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo wait for what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill you know him better\xe2\x80\x94till you consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell him any such nonsense as that.  I know him well enough, and I\r\nshall never consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we can wait a long time,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Catherine, in a tone which was\r\nmeant to express the humblest conciliation, but which had upon her\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s nerves the effect of an iteration not characterised by tact.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor answered, however, quietly enough: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course you can wait\r\ntill I die, if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine gave a cry of natural horror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour engagement will have one delightful effect upon you; it will make\r\nyou extremely impatient for that event.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine stood staring, and the Doctor enjoyed the point he had made.\r\nIt came to Catherine with the force\xe2\x80\x94or rather with the vague\r\nimpressiveness\xe2\x80\x94of a logical axiom which it was not in her province to\r\ncontrovert; and yet, though it was a scientific truth, she felt wholly\r\nunable to accept it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather not marry, if that were true,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me a proof of it, then; for it is beyond a question that by\r\nengaging yourself to Morris Townsend you simply wait for my death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned away, feeling sick and faint; and the Doctor went on.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if\r\nyou wait for it with impatience, judge, if you please, what _his_\r\neagerness will be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine turned it over\xe2\x80\x94her father\xe2\x80\x99s words had such an authority for her\r\nthat her very thoughts were capable of obeying him.  There was a dreadful\r\nugliness in it, which seemed to glare at her through the interposing\r\nmedium of her own feebler reason.  Suddenly, however, she had an\r\ninspiration\xe2\x80\x94she almost knew it to be an inspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I don\xe2\x80\x99t marry before your death, I will not after,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nTo her father, it must be admitted, this seemed only another epigram; and\r\nas obstinacy, in unaccomplished minds, does not usually select such a\r\nmode of expression, he was the more surprised at this wanton play of a\r\nfixed idea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that for an impertinence?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired; an inquiry of which,\r\nas he made it, he quite perceived the grossness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn impertinence?  Oh, father, what terrible things you say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t wait for my death, you might as well marry immediately;\r\nthere is nothing else to wait for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor some time Catherine made no answer; but finally she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Morris\xe2\x80\x94little by little\xe2\x80\x94might persuade you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never let him speak to me again.  I dislike him too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine gave a long, low sigh; she tried to stifle it, for she had made\r\nup her mind that it was wrong to make a parade of her trouble, and to\r\nendeavour to act upon her father by the meretricious aid of emotion.\r\nIndeed, she even thought it wrong\xe2\x80\x94in the sense of being inconsiderate\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nattempt to act upon his feelings at all; her part was to effect some\r\ngentle, gradual change in his intellectual perception of poor Morris\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncharacter.  But the means of effecting such a change were at present\r\nshrouded in mystery, and she felt miserably helpless and hopeless.  She\r\nhad exhausted all arguments, all replies.  Her father might have pitied\r\nher, and in fact he did so; but he was sure he was right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is one thing you can tell Mr. Townsend when you see him again,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cthat if you marry without my consent, I don\xe2\x80\x99t leave you a farthing\r\nof money.  That will interest him more than anything else you can tell\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be very right,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cI ought not in that\r\ncase to have a farthing of your money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor observed, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cyour simplicity is\r\ntouching.  Make that remark, in that tone, and with that expression of\r\ncountenance, to Mr. Townsend, and take a note of his answer.  It won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\npolite\xe2\x80\x94it will, express irritation; and I shall be glad of that, as it\r\nwill put me in the right; unless, indeed\xe2\x80\x94which is perfectly possible\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nshould like him the better for being rude to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will never be rude to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him what I say, all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at her father, and her quiet eyes filled with tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I will see him, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, in her timid voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly as you choose!\xe2\x80\x9d  And he went to the door and opened it for her\r\nto go out.  The movement gave her a terrible sense of his turning her\r\noff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be only once, for the present,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, lingering a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly as you choose,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, standing there with his hand on the\r\ndoor.  \xe2\x80\x9cI have told you what I think.  If you see him, you will be an\r\nungrateful, cruel child; you will have given your old father the greatest\r\npain of his life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was more than the poor girl could bear; her tears overflowed, and\r\nshe moved towards her grimly consistent parent with a pitiful cry.  Her\r\nhands were raised in supplication, but he sternly evaded this appeal.\r\nInstead of letting her sob out her misery on his shoulder, he simply took\r\nher by the arm and directed her course across the threshold, closing the\r\ndoor gently but firmly behind her.  After he had done so, he remained\r\nlistening.  For a long time there was no sound; he knew that she was\r\nstanding outside.  He was sorry for her, as I have said; but he was so\r\nsure he was right.  At last he heard her move away, and then her footstep\r\ncreaked faintly upon the stairs.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor took several turns round his study, with his hands in his\r\npockets, and a thin sparkle, possibly of irritation, but partly also of\r\nsomething like humour, in his eye.  \xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nbelieve she will stick\xe2\x80\x94I believe she will stick!\xe2\x80\x9d  And this idea of\r\nCatherine \xe2\x80\x9csticking\xe2\x80\x9d appeared to have a comical side, and to offer a\r\nprospect of entertainment.  He determined, as he said to himself, to see\r\nit out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was for reasons connected with this determination that on the morrow\r\nhe sought a few words of private conversation with Mrs. Penniman.  He\r\nsent for her to the library, and he there informed her that he hoped very\r\nmuch that, as regarded this affair of Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s, she would mind her\r\n_p\xe2\x80\x99s_ and _q\xe2\x80\x99s_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by such an expression,\xe2\x80\x9d said his sister.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak as if I were learning the alphabet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe alphabet of common sense is something you will never learn,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nDoctor permitted himself to respond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you called me here to insult me?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all.  Simply to advise you.  You have taken up young Townsend;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s your own affair.  I have nothing to do with your sentiments, your\r\nfancies, your affections, your delusions; but what I request of you is\r\nthat you will keep these things to yourself.  I have explained my views\r\nto Catherine; she understands them perfectly, and anything that she does\r\nfurther in the way of encouraging Mr. Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s attentions will be in\r\ndeliberate opposition to my wishes.  Anything that you should do in the\r\nway of giving her aid and comfort will be\xe2\x80\x94permit me the\r\nexpression\xe2\x80\x94distinctly treasonable.  You know high treason is a capital\r\noffence; take care how you incur the penalty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman threw back her head, with a certain expansion of the eye\r\nwhich she occasionally practised.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that you talk like a\r\ngreat autocrat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI talk like my daughter\xe2\x80\x99s father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot like your sister\xe2\x80\x99s brother!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lavinia.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Lavinia,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes wonder whether I am your brother.  We are so\r\nextremely different.  In spite of differences, however, we can, at a\r\npinch, understand each other; and that is the essential thing just now.\r\nWalk straight with regard to Mr. Townsend; that\xe2\x80\x99s all I ask.  It is\r\nhighly probable you have been corresponding with him for the last three\r\nweeks\xe2\x80\x94perhaps even seeing him.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you\xe2\x80\x94you needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d  He\r\nhad a moral conviction that she would contrive to tell a fib about the\r\nmatter, which it would disgust him to listen to.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever you have\r\ndone, stop doing it.  That\xe2\x80\x99s all I wish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you wish also by chance to murder our child?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary, I wish to make her live and be happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will kill her; she passed a dreadful night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe won\xe2\x80\x99t die of one dreadful night, nor of a dozen.  Remember that I am\r\na distinguished physician.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman hesitated a moment.  Then she risked her retort.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\nbeing a distinguished physician has not prevented you from already losing\r\n_two members_ of your family!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had risked it, but her brother gave her such a terribly incisive\r\nlook\xe2\x80\x94a look so like a surgeon\xe2\x80\x99s lancet\xe2\x80\x94that she was frightened at her\r\ncourage.  And he answered her in words that corresponded to the look: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nmay not prevent me, either, from losing the society of still another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman took herself off, with whatever air of depreciated merit\r\nwas at her command, and repaired to Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s room, where the poor girl\r\nwas closeted.  She knew all about her dreadful night, for the two had met\r\nagain, the evening before, after Catherine left her father.  Mrs.\r\nPenniman was on the landing of the second floor when her niece came\r\nupstairs.  It was not remarkable that a person of so much subtlety should\r\nhave discovered that Catherine had been shut up with the Doctor.  It was\r\nstill less remarkable that she should have felt an extreme curiosity to\r\nlearn the result of this interview, and that this sentiment, combined\r\nwith her great amiability and generosity, should have prompted her to\r\nregret the sharp words lately exchanged between her niece and herself.\r\nAs the unhappy girl came into sight, in the dusky corridor, she made a\r\nlively demonstration of sympathy.  Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s bursting heart was equally\r\noblivious.  She only knew that her aunt was taking her into her arms.\r\nMrs. Penniman drew her into Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s own room, and the two women sat\r\nthere together, far into the small hours; the younger one with her head\r\non the other\xe2\x80\x99s lap, sobbing and sobbing at first in a soundless, stifled\r\nmanner, and then at last perfectly still.  It gratified Mrs. Penniman to\r\nbe able to feel conscientiously that this scene virtually removed the\r\ninterdict which Catherine had placed upon her further communion with\r\nMorris Townsend.  She was not gratified, however, when, in coming back to\r\nher niece\xe2\x80\x99s room before breakfast, she found that Catherine had risen and\r\nwas preparing herself for this meal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not go to breakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not well enough,\r\nafter your fearful night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am very well, and I am only afraid of being late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t understand you!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman cried.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou should stay in bed\r\nfor three days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I could never do that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, to whom this idea presented\r\nno attractions.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was in despair, and she noted, with extreme annoyance, that\r\nthe trace of the night\xe2\x80\x99s tears had completely vanished from Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes.  She had a most impracticable _physique_.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat effect do you\r\nexpect to have upon your father,\xe2\x80\x9d her aunt demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cif you come\r\nplumping down, without a vestige of any sort of feeling, as if nothing in\r\nthe world had happened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would not like me to lie in bed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more reason for your doing it.  How else do you expect to move\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine thought a little.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how; but not in that way.  I\r\nwish to be just as usual.\xe2\x80\x9d  And she finished dressing, and, according to\r\nher aunt\xe2\x80\x99s expression, went plumping down into the paternal presence.\r\nShe was really too modest for consistent pathos.\r\n\r\nAnd yet it was perfectly true that she had had a dreadful night.  Even\r\nafter Mrs. Penniman left her she had had no sleep.  She lay staring at\r\nthe uncomforting gloom, with her eyes and ears filled with the movement\r\nwith which her father had turned her out of his room, and of the words in\r\nwhich he had told her that she was a heartless daughter.  Her heart was\r\nbreaking.  She had heart enough for that.  At moments it seemed to her\r\nthat she believed him, and that to do what she was doing, a girl must\r\nindeed be bad.  She _was_ bad; but she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it.  She would try\r\nto appear good, even if her heart were perverted; and from time to time\r\nshe had a fancy that she might accomplish something by ingenious\r\nconcessions to form, though she should persist in caring for Morris.\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s ingenuities were indefinite, and we are not called upon to\r\nexpose their hollowness.  The best of them perhaps showed itself in that\r\nfreshness of aspect which was so discouraging to Mrs. Penniman, who was\r\namazed at the absence of haggardness in a young woman who for a whole\r\nnight had lain quivering beneath a father\xe2\x80\x99s curse.  Poor Catherine was\r\nconscious of her freshness; it gave her a feeling about the future which\r\nrather added to the weight upon her mind.  It seemed a proof that she was\r\nstrong and solid and dense, and would live to a great age\xe2\x80\x94longer than\r\nmight be generally convenient; and this idea was depressing, for it\r\nappeared to saddle her with a pretension the more, just when the\r\ncultivation of any pretension was inconsistent with her doing right.  She\r\nwrote that day to Morris Townsend, requesting him to come and see her on\r\nthe morrow; using very few words, and explaining nothing.  She would\r\nexplain everything face to face.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXX\r\n\r\n\r\nON the morrow, in the afternoon, she heard his voice at the door, and his\r\nstep in the hall.  She received him in the big, bright front parlour, and\r\nshe instructed the servant that if any one should call she was\r\nparticularly engaged.  She was not afraid of her father\xe2\x80\x99s coming in, for\r\nat that hour he was always driving about town.  When Morris stood there\r\nbefore her, the first thing that she was conscious of was that he was\r\neven more beautiful to look at than fond recollection had painted him;\r\nthe next was that he had pressed her in his arms.  When she was free\r\nagain it appeared to her that she had now indeed thrown herself into the\r\ngulf of defiance, and even, for an instant, that she had been married to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nHe told her that she had been very cruel, and had made him very unhappy;\r\nand Catherine felt acutely the difficulty of her destiny, which forced\r\nher to give pain in such opposite quarters.  But she wished that, instead\r\nof reproaches, however tender, he would give her help; he was certainly\r\nwise enough, and clever enough, to invent some issue from their troubles.\r\nShe expressed this belief, and Morris received the assurance as if he\r\nthought it natural; but he interrogated, at first\xe2\x80\x94as was natural\r\ntoo\xe2\x80\x94rather than committed himself to marking out a course.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not have made me wait so long,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how I\r\nhave been living; every hour seemed like years.  You should have decided\r\nsooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecided?\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecided whether you would keep me or give me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Morris,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, with a long tender murmur, \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of\r\ngiving you up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, then, were you waiting for?\xe2\x80\x9d  The young man was ardently logical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought my father might\xe2\x80\x94might\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and she hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight see how unhappy you were?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no!  But that he might look at it differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now you have sent for me to tell me that at last he does so.  Is\r\nthat it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis hypothetical optimism gave the poor girl a pang.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, Morris,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid solemnly, \xe2\x80\x9che looks at it still in the same way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why have you sent for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I wanted to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Catherine piteously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s an excellent reason, surely.  But did you want to look at me\r\nonly?  Have you nothing to tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis beautiful persuasive eyes were fixed upon her face, and she wondered\r\nwhat answer would be noble enough to make to such a gaze as that.  For a\r\nmoment her own eyes took it in, and then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI _did_ want to look at you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said gently.  But after this speech, most inconsistently, she hid her\r\nface.\r\n\r\nMorris watched her for a moment, attentively.  \xe2\x80\x9cWill you marry me\r\nto-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext week, then.  Any time within a month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it better to wait?\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo wait for what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hardly knew for what; but this tremendous leap alarmed her.  \xe2\x80\x9cTill we\r\nhave thought about it a little more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook his head, sadly and reproachfully.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had been\r\nthinking about it these three weeks.  Do you want to turn it over in your\r\nmind for five years?  You have given me more than time enough.  My poor\r\ngirl,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not sincere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine coloured from brow to chin, and her eyes filled with tears.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how can you say that?\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you must take me or leave me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, very reasonably.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t please your father and me both; you must choose between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have chosen you!\xe2\x80\x9d she said passionately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen marry me next week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stood gazing at him.  \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t there any other way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone that I know of for arriving at the same result.  If there is, I\r\nshould be happy to hear of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine could think of nothing of the kind, and Morris\xe2\x80\x99s luminosity\r\nseemed almost pitiless.
