The only thing she could think of was that her\r\nfather might, after all, come round, and she articulated, with an awkward\r\nsense of her helplessness in doing so, a wish that this miracle might\r\nhappen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it is in the least degree likely?\xe2\x80\x9d Morris asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be, if he could only know you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can know me if he will.  What is to prevent it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis ideas, his reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cThey are so\xe2\x80\x94so terribly\r\nstrong.\xe2\x80\x9d  She trembled with the recollection of them yet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrong?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cI would rather you should think them weak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing about my father is weak!\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl.\r\n\r\nMorris turned away, walking to the window, where he stood looking out.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are terribly afraid of him!\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked at last.\r\n\r\nShe felt no impulse to deny it, because she had no shame in it; for if it\r\nwas no honour to herself, at least it was an honour to him.  \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I\r\nmust be,\xe2\x80\x9d she said simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you don\xe2\x80\x99t love me\xe2\x80\x94not as I love you.  If you fear your father more\r\nthan you love me, then your love is not what I hoped it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my friend!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, going to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo _I_ fear anything?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded, turning round on her.  \xe2\x80\x9cFor your\r\nsake what am I not ready to face?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are noble\xe2\x80\x94you are brave!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, stopping short at a distance\r\nthat was almost respectful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmall good it does me, if you are so timid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think that I am\xe2\x80\x94_really_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by \xe2\x80\x98really.\xe2\x80\x99  It is really enough to make us\r\nmiserable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be strong enough to wait\xe2\x80\x94to wait a long time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd suppose after a long time your father should hate me worse than\r\never?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would be touched by my fidelity?  Is that what you mean?  If he is so\r\neasily touched, then why should you be afraid of him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was much to the point, and Catherine was struck by it.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will try\r\nnot to be,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  And she stood there submissively, the image, in\r\nadvance, of a dutiful and responsible wife.  This image could not fail to\r\nrecommend itself to Morris Townsend, and he continued to give proof of\r\nthe high estimation in which he held her.  It could only have been at the\r\nprompting of such a sentiment that he presently mentioned to her that the\r\ncourse recommended by Mrs. Penniman was an immediate union, regardless of\r\nconsequences.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Aunt Penniman would like that,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine said simply\xe2\x80\x94and yet with\r\na certain shrewdness.  It must, however, have been in pure simplicity,\r\nand from motives quite untouched by sarcasm, that, a few moments after,\r\nshe went on to say to Morris that her father had given her a message for\r\nhim.  It was quite on her conscience to deliver this message, and had the\r\nmission been ten times more painful she would have as scrupulously\r\nperformed it.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe told me to tell you\xe2\x80\x94to tell you very distinctly, and\r\ndirectly from himself, that if I marry without his consent, I shall not\r\ninherit a penny of his fortune.  He made a great point of this.  He\r\nseemed to think\xe2\x80\x94he seemed to think\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris flushed, as any young man of spirit might have flushed at an\r\nimputation of baseness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he seem to think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat it would make a difference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _will_ make a difference\xe2\x80\x94in many things.  We shall be by many\r\nthousands of dollars the poorer; and that is a great difference.  But it\r\nwill make none in my affection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall not want the money,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cfor you know I have a\r\ngood deal myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my dear girl, I know you have something. I disappoint every one\xe2\x80\x94father and Aunt\r\nPenniman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter with me, because I am fonder of you than they\r\nare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Morris,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, with her imagination\xe2\x80\x94what there was of\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94swimming in this happy truth, which seemed, after all, invidious to no\r\none.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it your belief that he will stick to it\xe2\x80\x94stick to it for ever, to this\r\nidea of disinheriting you?\xe2\x80\x94that your goodness and patience will never\r\nwear out his cruelty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trouble is that if I marry you, he will think I am not good. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere, suddenly, Morris was interrupted; these ingenious inquiries had not\r\nreached Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s ears.  The terrible word \xe2\x80\x9cdisinheritance,\xe2\x80\x9d with all\r\nits impressive moral reprobation, was still ringing there; seemed indeed\r\nto gather force as it lingered.  The mortal chill of her situation struck\r\nmore deeply into her child-like heart, and she was overwhelmed by a\r\nfeeling of loneliness and danger.  But her refuge was there, close to\r\nher, and she put out her hands to grasp it.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Morris,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with\r\na shudder, \xe2\x80\x9cI will marry you as soon as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d  And she surrendered\r\nherself, leaning her head on his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear good girl!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, looking down at his prize.  And then\r\nhe looked up again, rather vaguely, with parted lips and lifted eyebrows.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXI\r\n\r\n\r\nDR. SLOPER very soon imparted his conviction to Mrs. Almond, in the same\r\nterms in which he had announced it to himself.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s going to stick, by\r\nJove! she\xe2\x80\x99s going to stick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that she is going to marry him?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that; but she is not going to break down.  She is going to\r\ndrag out the engagement, in the hope of making me relent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd shall you not relent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall a geometrical proposition relent?  I am not so superficial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t geometry treat of surfaces?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Almond, who, as we know,\r\nwas clever, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but it treats of them profoundly.  Catherine and her young man are\r\nmy surfaces; I have taken their measure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak as if it surprised you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is immense; there will be a great deal to observe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are shockingly cold-blooded!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI need to be with all this hot blood about me.  Young Townsend indeed is\r\ncool; I must allow him that merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t judge him,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond answered; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am not at all surprised\r\nat Catherine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess I am a little; she must have been so deucedly divided and\r\nbothered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay it amuses you outright!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why it should be such a joke\r\nthat your daughter adores you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the point where the adoration stops that I find it interesting to\r\nfix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt stops where the other sentiment begins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all\xe2\x80\x94that would be simple enough.  The two things are extremely\r\nmixed up, and the mixture is extremely odd.  It will produce some third\r\nelement, and that\xe2\x80\x99s what I am waiting to see.  I wait with suspense\xe2\x80\x94with\r\npositive excitement; and that is a sort of emotion that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose\r\nCatherine would ever provide for me.  I am really very much obliged to\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will cling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond; \xe2\x80\x9cshe will certainly cling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; as I say, she will stick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCling is prettier.  That\xe2\x80\x99s what those very simple natures always do, and\r\nnothing could be simpler than Catherine.  She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t take many\r\nimpressions; but when she takes one she keeps it.  She is like a copper\r\nkettle that receives a dent; you may polish up the kettle, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nefface the mark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must try and polish up Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will take her\r\nto Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe won\xe2\x80\x99t forget him in Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will forget her, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Almond looked grave.  \xe2\x80\x9cShould you really like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExtremely!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, meanwhile, lost little time in putting herself again in\r\ncommunication with Morris Townsend.  She requested him to favour her with\r\nanother interview, but she did not on this occasion select an oyster\r\nsaloon as the scene of their meeting.  She proposed that he should join\r\nher at the door of a certain church, after service on Sunday afternoon,\r\nand she was careful not to appoint the place of worship which she usually\r\nvisited, and where, as she said, the congregation would have spied upon\r\nher.  She picked out a less elegant resort, and on issuing from its\r\nportal at the hour she had fixed she saw the young man standing apart.\r\nShe offered him no recognition till she had crossed the street and he had\r\nfollowed her to some distance.  Here, with a smile\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse my apparent\r\nwant of cordiality,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou know what to believe about that.\r\nPrudence before everything.\xe2\x80\x9d  And on his asking her in what direction\r\nthey should walk, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere we shall be least observed,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\nMorris was not in high good-humour, and his response to this speech was\r\nnot particularly gallant.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t flatter myself we shall be much\r\nobserved anywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d  Then he turned recklessly toward the centre of the\r\ntown.  \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you have come to tell me that he has knocked under,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwent on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I am not altogether a harbinger of good; and yet, too, I am\r\nto a certain extent a messenger of peace.  I have been thinking a great\r\ndeal, Mr. Townsend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I do; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it, my mind is so terribly active.\r\nWhen I give myself, I give myself.  I pay the penalty in my headaches, my\r\nfamous headaches\xe2\x80\x94a perfect circlet of pain!  But I carry it as a queen\r\ncarries her crown.  Would you believe that I have one now?  I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nhowever, have missed our rendezvous for anything.  I have something very\r\nimportant to tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let\xe2\x80\x99s have it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was perhaps a little headlong the other day in advising you to marry\r\nimmediately.  I have been thinking it over, and now I see it just a\r\nlittle differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to have a great many different ways of seeing the same object.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTheir number is infinite!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, in a tone which seemed to\r\nsuggest that this convenient faculty was one of her brightest attributes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI recommend you to take one way and stick to it,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but it isn\xe2\x80\x99t easy to choose.  My imagination is never quiet, never\r\nsatisfied.  It makes me a bad adviser, perhaps; but it makes me a capital\r\nfriend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA capital friend who gives bad advice!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot intentionally\xe2\x80\x94and who hurries off, at every risk, to make the most\r\nhumble excuses!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you advise me now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be very patient; to watch and wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is that bad advice or good?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not for me to say,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman rejoined, with some dignity.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only pretend it\xe2\x80\x99s sincere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you come to me next week and recommend something different and\r\nequally sincere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may come to you next week and tell you that I am in the streets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the streets?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had a terrible scene with my brother, and he threatens, if\r\nanything happens, to turn me out of the house.  You know I am a poor\r\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris had a speculative idea that she had a little property; but he\r\nnaturally did not press this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be very sorry to see you suffer martyrdom for me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you make your brother out a regular Turk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman hesitated a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly do not regard Austin as a satisfactory Christian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd am I to wait till he is converted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait, at any rate, till he is less violent.  Bide your time, Mr.\r\nTownsend; remember the prize is great!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris walked along some time in silence, tapping the railings and\r\ngateposts very sharply with his stick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly are devilish inconsistent!\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out at last.  \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nalready got Catherine to consent to a private marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was indeed inconsistent, for at this news she gave a little\r\njump of gratification.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! when and where?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.  And then she stopped short.\r\n\r\nMorris was a little vague about this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat isn\xe2\x80\x99t fixed; but she consents.  It\xe2\x80\x99s deuced awkward, now, to back\r\nout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, as I say, had stopped short; and she stood there with her\r\neyes fixed brilliantly on her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Townsend,\xe2\x80\x9d she proceeded, \xe2\x80\x9cshall I tell you something?  Catherine\r\nloves you so much that you may do anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis declaration was slightly ambiguous, and Morris opened his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am happy to hear it!  But what do you mean by \xe2\x80\x98anything\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may postpone\xe2\x80\x94you may change about; she won\xe2\x80\x99t think the worse of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris stood there still, with his raised eyebrows; then he said simply\r\nand rather dryly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d  After this he remarked to Mrs. Penniman that if\r\nshe walked so slowly she would attract notice, and he succeeded, after a\r\nfashion, in hurrying her back to the domicile of which her tenure had\r\nbecome so insecure.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXII\r\n\r\n\r\nHE had slightly misrepresented the matter in saying that Catherine had\r\nconsented to take the great step.  We left her just now declaring that\r\nshe would burn her ships behind her; but Morris, after having elicited\r\nthis declaration, had become conscious of good reasons for not taking it\r\nup.  He avoided, gracefully enough, fixing a day, though he left her\r\nunder the impression that he had his eye on one.  Catherine may have had\r\nher difficulties; but those of her circumspect suitor are also worthy of\r\nconsideration.  The prize was certainly great; but it was only to be won\r\nby striking the happy mean between precipitancy and caution.  It would be\r\nall very well to take one\xe2\x80\x99s jump and trust to Providence; Providence was\r\nmore especially on the side of clever people, and clever people were\r\nknown by an indisposition to risk their bones.  The ultimate reward of a\r\nunion with a young woman who was both unattractive and impoverished ought\r\nto be connected with immediate disadvantages by some very palpable chain.\r\nBetween the fear of losing Catherine and her possible fortune altogether,\r\nand the fear of taking her too soon and finding this possible fortune as\r\nvoid of actuality as a collection of emptied bottles, it was not\r\ncomfortable for Morris Townsend to choose; a fact that should be\r\nremembered by readers disposed to judge harshly of a young man who may\r\nhave struck them as making but an indifferently successful use of fine\r\nnatural parts.  He had not forgotten that in any event Catherine had her\r\nown ten thousand a year; he had devoted an abundance of meditation to\r\nthis circumstance.  But with his fine parts he rated himself high, and he\r\nhad a perfectly definite appreciation of his value, which seemed to him\r\ninadequately represented by the sum I have mentioned.  At the same time\r\nhe reminded himself that this sum was considerable, that everything is\r\nrelative, and that if a modest income is less desirable than a large one,\r\nthe complete absence of revenue is nowhere accounted an advantage.  These\r\nreflexions gave him plenty of occupation, and made it necessary that he\r\nshould trim his sail.  Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s opposition was the unknown quantity\r\nin the problem he had to work out.  The natural way to work it out was by\r\nmarrying Catherine; but in mathematics there are many short cuts, and\r\nMorris was not without a hope that he should yet discover one.  When\r\nCatherine took him at his word and consented to renounce the attempt to\r\nmollify her father, he drew back skilfully enough, as I have said, and\r\nkept the wedding-day still an open question.  Her faith in his sincerity\r\nwas so complete that she was incapable of suspecting that he was playing\r\nwith her; her trouble just now was of another kind.  The poor girl had an\r\nadmirable sense of honour; and from the moment she had brought herself to\r\nthe point of violating her father\xe2\x80\x99s wish, it seemed to her that she had\r\nno right to enjoy his protection.  It was on her conscience that she\r\nought to live under his roof only so long as she conformed to his wisdom.\r\nThere was a great deal of glory in such a position, but poor Catherine\r\nfelt that she had forfeited her claim to it.  She had cast her lot with a\r\nyoung man against whom he had solemnly warned her, and broken the\r\ncontract under which he provided her with a happy home.  She could not\r\ngive up the young man, so she must leave the home; and the sooner the\r\nobject of her preference offered her another the sooner her situation\r\nwould lose its awkward twist.  This was close reasoning; but it was\r\ncommingled with an infinite amount of merely instinctive penitence.\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s days at this time were dismal, and the weight of some of her\r\nhours was almost more than she could bear.  Her father never looked at\r\nher, never spoke to her.  He knew perfectly what he was about, and this\r\nwas part of a plan.  She looked at him as much as she dared (for she was\r\nafraid of seeming to offer herself to his observation), and she pitied\r\nhim for the sorrow she had brought upon him.  She held up her head and\r\nbusied her hands, and went about her daily occupations; and when the\r\nstate of things in Washington Square seemed intolerable, she closed her\r\neyes and indulged herself with an intellectual vision of the man for\r\nwhose sake she had broken a sacred law.  Mrs. Penniman, of the three\r\npersons in Washington Square, had much the most of the manner that\r\nbelongs to a great crisis.  If Catherine was quiet, she was quietly\r\nquiet, as I may say, and her pathetic effects, which there was no one to\r\nnotice, were entirely unstudied and unintended.  If the Doctor was stiff\r\nand dry and absolutely indifferent to the presence of his companions, it\r\nwas so lightly, neatly, easily done, that you would have had to know him\r\nwell to discover that, on the whole, he rather enjoyed having to be so\r\ndisagreeable.  But Mrs. Penniman was elaborately reserved and\r\nsignificantly silent; there was a richer rustle in the very deliberate\r\nmovements to which she confined herself, and when she occasionally spoke,\r\nin connexion with some very trivial event, she had the air of meaning\r\nsomething deeper than what she said.  Between Catherine and her father\r\nnothing had passed since the evening she went to speak to him in his\r\nstudy.  She had something to say to him\xe2\x80\x94it seemed to her she ought to say\r\nit; but she kept it back, for fear of irritating him.  He also had\r\nsomething to say to her; but he was determined not to speak first.  He\r\nwas interested, as we know, in seeing how, if she were left to herself,\r\nshe would \xe2\x80\x9cstick.\xe2\x80\x9d  At last she told him she had seen Morris Townsend\r\nagain, and that their relations remained quite the same.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we shall marry\xe2\x80\x94before very long.  And probably, meanwhile, I\r\nshall see him rather often; about once a week, not more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor looked at her coldly from head to foot, as if she had been a\r\nstranger.  It was the first time his eyes had rested on her for a week,\r\nwhich was fortunate, if that was to be their expression.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not three\r\ntimes a day?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat prevents your meeting as often as you\r\nchoose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned away a moment; there were tears in her eyes.  Then she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is better once a week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how it is better.  It is as bad as it can be.  If you\r\nflatter yourself that I care for little modifications of that sort, you\r\nare very much mistaken.  It is as wrong of you to see him once a week as\r\nit would be to see him all day long.  Not that it matters to me,\r\nhowever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine tried to follow these words, but they seemed to lead towards a\r\nvague horror from which she recoiled.  \xe2\x80\x9cI think we shall marry pretty\r\nsoon,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated at last.\r\n\r\nHer father gave her his dreadful look again, as if she were some one\r\nelse.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you tell me that?  It\xe2\x80\x99s no concern of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father!\xe2\x80\x9d she broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you care, even if you do feel so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a button.  Once you marry, it\xe2\x80\x99s quite the same to me when or where\r\nor why you do it; and if you think to compound for your folly by hoisting\r\nyour flag in this way, you may spare yourself the trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith this he turned away.  But the next day he spoke to her of his own\r\naccord, and his manner was somewhat changed.  \xe2\x80\x9cShall you be married\r\nwithin the next four or five months?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not very easy for us to\r\nmake up our minds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut it off, then, for six months, and in the meantime I will take you to\r\nEurope.  I should like you very much to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt gave her such delight, after his words of the day before, to hear that\r\nhe should \xe2\x80\x9clike\xe2\x80\x9d her to do something, and that he still had in his heart\r\nany of the tenderness of preference, that she gave a little exclamation\r\nof joy.  But then she became conscious that Morris was not included in\r\nthis proposal, and that\xe2\x80\x94as regards really going\xe2\x80\x94she would greatly prefer\r\nto remain at home with him.  But she blushed, none the less, more\r\ncomfortably than she had done of late.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be delightful to go to\r\nEurope,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked, with a sense that the idea was not original, and\r\nthat her tone was not all it might be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, we will go.  Pack up your clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had better tell Mr. Townsend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nHer father fixed his cold eyes upon her.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean that you had\r\nbetter ask his leave, all that remains to me is to hope he will give it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl was sharply touched by the pathetic ring of the words; it was\r\nthe most calculated, the most dramatic little speech the Doctor had ever\r\nuttered.  She felt that it was a great thing for her, under the\r\ncircumstances, to have this fine opportunity of showing him her respect;\r\nand yet there was something else that she felt as well, and that she\r\npresently expressed.  \xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes think that if I do what you dislike so\r\nmuch, I ought not to stay with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo stay with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I live with you, I ought to obey you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf that\xe2\x80\x99s your theory, it\xe2\x80\x99s certainly mine,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, with a dry\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if I don\xe2\x80\x99t obey you, I ought not to live with you\xe2\x80\x94to enjoy your\r\nkindness and protection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis striking argument gave the Doctor a sudden sense of having\r\nunderestimated his daughter; it seemed even more than worthy of a young\r\nwoman who had revealed the quality of unaggressive obstinacy.  But it\r\ndispleased him\xe2\x80\x94displeased him deeply, and he signified as much.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nidea is in very bad taste,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cDid you get it from Mr. Townsend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; it\xe2\x80\x99s my own!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep it to yourself, then,\xe2\x80\x9d her father answered, more than ever\r\ndetermined she should go to Europe.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nIF Morris Townsend was not to be included in this journey, no more was\r\nMrs. Penniman, who would have been thankful for an invitation, but who\r\n(to do her justice) bore her disappointment in a perfectly ladylike\r\nmanner.  \xe2\x80\x9cI should enjoy seeing the works of Raphael and the ruins\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nruins of the Pantheon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Mrs. Almond; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, on the other hand,\r\nI shall not be sorry to be alone and at peace for the next few months in\r\nWashington Square.  I want rest; I have been through so much in the last\r\nfour months.\xe2\x80\x9d  Mrs. Almond thought it rather cruel that her brother\r\nshould not take poor Lavinia abroad; but she easily understood that, if\r\nthe purpose of his expedition was to make Catherine forget her lover, it\r\nwas not in his interest to give his daughter this young man\xe2\x80\x99s best friend\r\nas a companion.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf Lavinia had not been so foolish, she might visit the\r\nruins of the Pantheon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to herself; and she continued to regret\r\nher sister\xe2\x80\x99s folly, even though the latter assured her that she had often\r\nheard the relics in question most satisfactorily described by Mr.\r\nPenniman.  Mrs. Penniman was perfectly aware that her brother\xe2\x80\x99s motive in\r\nundertaking a foreign tour was to lay a trap for Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s constancy;\r\nand she imparted this conviction very frankly to her niece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe thinks it will make you forget Morris,\xe2\x80\x9d she said (she always called\r\nthe young man \xe2\x80\x9cMorris\xe2\x80\x9d now); \xe2\x80\x9cout of sight, out of mind, you know.  He\r\nthinks that all the things you will see over there will drive him out of\r\nyour thoughts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine looked greatly alarmed.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf he thinks that, I ought to tell\r\nhim beforehand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman shook her head.  \xe2\x80\x9cTell him afterwards, my dear!  After he\r\nhas had all the trouble and the expense!  That\xe2\x80\x99s the way to serve him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd she added, in a softer key, that it must be delightful to think of\r\nthose who love us among the ruins of the Pantheon.\r\n\r\nHer father\xe2\x80\x99s displeasure had cost the girl, as we know, a great deal of\r\ndeep-welling sorrow\xe2\x80\x94sorrow of the purest and most generous kind, without\r\na touch of resentment or rancour; but for the first time, after he had\r\ndismissed with such contemptuous brevity her apology for being a charge\r\nupon him, there was a spark of anger in her grief.  She had felt his\r\ncontempt; it had scorched her; that speech about her bad taste made her\r\nears burn for three days.  During this period she was less considerate;\r\nshe had an idea\xe2\x80\x94a rather vague one, but it was agreeable to her sense of\r\ninjury\xe2\x80\x94that now she was absolved from penance, and might do what she\r\nchose.  She chose to write to Morris Townsend to meet her in the Square\r\nand take her to walk about the town.  If she were going to Europe out of\r\nrespect to her father, she might at least give herself this satisfaction.\r\nShe felt in every way at present more free and more resolute; there was a\r\nforce that urged her.  Now at last, completely and unreservedly, her\r\npassion possessed her.\r\n\r\nMorris met her at last, and they took a long walk.  She told him\r\nimmediately what had happened\xe2\x80\x94that her father wished to take her away.\r\nIt would be for six months, to Europe; she would do absolutely what\r\nMorris should think best.  She hoped inexpressibly that he would think it\r\nbest she should stay at home.  It was some time before he said what he\r\nthought: he asked, as they walked along, a great many questions.  There\r\nwas one that especially struck her; it seemed so incongruous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you like to see all those celebrated things over there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, Morris!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, quite deprecatingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGracious Heaven, what a dull woman!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris exclaimed to himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe thinks I will forget you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine: \xe2\x80\x9cthat all these things\r\nwill drive you out of my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear, perhaps they will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered gently, as they walked along.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor father will be disappointed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris gave a little laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I verily believe that your poor father\r\nwill be disappointed!  But you will have seen Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d he added\r\nhumorously.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a take-in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for seeing Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to care, my dear.  And it may mollify your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine, conscious of her obstinacy, expected little of this, and could\r\nnot rid herself of the idea that in going abroad and yet remaining firm,\r\nshe should play her father a trick.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think it would be a kind\r\nof deception?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t he want to deceive you?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt will serve him\r\nright!  I really think you had better go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd not be married for so long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe married when you come back.  You can buy your wedding clothes in\r\nParis.\xe2\x80\x9d  And then Morris, with great kindness of tone, explained his view\r\nof the matter.  It would be a good thing that she should go; it would put\r\nthem completely in the right.  It would show they were reasonable and\r\nwilling to wait.  Once they were so sure of each other, they could afford\r\nto wait\xe2\x80\x94what had they to fear?  If there was a particle of chance that\r\nher father would be favourably affected by her going, that ought to\r\nsettle it; for, after all, Morris was very unwilling to be the cause of\r\nher being disinherited.  It was not for himself, it was for her and for\r\nher children.  He was willing to wait for her; it would be hard, but he\r\ncould do it.  And over there, among beautiful scenes and noble monuments,\r\nperhaps the old gentleman would be softened; such things were supposed to\r\nexert a humanising influence.  He might be touched by her gentleness, her\r\npatience, her willingness to make any sacrifice but _that_ one; and if\r\nshe should appeal to him some day, in some celebrated spot\xe2\x80\x94in Italy, say,\r\nin the evening; in Venice, in a gondola, by moonlight\xe2\x80\x94if she should be a\r\nlittle clever about it and touch the right chord, perhaps he would fold\r\nher in his arms and tell her that he forgave her.  Catherine was\r\nimmensely struck with this conception of the affair, which seemed\r\neminently worthy of her lover\xe2\x80\x99s brilliant intellect; though she viewed it\r\naskance in so far as it depended upon her own powers of execution.  The\r\nidea of being \xe2\x80\x9cclever\xe2\x80\x9d in a gondola by moonlight appeared to her to\r\ninvolve elements of which her grasp was not active.  But it was settled\r\nbetween them that she should tell her father that she was ready to follow\r\nhim obediently anywhere, making the mental reservation that she loved\r\nMorris Townsend more than ever.\r\n\r\nShe informed the Doctor she was ready to embark, and he made rapid\r\narrangements for this event.  Catherine had many farewells to make, but\r\nwith only two of them are we actively concerned.  Mrs. Penniman took a\r\ndiscriminating view of her niece\xe2\x80\x99s journey; it seemed to her very proper\r\nthat Mr. Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s destined bride should wish to embellish her mind by a\r\nforeign tour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou leave him in good hands,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, pressing her lips to Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nforehead.  (She was very fond of kissing people\xe2\x80\x99s foreheads; it was an\r\ninvoluntary expression of sympathy with the intellectual part.)  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nsee him often; I shall feel like one of the vestals of old, tending the\r\nsacred flame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou behave beautifully about not going with us,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered, not\r\npresuming to examine this analogy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my pride that keeps me up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, tapping the body\r\nof her dress, which always gave forth a sort of metallic ring.\r\n\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s parting with her lover was short, and few words were\r\nexchanged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I find you just the same when I come back?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked; though the\r\nquestion was not the fruit of scepticism.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same\xe2\x80\x94only more so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, smiling.\r\n\r\nIt does not enter into our scheme to narrate in detail Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nproceedings in the eastern hemisphere.  He made the grand tour of Europe,\r\ntravelled in considerable splendour, and (as was to have been expected in\r\na man of his high cultivation) found so much in art and antiquity to\r\ninterest him, that he remained abroad, not for six months, but for\r\ntwelve.  Mrs. Penniman, in Washington Square, accommodated herself to his\r\nabsence.  She enjoyed her uncontested dominion in the empty house, and\r\nflattered herself that she made it more attractive to their friends than\r\nwhen her brother was at home.  To Morris Townsend, at least, it would\r\nhave appeared that she made it singularly attractive.  He was altogether\r\nher most frequent visitor, and Mrs. Penniman was very fond of asking him\r\nto tea.  He had his chair\xe2\x80\x94a very easy one at the fireside in the back\r\nparlour (when the great mahogany sliding-doors, with silver knobs and\r\nhinges, which divided this apartment from its more formal neighbour, were\r\nclosed), and he used to smoke cigars in the Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s study, where he\r\noften spent an hour in turning over the curious collections of its absent\r\nproprietor.  He thought Mrs. Penniman a goose, as we know; but he was no\r\ngoose himself, and, as a young man of luxurious tastes and scanty\r\nresources, he found the house a perfect castle of indolence.  It became\r\nfor him a club with a single member.  Mrs. Penniman saw much less of her\r\nsister than while the Doctor was at home; for Mrs. Almond had felt moved\r\nto tell her that she disapproved of her relations with Mr. Townsend.  She\r\nhad no business to be so friendly to a young man of whom their brother\r\nthought so meanly, and Mrs. Almond was surprised at her levity in\r\nfoisting a most deplorable engagement upon Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeplorable?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lavinia.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe will make her a lovely husband!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in lovely husbands,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond; \xe2\x80\x9cI only believe\r\nin good ones.  If he marries her, and she comes into Austin\xe2\x80\x99s money, they\r\nmay get on.  He will be an idle, amiable, selfish, and doubtless\r\ntolerably good-natured fellow.  But if she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get the money and he\r\nfinds himself tied to her, Heaven have mercy on her!  He will have none.\r\nHe will hate her for his disappointment, and take his revenge; he will be\r\npitiless and cruel.  Woe betide poor Catherine!  I recommend you to talk\r\na little with his sister; it\xe2\x80\x99s a pity Catherine can\xe2\x80\x99t marry _her_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman had no appetite whatever for conversation with Mrs.\r\nMontgomery, whose acquaintance she made no trouble to cultivate; and the\r\neffect of this alarming forecast of her niece\xe2\x80\x99s destiny was to make her\r\nthink it indeed a thousand pities that Mr. Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s generous nature\r\nshould be embittered.  Bright enjoyment was his natural element, and how\r\ncould he be comfortable if there should prove to be nothing to enjoy?  It\r\nbecame a fixed idea with Mrs. Penniman that he should yet enjoy her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s fortune, on which she had acuteness enough to perceive that her\r\nown claim was small.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t leave it to Catherine, it certainly won\xe2\x80\x99t be to leave it\r\nto me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE Doctor, during the first six months he was abroad, never spoke to his\r\ndaughter of their little difference; partly on system, and partly because\r\nhe had a great many other things to think about.  It was idle to attempt\r\nto ascertain the state of her affections without direct inquiry, because,\r\nif she had not had an expressive manner among the familiar influences of\r\nhome, she failed to gather animation from the mountains of Switzerland or\r\nthe monuments of Italy.  She was always her father\xe2\x80\x99s docile and\r\nreasonable associate\xe2\x80\x94going through their sight-seeing in deferential\r\nsilence, never complaining of fatigue, always ready to start at the hour\r\nhe had appointed over-night, making no foolish criticisms and indulging\r\nin no refinements of appreciation.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe is about as intelligent as the\r\nbundle of shawls,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor said; her main superiority being that while\r\nthe bundle of shawls sometimes got lost, or tumbled out of the carriage,\r\nCatherine was always at her post, and had a firm and ample seat.  But her\r\nfather had expected this, and he was not constrained to set down her\r\nintellectual limitations as a tourist to sentimental depression; she had\r\ncompletely divested herself of the characteristics of a victim, and\r\nduring the whole time that they were abroad she never uttered an audible\r\nsigh.  He supposed she was in correspondence with Morris Townsend; but he\r\nheld his peace about it, for he never saw the young man\xe2\x80\x99s letters, and\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s own missives were always given to the courier to post.  She\r\nheard from her lover with considerable regularity, but his letters came\r\nenclosed in Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s; so that whenever the Doctor handed her a\r\npacket addressed in his sister\xe2\x80\x99s hand, he was an involuntary instrument\r\nof the passion he condemned.  Catherine made this reflexion, and six\r\nmonths earlier she would have felt bound to give him warning; but now she\r\ndeemed herself absolved.  There was a sore spot in her heart that his own\r\nwords had made when once she spoke to him as she thought honour prompted;\r\nshe would try and please him as far as she could, but she would never\r\nspeak that way again.  She read her lover\xe2\x80\x99s letters in secret.\r\n\r\nOne day at the end of the summer, the two travellers found themselves in\r\na lonely valley of the Alps.  They were crossing one of the passes, and\r\non the long ascent they had got out of the carriage and had wandered much\r\nin advance.  After a while the Doctor descried a footpath which, leading\r\nthrough a transverse valley, would bring them out, as he justly supposed,\r\nat a much higher point of the ascent.  They followed this devious way,\r\nand finally lost the path; the valley proved very wild and rough, and\r\ntheir walk became rather a scramble.  They were good walkers, however,\r\nand they took their adventure easily; from time to time they stopped,\r\nthat Catherine might rest; and then she sat upon a stone and looked about\r\nher at the hard-featured rocks and the glowing sky.  It was late in the\r\nafternoon, in the last of August; night was coming on, and, as they had\r\nreached a great elevation, the air was cold and sharp.  In the west there\r\nwas a great suffusion of cold, red light, which made the sides of the\r\nlittle valley look only the more rugged and dusky.  During one of their\r\npauses, her father left her and wandered away to some high place, at a\r\ndistance, to get a view.  He was out of sight; she sat there alone, in\r\nthe stillness, which was just touched by the vague murmur, somewhere, of\r\na mountain brook.  She thought of Morris Townsend, and the place was so\r\ndesolate and lonely that he seemed very far away.  Her father remained\r\nabsent a long time; she began to wonder what had become of him.  But at\r\nlast he reappeared, coming towards her in the clear twilight, and she got\r\nup, to go on.  He made no motion to proceed, however, but came close to\r\nher, as if he had something to say.  He stopped in front of her and stood\r\nlooking at her, with eyes that had kept the light of the flushing\r\nsnow-summits on which they had just been fixed.  Then, abruptly, in a low\r\ntone, he asked her an unexpected question:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you given him up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe question was unexpected, but Catherine was only superficially\r\nunprepared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, father!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her again for some moments, without speaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he write to you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94about twice a month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor looked up and down the valley, swinging his stick; then he\r\nsaid to her, in the same low tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe wondered what he meant\xe2\x80\x94whether he wished to frighten her.  If he did,\r\nthe place was well chosen; this hard, melancholy dell, abandoned by the\r\nsummer light, made her feel her loneliness.  She looked around her, and\r\nher heart grew cold; for a moment her fear was great.  But she could\r\nthink of nothing to say, save to murmur gently, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou try my patience,\xe2\x80\x9d her father went on, \xe2\x80\x9cand you ought to know what I\r\nam, I am not a very good man.  Though I am very smooth externally, at\r\nbottom I am very passionate; and I assure you I can be very hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could not think why he told her these things.  Had he brought her\r\nthere on purpose, and was it part of a plan?  What was the plan?\r\nCatherine asked herself.  Was it to startle her suddenly into a\r\nretractation\xe2\x80\x94to take an advantage of her by dread?  Dread of what?  The\r\nplace was ugly and lonely, but the place could do her no harm.  There was\r\na kind of still intensity about her father, which made him dangerous, but\r\nCatherine hardly went so far as to say to herself that it might be part\r\nof his plan to fasten his hand\xe2\x80\x94the neat, fine, supple hand of a\r\ndistinguished physician\xe2\x80\x94in her throat.  Nevertheless, she receded a step.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you can be anything you please,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  And it was her\r\nsimple belief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very angry,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, more sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy has it taken you so suddenly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has not taken me suddenly.  I have been raging inwardly for the last\r\nsix months.  But just now this seemed a good place to flare out.  It\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\nquiet, and we are alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s very quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine vaguely, looking about her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you come back to the carriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a moment.  Do you mean that in all this time you have not yielded an\r\ninch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would if I could, father; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor looked round him too.  \xe2\x80\x9cShould you like to be left in such a\r\nplace as this, to starve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be your fate\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s how he will leave you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe would not touch her, but he had touched Morris.  The warmth came back\r\nto her heart.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat is not true, father,\xe2\x80\x9d she broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cand you ought\r\nnot to say it!  It is not right, and it\xe2\x80\x99s not true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook his head slowly.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s not right, because you won\xe2\x80\x99t believe\r\nit.  But it _is_ true.  Come back to the carriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned away, and she followed him; he went faster, and was presently\r\nmuch in advance.  But from time to time he stopped, without turning\r\nround, to let her keep up with him, and she made her way forward with\r\ndifficulty, her heart beating with the excitement of having for the first\r\ntime spoken to him in violence.  By this time it had grown almost dark,\r\nand she ended by losing sight of him.  But she kept her course, and after\r\na little, the valley making a sudden turn, she gained the road, where the\r\ncarriage stood waiting.  In it sat her father, rigid and silent; in\r\nsilence, too, she took her place beside him.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to her, later, in looking back upon all this, that for days\r\nafterwards not a word had been exchanged between them.  The scene had\r\nbeen a strange one, but it had not permanently affected her feeling\r\ntowards her father, for it was natural, after all, that he should\r\noccasionally make a scene of some kind, and he had let her alone for six\r\nmonths.  The strangest part of it was that he had said he was not a good\r\nman; Catherine wondered a great deal what he had meant by that.  The\r\nstatement failed to appeal to her credence, and it was not grateful to\r\nany resentment that she entertained.  Even in the utmost bitterness that\r\nshe might feel, it would give her no satisfaction to think him less\r\ncomplete.  Such a saying as that was a part of his great subtlety\xe2\x80\x94men so\r\nclever as he might say anything and mean anything.  And as to his being\r\nhard, that surely, in a man, was a virtue.\r\n\r\nHe let her alone for six months more\xe2\x80\x94six months during which she\r\naccommodated herself without a protest to the extension of their tour.\r\nBut he spoke again at the end of this time; it was at the very last, the\r\nnight before they embarked for New York, in the hotel at Liverpool.  They\r\nhad been dining together in a great dim, musty sitting-room; and then the\r\ncloth had been removed, and the Doctor walked slowly up and down.\r\nCatherine at last took her candle to go to bed, but her father motioned\r\nher to stay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean to do when you get home?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, while she stood\r\nthere with her candle in her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean about Mr. Townsend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout Mr. Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall probably marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor took several turns again while she waited.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear from\r\nhim as much as ever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; twice a month,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine promptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd does he always talk about marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes!  That is, he talks about other things too, but he always says\r\nsomething about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear he varies his subjects; his letters might otherwise be\r\nmonotonous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe writes beautifully,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, who was very glad of a chance to\r\nsay it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey always write beautifully.  However, in a given case that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndiminish the merit.  So, as soon as you arrive, you are going off with\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis seemed a rather gross way of putting it, and something that there\r\nwas of dignity in Catherine resented it.  \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot tell you till we\r\narrive,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s reasonable enough,\xe2\x80\x9d her father answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all I ask of\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94that you _do_ tell me, that you give me definite notice.  When a poor\r\nman is to lose his only child, he likes to have an inkling of it\r\nbeforehand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father, you will not lose me!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine said, spilling her\r\ncandle-wax.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree days before will do,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cif you are in a position to be\r\npositive then.  He ought to be very thankful to me, do you know.  I have\r\ndone a mighty good thing for him in taking you abroad; your value is\r\ntwice as great, with all the knowledge and taste that you have acquired.\r\nA year ago, you were perhaps a little limited\xe2\x80\x94a little rustic; but now\r\nyou have seen everything, and appreciated everything, and you will be a\r\nmost entertaining companion.  We have fattened the sheep for him before\r\nhe kills it!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine turned away, and stood staring at the blank door.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to bed,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father; \xe2\x80\x9cand, as we don\xe2\x80\x99t go aboard till noon, you\r\nmay sleep late.  We shall probably have a most uncomfortable voyage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXV\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE voyage was indeed uncomfortable, and Catherine, on arriving in New\r\nYork, had not the compensation of \xe2\x80\x9cgoing off,\xe2\x80\x9d in her father\xe2\x80\x99s phrase,\r\nwith Morris Townsend.  She saw him, however, the day after she landed;\r\nand, in the meantime, he formed a natural subject of conversation between\r\nour heroine and her Aunt Lavinia, with whom, the night she disembarked,\r\nthe girl was closeted for a long time before either lady retired to rest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen a great deal of him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is not very\r\neasy to know.  I suppose you think you know him; but you don\xe2\x80\x99t, my dear.\r\nYou will some day; but it will only be after you have lived with him.  I\r\nmay almost say _I_ have lived with him,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman proceeded, while\r\nCatherine stared.  \xe2\x80\x9cI think I know him now; I have had such remarkable\r\nopportunities.  You will have the same\xe2\x80\x94or rather, you will have better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand Aunt Lavinia smiled.  \xe2\x80\x9cThen you will see what I mean.  It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nwonderful character, full of passion and energy, and just as true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine listened with a mixture of interest and apprehension.  Aunt\r\nLavinia was intensely sympathetic, and Catherine, for the past year,\r\nwhile she wandered through foreign galleries and churches, and rolled\r\nover the smoothness of posting roads, nursing the thoughts that never\r\npassed her lips, had often longed for the company of some intelligent\r\nperson of her own sex.  To tell her story to some kind woman\xe2\x80\x94at moments\r\nit seemed to her that this would give her comfort, and she had more than\r\nonce been on the point of taking the landlady, or the nice young person\r\nfrom the dressmaker\xe2\x80\x99s, into her confidence.  If a woman had been near her\r\nshe would on certain occasions have treated such a companion to a fit of\r\nweeping; and she had an apprehension that, on her return, this would form\r\nher response to Aunt Lavinia\xe2\x80\x99s first embrace.  In fact, however, the two\r\nladies had met, in Washington Square, without tears, and when they found\r\nthemselves alone together a certain dryness fell upon the girl\xe2\x80\x99s emotion.\r\nIt came over her with a greater force that Mrs. Penniman had enjoyed a\r\nwhole year of her lover\xe2\x80\x99s society, and it was not a pleasure to her to\r\nhear her aunt explain and interpret the young man, speaking of him as if\r\nher own knowledge of him were supreme.  It was not that Catherine was\r\njealous; but her sense of Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s innocent falsity, which had\r\nlain dormant, began to haunt her again, and she was glad that she was\r\nsafely at home.  With this, however, it was a blessing to be able to talk\r\nof Morris, to sound his name, to be with a person who was not unjust to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been very kind to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe has written me\r\nthat, often.  I shall never forget that, Aunt Lavinia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have done what I could; it has been very little.  To let him come and\r\ntalk to me, and give him his cup of tea\xe2\x80\x94that was all.  Your Aunt Almond\r\nthought it was too much, and used to scold me terribly; but she promised\r\nme, at least, not to betray me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo betray you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to tell your father.  He used to sit in your father\xe2\x80\x99s study!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMrs. Penniman, with a little laugh.\r\n\r\nCatherine was silent a moment.  This idea was disagreeable to her, and\r\nshe was reminded again, with pain, of her aunt\xe2\x80\x99s secretive habits.\r\nMorris, the reader may be informed, had had the tact not to tell her that\r\nhe sat in her father\xe2\x80\x99s study.  He had known her but for a few months, and\r\nher aunt had known her for fifteen years; and yet he would not have made\r\nthe mistake of thinking that Catherine would see the joke of the thing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry you made him go into father\xe2\x80\x99s room,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after a while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t make him go; he went himself.  He liked to look at the books,\r\nand all those things in the glass cases.  He knows all about them; he\r\nknows all about everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine was silent again; then, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he had found some employment,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has found some employment!  It\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful news, and he told me to\r\ntell you as soon as you arrived.  He has gone into partnership with a\r\ncommission merchant.  It was all settled, quite suddenly, a week ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis seemed to Catherine indeed beautiful news; it had a fine prosperous\r\nair.  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad!\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and now, for a moment, she was\r\ndisposed to throw herself on Aunt Lavinia\xe2\x80\x99s neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s much better than being under some one; and he has never been used\r\nto that,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman went on.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is just as good as his partner\xe2\x80\x94they\r\nare perfectly equal!  You see how right he was to wait.  I should like to\r\nknow what your father can say now!  They have got an office in Duane\r\nStreet, and little printed cards; he brought me one to show me.  I have\r\ngot it in my room, and you shall see it to-morrow.  That\xe2\x80\x99s what he said\r\nto me the last time he was here\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98You see how right I was to wait!\xe2\x80\x99  He\r\nhas got other people under him, instead of being a subordinate.  He could\r\nnever be a subordinate; I have often told him I could never think of him\r\nin that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine assented to this proposition, and was very happy to know that\r\nMorris was his own master; but she was deprived of the satisfaction of\r\nthinking that she might communicate this news in triumph to her father.\r\nHer father would care equally little whether Morris were established in\r\nbusiness or transported for life.  Her trunks had been brought into her\r\nroom, and further reference to her lover was for a short time suspended,\r\nwhile she opened them and displayed to her aunt some of the spoils of\r\nforeign travel.  These were rich and abundant; and Catherine had brought\r\nhome a present to every one\xe2\x80\x94to every one save Morris, to whom she had\r\nbrought simply her undiverted heart.  To Mrs. Penniman she had been\r\nlavishly generous, and Aunt Lavinia spent half an hour in unfolding and\r\nfolding again, with little ejaculations of gratitude and taste.  She\r\nmarched about for some time in a splendid cashmere shawl, which Catherine\r\nhad begged her to accept, settling it on her shoulders, and twisting down\r\nher head to see how low the point descended behind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall regard it only as a loan,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will leave it to you\r\nagain when I die; or rather,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, kissing her niece again, \xe2\x80\x9cI will\r\nleave it to your first-born little girl!\xe2\x80\x9d  And draped in her shawl, she\r\nstood there smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better wait till she comes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the way you say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman rejoined, in a moment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine, are you changed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not swerved a line?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am exactly the same,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine repeated, wishing her aunt were a\r\nlittle less sympathetic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am glad!\xe2\x80\x9d and Mrs. Penniman surveyed her cashmere in the glass.\r\nThen, \xe2\x80\x9cHow is your father?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked in a moment, with her eyes on her\r\nniece.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour letters were so meagre\xe2\x80\x94I could never tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather is very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you know what I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with a dignity to which\r\nthe cashmere gave a richer effect.  \xe2\x80\x9cIs he still implacable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite unchanged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is, if possible, more firm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman took off her great shawl, and slowly folded it up.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nis very bad.  You had no success with your little project?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat little project?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMorris told me all about it.  The idea of turning the tables on him, in\r\nEurope; of watching him, when he was agreeably impressed by some\r\ncelebrated sight\xe2\x80\x94he pretends to be so artistic, you know\xe2\x80\x94and then just\r\npleading with him and bringing him round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never tried it.  It was Morris\xe2\x80\x99s idea; but if he had been with us, in\r\nEurope, he would have seen that father was never impressed in that way.\r\nHe _is_ artistic\xe2\x80\x94tremendously artistic; but the more celebrated places we\r\nvisited, and the more he admired them, the less use it would have been to\r\nplead with him.  They seemed only to make him more determined\xe2\x80\x94more\r\nterrible,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never bring him round, and I\r\nexpect nothing now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must say,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI never supposed you were\r\ngoing to give it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have given it up.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t care now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have grown very brave,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with a short laugh.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t advise you to sacrifice your property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am braver than I was.  You asked me if I had changed; I have\r\nchanged in that way.  Oh,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl went on, \xe2\x80\x9cI have changed very much.\r\nAnd it isn\xe2\x80\x99t my property.  If _he_ doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care for it, why should I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman hesitated.  \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he does care for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe cares for it for my sake, because he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want to injure me.  But\r\nhe will know\xe2\x80\x94he knows already\xe2\x80\x94how little he need be afraid about that.\r\nBesides,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, \xe2\x80\x9cI have got plenty of money of my own.  We\r\nshall be very well off; and now hasn\xe2\x80\x99t he got his business?  I am\r\ndelighted about that business.\xe2\x80\x9d  She went on talking, showing a good deal\r\nof excitement as she proceeded.  Her aunt had never seen her with just\r\nthis manner, and Mrs. Penniman, observing her, set it down to foreign\r\ntravel, which had made her more positive, more mature.  She thought also\r\nthat Catherine had improved in appearance; she looked rather handsome.\r\nMrs. Penniman wondered whether Morris Townsend would be struck with that.\r\nWhile she was engaged in this speculation, Catherine broke out, with a\r\ncertain sharpness, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so contradictory, Aunt Penniman?  You\r\nseem to think one thing at one time, and another at another.  A year ago,\r\nbefore I went away, you wished me not to mind about displeasing father;\r\nand now you seem to recommend me to take another line.  You change about\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis attack was unexpected, for Mrs. Penniman was not used, in any\r\ndiscussion, to seeing the war carried into her own country\xe2\x80\x94possibly\r\nbecause the enemy generally had doubts of finding subsistence there.  To\r\nher own consciousness, the flowery fields of her reason had rarely been\r\nravaged by a hostile force.  It was perhaps on this account that in\r\ndefending them she was majestic rather than agile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you accuse me of, save of being too deeply interested\r\nin your happiness.  It is the first time I have been told I am\r\ncapricious.  That fault is not what I am usually reproached with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were angry last year that I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry immediately, and now you\r\ntalk about my winning my father over. Nothing is changed\xe2\x80\x94nothing\r\nbut my feeling about father.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind nearly so much now.  I have\r\nbeen as good as I could, but he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care.  Now I don\xe2\x80\x99t care either.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I have grown bad; perhaps I have.  But I don\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nfor that. You may do as you please; but you must never speak to me again\r\nabout pleading with father.  I shall never plead with him for anything;\r\nthat is all over.  He has put me off.  I am come home to be married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a more authoritative speech than she had ever heard on her\r\nniece\xe2\x80\x99s lips, and Mrs. Penniman was proportionately startled.  She was\r\nindeed a little awestruck, and the force of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s emotion and\r\nresolution left her nothing to reply.  She was easily frightened, and she\r\nalways carried off her discomfiture by a concession; a concession which\r\nwas often accompanied, as in the present case, by a little nervous laugh.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nIF she had disturbed her niece\xe2\x80\x99s temper\xe2\x80\x94she began from this moment\r\nforward to talk a good deal about Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s temper, an article which up\r\nto that time had never been mentioned in connexion with our\r\nheroine\xe2\x80\x94Catherine had opportunity, on the morrow, to recover her\r\nserenity.  Mrs. Penniman had given her a message from Morris Townsend, to\r\nthe effect that he would come and welcome her home on the day after her\r\narrival.  He came in the afternoon; but, as may be imagined, he was not\r\non this occasion made free of Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s study.  He had been coming and\r\ngoing, for the past year, so comfortably and irresponsibly, that he had a\r\ncertain sense of being wronged by finding himself reminded that he must\r\nnow limit his horizon to the front parlour, which was Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nparticular province.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad you have come back,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cit makes me very happy to\r\nsee you again.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he looked at her, smiling, from head to foot; though\r\nit did not appear, afterwards, that he agreed with Mrs. Penniman (who,\r\nwomanlike, went more into details) in thinking her embellished.\r\n\r\nTo Catherine he appeared resplendent; it was some time before she could\r\nbelieve again that this beautiful young man was her own exclusive\r\nproperty.  They had a great deal of characteristic lovers\xe2\x80\x99 talk\xe2\x80\x94a soft\r\nexchange of inquiries and assurances.  In these matters Morris had an\r\nexcellent grace, which flung a picturesque interest even over the account\r\nof his d\xc3\xa9but in the commission business\xe2\x80\x94a subject as to which his\r\ncompanion earnestly questioned him.  From time to time he got up from the\r\nsofa where they sat together, and walked about the room; after which he\r\ncame back, smiling and passing his hand through his hair.  He was\r\nunquiet, as was natural in a young man who has just been reunited to a\r\nlong-absent mistress, and Catherine made the reflexion that she had never\r\nseen him so excited.  It gave her pleasure, somehow, to note this fact.\r\nHe asked her questions about her travels, to some of which she was unable\r\nto reply, for she had forgotten the names of places, and the order of her\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s journey.  But for the moment she was so happy, so lifted up by\r\nthe belief that her troubles at last were over, that she forgot to be\r\nashamed of her meagre answers.  It seemed to her now that she could marry\r\nhim without the remnant of a scruple or a single tremor save those that\r\nbelonged to joy.  Without waiting for him to ask, she told him that her\r\nfather had come back in exactly the same state of mind\xe2\x80\x94that he had not\r\nyielded an inch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must not expect it now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand we must do without it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris sat looking and smiling.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor dear girl!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t pity me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it now\xe2\x80\x94I am used to\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris continued to smile, and then he got up and walked about again.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better let me try him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry to bring him over?  You would only make him worse,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine\r\nanswered resolutely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say that because I managed it so badly before.  But I should manage\r\nit differently now.  I am much wiser; I have had a year to think of it.\r\nI have more tact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that what you have been thinking of for a year?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch of the time.  You see, the idea sticks in my crop.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to\r\nbe beaten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you beaten if we marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I am not beaten on the main issue; but I am, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see,\r\non all the rest of it\xe2\x80\x94on the question of my reputation, of my relations\r\nwith your father, of my relations with my own children, if we should have\r\nany.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have enough for our children\xe2\x80\x94we shall have enough for\r\neverything. But with me it is different.  I have staked my\r\npride on proving to your father that he is wrong; and now that I am at\r\nthe head of a flourishing business, I can deal with him as an equal.  I\r\nhave a capital plan\xe2\x80\x94do let me go at him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood before her with his bright face, his jaunty air, his hands in\r\nhis pockets; and she got up, with her eyes resting on his own.  \xe2\x80\x9cPlease\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t, Morris; please don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and there was a certain mild, sad\r\nfirmness in her tone which he heard for the first time.  \xe2\x80\x9cWe must ask no\r\nfavours of him\xe2\x80\x94we must ask nothing more.  He won\xe2\x80\x99t relent, and nothing\r\ngood will come of it.  I know it now\xe2\x80\x94I have a very good reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pray; what is your reason?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated to bring it out, but at last it came.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe is not very fond\r\nof me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bother!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris angrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say such a thing without being sure.  I saw it, I felt it, in\r\nEngland, just before he came away.  He talked to me one night\xe2\x80\x94the last\r\nnight; and then it came over me.  You can tell when a person feels that\r\nway.  I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t accuse him if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t made me feel that way.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\naccuse him; I just tell you that that\xe2\x80\x99s how it is. It\xe2\x80\x99s because he is so fond of my mother, whom we lost so long ago.\r\nShe was beautiful, and very, very brilliant; he is always thinking of\r\nher. I\r\nfeel differently; I feel separated from my father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are a queer family!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t say anything unkind,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl entreated.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmust be very kind to me now, because, Morris\xe2\x80\x94because,\xe2\x80\x9d and she hesitated\r\na moment\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbecause I have done a great deal for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know that, my dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had spoken up to this moment without vehemence or outward sign of\r\nemotion, gently, reasoningly, only trying to explain.  But her emotion\r\nhad been ineffectually smothered, and it betrayed itself at last in the\r\ntrembling of her voice.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a great thing to be separated like that\r\nfrom your father, when you have worshipped him before.  It has made me\r\nvery unhappy; or it would have made me so if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t love you. It\r\nwould not be natural now.  We must be very happy together, and we must\r\nnot seem to depend upon his forgiveness.  And Morris, Morris, you must\r\nnever despise me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was an easy promise to make, and Morris made it with fine effect.\r\nBut for the moment he undertook nothing more onerous.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE Doctor, of course, on his return, had a good deal of talk with his\r\nsisters.  He was at no great pains to narrate his travels or to\r\ncommunicate his impressions of distant lands to Mrs. Penniman, upon whom\r\nhe contented himself with bestowing a memento of his enviable experience,\r\nin the shape of a velvet gown.  But he conversed with her at some length\r\nabout matters nearer home, and lost no time in assuring her that he was\r\nstill an inflexible father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt you have seen a great deal of Mr. Townsend, and done\r\nyour best to console him for Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s absence,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t ask\r\nyou, and you needn\xe2\x80\x99t deny it.  I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t put the question to you for the\r\nworld, and expose you to the inconvenience of having to\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94excogitate an\r\nanswer.  No one has betrayed you, and there has been no spy upon your\r\nproceedings.  Elizabeth has told no tales, and has never mentioned you\r\nexcept to praise your good looks and good spirits.  The thing is simply\r\nan inference of my own\xe2\x80\x94an induction, as the philosophers say.  It seems\r\nto me likely that you would have offered an asylum to an interesting\r\nsufferer.  Mr. Townsend has been a good deal in the house; there is\r\nsomething in the house that tells me so.  We doctors, you know, end by\r\nacquiring fine perceptions, and it is impressed upon my sensorium that he\r\nhas sat in these chairs, in a very easy attitude, and warmed himself at\r\nthat fire.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t grudge him the comfort of it; it is the only one he\r\nwill ever enjoy at my expense.  It seems likely, indeed, that I shall be\r\nable to economise at his own.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you may have said to\r\nhim, or what you may say hereafter; but I should like you to know that if\r\nyou have encouraged him to believe that he will gain anything by hanging\r\non, or that I have budged a hair\xe2\x80\x99s-breadth from the position I took up a\r\nyear ago, you have played him a trick for which he may exact reparation.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m not sure that he may not bring a suit against you.  Of course you\r\nhave done it conscientiously; you have made yourself believe that I can\r\nbe tired out.  This is the most baseless hallucination that ever visited\r\nthe brain of a genial optimist.  I am not in the least tired; I am as\r\nfresh as when I started; I am good for fifty years yet.  Catherine\r\nappears not to have budged an inch either; she is equally fresh; so we\r\nare about where we were before.  This, however, you know as well as I.\r\nWhat I wish is simply to give you notice of my own state of mind!  Take\r\nit to heart, dear Lavinia.  Beware of the just resentment of a deluded\r\nfortune-hunter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I expected it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I had a sort of\r\nfoolish hope that you would come home without that odious ironical tone\r\nwith which you treat the most sacred subjects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t undervalue irony, it is often of great use.  It is not, however,\r\nalways necessary, and I will show you how gracefully I can lay it aside.\r\nI should like to know whether you think Morris Townsend will hang on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will answer you with your own weapons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou had\r\nbetter wait and see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call such a speech as that one of my own weapons?  I never said\r\nanything so rough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will hang on long enough to make you very uncomfortable, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Lavinia,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you call that irony?  I call\r\nit pugilism.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, however, in spite of her pugilism, was a good deal\r\nfrightened, and she took counsel of her fears.  Her brother meanwhile\r\ntook counsel, with many reservations, of Mrs. Almond, to whom he was no\r\nless generous than to Lavinia, and a good deal more communicative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose she has had him there all the while,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI must look\r\ninto the state of my wine!  You needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind telling me now; I have\r\nalready said all I mean to say to her on the subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he was in the house a good deal,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nyou must admit that your leaving Lavinia quite alone was a great change\r\nfor her, and that it was natural she should want some society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do admit that, and that is why I shall make no row about the wine; I\r\nshall set it down as compensation to Lavinia.  She is capable of telling\r\nme that she drank it all herself.  Think of the inconceivable bad taste,\r\nin the circumstances, of that fellow making free with the house\xe2\x80\x94or coming\r\nthere at all!  If that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t describe him, he is indescribable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis plan is to get what he can.  Lavinia will have supported him for a\r\nyear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so much gained.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will have to support him for the rest of his life, then!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the\r\nDoctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut without wine, as they say at the _tables d\xe2\x80\x99h\xc3\xb4te_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine tells me he has set up a business, and is making a great deal\r\nof money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor stared.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe has not told me that\xe2\x80\x94and Lavinia didn\xe2\x80\x99t deign.\r\nAh!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cCatherine has given me up.  Not that it matters, for all\r\nthat the business amounts to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has not given up Mr. Townsend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.  \xe2\x80\x9cI saw that in\r\nthe first half minute.  She has come home exactly the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly the same; not a grain more intelligent.  She didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice a\r\nstick or a stone all the while we were away\xe2\x80\x94not a picture nor a view, not\r\na statue nor a cathedral.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could she notice?  She had other things to think of; they are never\r\nfor an instant out of her mind.  She touches me very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would touch me if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t irritate me.  That\xe2\x80\x99s the effect she has\r\nupon me now.  I have tried everything upon her; I really have been quite\r\nmerciless.  But it is of no use whatever; she is absolutely _glued_.  I\r\nhave passed, in consequence, into the exasperated stage.  At first I had\r\na good deal of a certain genial curiosity about it; I wanted to see if\r\nshe really would stick.  But, good Lord, one\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity is satisfied!  I\r\nsee she is capable of it, and now she can let go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will never let go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care, or you will exasperate me too.  If she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t let go, she\r\nwill be shaken off\xe2\x80\x94sent tumbling into the dust!  That\xe2\x80\x99s a nice position\r\nfor my daughter.
