She can\xe2\x80\x99t see that if you are going to be pushed you\r\nhad better jump.  And then she will complain of her bruises.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will never complain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I shall object to even more.  But the deuce will be that I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nprevent anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she is to have a fall,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond, with a gentle laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\r\nmust spread as many carpets as we can.\xe2\x80\x9d  And she carried out this idea by\r\nshowing a great deal of motherly kindness to the girl.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman immediately wrote to Morris Townsend.  The intimacy between\r\nthese two was by this time consummate, but I must content myself with\r\nnoting but a few of its features.  Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s own share in it was a\r\nsingular sentiment, which might have been misinterpreted, but which in\r\nitself was not discreditable to the poor lady.  It was a romantic\r\ninterest in this attractive and unfortunate young man, and yet it was not\r\nsuch an interest as Catherine might have been jealous of.  Mrs. Penniman\r\nhad not a particle of jealousy of her niece.  For herself, she felt as if\r\nshe were Morris\xe2\x80\x99s mother or sister\xe2\x80\x94a mother or sister of an emotional\r\ntemperament\xe2\x80\x94and she had an absorbing desire to make him comfortable and\r\nhappy.  She had striven to do so during the year that her brother left\r\nher an open field, and her efforts had been attended with the success\r\nthat has been pointed out.  She had never had a child of her own, and\r\nCatherine, whom she had done her best to invest with the importance that\r\nwould naturally belong to a youthful Penniman, had only partly rewarded\r\nher zeal.  Catherine, as an object of affection and solicitude, had never\r\nhad that picturesque charm which (as it seemed to her) would have been a\r\nnatural attribute of her own progeny.  Even the maternal passion in Mrs.\r\nPenniman would have been romantic and factitious, and Catherine was not\r\nconstituted to inspire a romantic passion.  Mrs. Penniman was as fond of\r\nher as ever, but she had grown to feel that with Catherine she lacked\r\nopportunity.  Sentimentally speaking, therefore, she had (though she had\r\nnot disinherited her niece) adopted Morris Townsend, who gave her\r\nopportunity in abundance.  She would have been very happy to have a\r\nhandsome and tyrannical son, and would have taken an extreme interest in\r\nhis love affairs.  This was the light in which she had come to regard\r\nMorris, who had conciliated her at first, and made his impression by his\r\ndelicate and calculated deference\xe2\x80\x94a sort of exhibition to which Mrs.\r\nPenniman was particularly sensitive.  He had largely abated his deference\r\nafterwards, for he economised his resources, but the impression was made,\r\nand the young man\xe2\x80\x99s very brutality came to have a sort of filial value.\r\nIf Mrs. Penniman had had a son, she would probably have been afraid of\r\nhim, and at this stage of our narrative she was certainly afraid of\r\nMorris Townsend.  This was one of the results of his domestication in\r\nWashington Square.  He took his ease with her\xe2\x80\x94as, for that matter, he\r\nwould certainly have done with his own mother.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE letter was a word of warning; it informed him that the Doctor had\r\ncome home more impracticable than ever.  She might have reflected that\r\nCatherine would supply him with all the information he needed on this\r\npoint; but we know that Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s reflexions were rarely just; and,\r\nmoreover, she felt that it was not for her to depend on what Catherine\r\nmight do.  She was to do her duty, quite irrespective of Catherine.  I\r\nhave said that her young friend took his ease with her, and it is an\r\nillustration of the fact that he made no answer to her letter.  He took\r\nnote of it, amply; but he lighted his cigar with it, and he waited, in\r\ntranquil confidence that he should receive another.  \xe2\x80\x9cHis state of mind\r\nreally freezes my blood,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman had written, alluding to her\r\nbrother; and it would have seemed that upon this statement she could\r\nhardly improve.  Nevertheless, she wrote again, expressing herself with\r\nthe aid of a different figure.  \xe2\x80\x9cHis hatred of you burns with a lurid\r\nflame\xe2\x80\x94the flame that never dies,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t light up\r\nthe darkness of your future.  If my affection could do so, all the years\r\nof your life would be an eternal sunshine.  I can extract nothing from\r\nC.; she is so terribly secretive, like her father.  She seems to expect\r\nto be married very soon, and has evidently made preparations in\r\nEurope\xe2\x80\x94quantities of clothing, ten pairs of shoes, etc.  My dear friend,\r\nyou cannot set up in married life simply with a few pairs of shoes, can\r\nyou?  Tell me what you think of this.  I am intensely anxious to see you;\r\nI have so much to say.  I miss you dreadfully; the house seems so empty\r\nwithout you.  What is the news down town?  Is the business extending?\r\nThat dear little business\xe2\x80\x94I think it\xe2\x80\x99s so brave of you!  Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I come\r\nto your office?\xe2\x80\x94just for three minutes?  I might pass for a customer\xe2\x80\x94is\r\nthat what you call them?  I might come in to buy something\xe2\x80\x94some shares or\r\nsome railroad things.  _Tell me what you think of this plan_.  I would\r\ncarry a little reticule, like a woman of the people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of the suggestion about the reticule, Morris appeared to think\r\npoorly of the plan, for he gave Mrs. Penniman no encouragement whatever\r\nto visit his office, which he had already represented to her as a place\r\npeculiarly and unnaturally difficult to find.  But as she persisted in\r\ndesiring an interview\xe2\x80\x94up to the last, after months of intimate colloquy,\r\nshe called these meetings \xe2\x80\x9cinterviews\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he agreed that they should take a\r\nwalk together, and was even kind enough to leave his office for this\r\npurpose, during the hours at which business might have been supposed to\r\nbe liveliest.  It was no surprise to him, when they met at a street\r\ncorner, in a region of empty lots and undeveloped pavements (Mrs.\r\nPenniman being attired as much as possible like a \xe2\x80\x9cwoman of the people\xe2\x80\x9d),\r\nto find that, in spite of her urgency, what she chiefly had to convey to\r\nhim was the assurance of her sympathy.  Of such assurances, however, he\r\nhad already a voluminous collection, and it would not have been worth his\r\nwhile to forsake a fruitful avocation merely to hear Mrs. Penniman say,\r\nfor the thousandth time, that she had made his cause her own.  Morris had\r\nsomething of his own to say.  It was not an easy thing to bring out, and\r\nwhile he turned it over the difficulty made him acrimonious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I know perfectly that he combines the properties of a lump of\r\nice and a red-hot coal,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed.  \xe2\x80\x9cCatherine has made it thoroughly\r\nclear, and you have told me so till I am sick of it.  You needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me\r\nagain; I am perfectly satisfied.  He will never give us a penny; I regard\r\nthat as mathematically proved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman at this point had an inspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you bring a lawsuit against him?\xe2\x80\x9d  She wondered that this\r\nsimple expedient had never occurred to her before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will bring a lawsuit against _you_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, \xe2\x80\x9cif you ask me any\r\nmore such aggravating questions.  A man should know when he is beaten,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe added, in a moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cI must give her up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman received this declaration in silence, though it made her\r\nheart beat a little.  It found her by no means unprepared, for she had\r\naccustomed herself to the thought that, if Morris should decidedly not be\r\nable to get her brother\xe2\x80\x99s money, it would not do for him to marry\r\nCatherine without it.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt would not do\xe2\x80\x9d was a vague way of putting the\r\nthing; but Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s natural affection completed the idea, which,\r\nthough it had not as yet been so crudely expressed between them as in the\r\nform that Morris had just given it, had nevertheless been implied so\r\noften, in certain easy intervals of talk, as he sat stretching his legs\r\nin the Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s well-stuffed armchairs, that she had grown first to\r\nregard it with an emotion which she flattered herself was philosophic,\r\nand then to have a secret tenderness for it.  The fact that she kept her\r\ntenderness secret proves, of course, that she was ashamed of it; but she\r\nmanaged to blink her shame by reminding herself that she was, after all,\r\nthe official protector of her niece\xe2\x80\x99s marriage.  Her logic would scarcely\r\nhave passed muster with the Doctor.  In the first place, Morris _must_\r\nget the money, and she would help him to it.  In the second, it was plain\r\nit would never come to him, and it would be a grievous pity he should\r\nmarry without it\xe2\x80\x94a young man who might so easily find something better.\r\nAfter her brother had delivered himself, on his return from Europe, of\r\nthat incisive little address that has been quoted, Morris\xe2\x80\x99s cause seemed\r\nso hopeless that Mrs. Penniman fixed her attention exclusively upon the\r\nlatter branch of her argument.  If Morris had been her son, she would\r\ncertainly have sacrificed Catherine to a superior conception of his\r\nfuture; and to be ready to do so as the case stood was therefore even a\r\nfiner degree of devotion.  Nevertheless, it checked her breath a little\r\nto have the sacrificial knife, as it were, suddenly thrust into her hand.\r\n\r\nMorris walked along a moment, and then he repeated harshly: \xe2\x80\x9cI must give\r\nher up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly say it distinctly enough\xe2\x80\x94brutally and vulgarly enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was ashamed of himself, and his shame was uncomfortable; and as he was\r\nextremely intolerant of discomfort, he felt vicious and cruel.  He wanted\r\nto abuse somebody, and he began, cautiously\xe2\x80\x94for he was always\r\ncautious\xe2\x80\x94with himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you take her down a little?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake her down?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrepare her\xe2\x80\x94try and ease me off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman stopped, looking at him very solemnly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor Morris, do you know how much she loves you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to know.  I have always tried to keep from\r\nknowing.  It would be too painful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will suffer much,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must console her.  If you are as good a friend to me as you pretend\r\nto be, you will manage it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman shook her head sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk of my \xe2\x80\x98pretending\xe2\x80\x99 to like you; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to hate\r\nyou.  I can only tell her I think very highly of you; and how will that\r\nconsole her for losing you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Doctor will help you.  He will be delighted at the thing being\r\nbroken off, and, as he is a knowing fellow, he will invent something to\r\ncomfort her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will invent a new torture!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven deliver her\r\nfrom her father\xe2\x80\x99s comfort.  It will consist of his crowing over her and\r\nsaying, \xe2\x80\x98I always told you so!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris coloured a most uncomfortable red.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t console her any better than you console me, you certainly\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be of much use!  It\xe2\x80\x99s a damned disagreeable necessity; I feel it\r\nextremely, and you ought to make it easy for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will be your friend for life!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe my friend _now_!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Morris walked on.\r\n\r\nShe went with him; she was almost trembling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you like me to tell her?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t tell her, but\r\nyou can\xe2\x80\x94you can\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d  And he hesitated, trying to think what Mrs. Penniman\r\ncould do.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou can explain to her why it is.  It\xe2\x80\x99s because I can\xe2\x80\x99t bring\r\nmyself to step in between her and her father\xe2\x80\x94to give him the pretext he\r\ngrasps at\xe2\x80\x94so eagerly (it\xe2\x80\x99s a hideous sight) for depriving her of her\r\nrights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman felt with remarkable promptitude the charm of this formula.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so like you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s so finely felt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris gave his stick an angry swing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, botheration!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed perversely.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, however, was not discouraged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may turn out better than you think.  Catherine is, after all, so very\r\npeculiar.\xe2\x80\x9d  And she thought she might take it upon herself to assure him\r\nthat, whatever happened, the girl would be very quiet\xe2\x80\x94she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\nnoise.  They extended their walk, and, while they proceeded, Mrs.\r\nPenniman took upon herself other things besides, and ended by having\r\nassumed a considerable burden; Morris being ready enough, as may be\r\nimagined, to put everything off upon her.  But he was not for a single\r\ninstant the dupe of her blundering alacrity; he knew that of what she\r\npromised she was competent to perform but an insignificant fraction, and\r\nthe more she professed her willingness to serve him, the greater fool he\r\nthought her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do if you don\xe2\x80\x99t marry her?\xe2\x80\x9d she ventured to inquire in the\r\ncourse of this conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething brilliant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like me to do\r\nsomething brilliant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe idea gave Mrs. Penniman exceeding pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall feel sadly taken in if you don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to, to make up for this.  This isn\xe2\x80\x99t at all brilliant, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman mused a little, as if there might be some way of making out\r\nthat it was; but she had to give up the attempt, and, to carry off the\r\nawkwardness of failure, she risked a new inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean\xe2\x80\x94do you mean another marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris greeted this question with a reflexion which was hardly the less\r\nimpudent from being inaudible.  \xe2\x80\x9cSurely, women are more crude than men!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd then he answered audibly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman felt disappointed and snubbed, and she relieved herself in\r\na little vaguely-sarcastic cry.  He was certainly perverse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give her up, not for another woman, but for a wider career!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris\r\nannounced.\r\n\r\nThis was very grand; but still Mrs. Penniman, who felt that she had\r\nexposed herself, was faintly rancorous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean never to come to see her again?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, with some\r\nsharpness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I shall come again; but what is the use of dragging it out?  I\r\nhave been four times since she came back, and it\xe2\x80\x99s terribly awkward work.\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t keep it up indefinitely; she oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to expect that, you know.\r\nA woman should never keep a man dangling!\xe2\x80\x9d he added finely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but you must have your last parting!\xe2\x80\x9d urged his companion, in whose\r\nimagination the idea of last partings occupied a place inferior in\r\ndignity only to that of first meetings.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nHE came again, without managing the last parting; and again and again,\r\nwithout finding that Mrs. Penniman had as yet done much to pave the path\r\nof retreat with flowers.  It was devilish awkward, as he said, and he\r\nfelt a lively animosity for Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s aunt, who, as he had now quite\r\nformed the habit of saying to himself, had dragged him into the mess and\r\nwas bound in common charity to get him out of it.  Mrs. Penniman, to tell\r\nthe truth, had, in the seclusion of her own apartment\xe2\x80\x94and, I may add,\r\namid the suggestiveness of Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s, which wore in those days the\r\nappearance of that of a young lady laying out her _trousseau_\xe2\x80\x94Mrs.\r\nPenniman had measured her responsibilities, and taken fright at their\r\nmagnitude.  The task of preparing Catherine and easing off Morris\r\npresented difficulties which increased in the execution, and even led the\r\nimpulsive Lavinia to ask herself whether the modification of the young\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s original project had been conceived in a happy spirit.  A brilliant\r\nfuture, a wider career, a conscience exempt from the reproach of\r\ninterference between a young lady and her natural rights\xe2\x80\x94these excellent\r\nthings might be too troublesomely purchased.  From Catherine herself Mrs.\r\nPenniman received no assistance whatever; the poor girl was apparently\r\nwithout suspicion of her danger.  She looked at her lover with eyes of\r\nundiminished trust, and though she had less confidence in her aunt than\r\nin a young man with whom she had exchanged so many tender vows, she gave\r\nher no handle for explaining or confessing.  Mrs. Penniman, faltering and\r\nwavering, declared Catherine was very stupid, put off the great scene, as\r\nshe would have called it, from day to day, and wandered about very\r\nuncomfortably, primed, to repletion, with her apology, but unable to\r\nbring it to the light.  Morris\xe2\x80\x99s own scenes were very small ones just\r\nnow; but even these were beyond his strength.  He made his visits as\r\nbrief as possible, and while he sat with his mistress, found terribly\r\nlittle to talk about.  She was waiting for him, in vulgar parlance, to\r\nname the day; and so long as he was unprepared to be explicit on this\r\npoint it seemed a mockery to pretend to talk about matters more abstract.\r\nShe had no airs and no arts; she never attempted to disguise her\r\nexpectancy.  She was waiting on his good pleasure, and would wait\r\nmodestly and patiently; his hanging back at this supreme time might\r\nappear strange, but of course he must have a good reason for it.\r\nCatherine would have made a wife of the gentle old-fashioned\r\npattern\xe2\x80\x94regarding reasons as favours and windfalls, but no more expecting\r\none every day than she would have expected a bouquet of camellias.\r\nDuring the period of her engagement, however, a young lady even of the\r\nmost slender pretensions counts upon more bouquets than at other times;\r\nand there was a want of perfume in the air at this moment which at last\r\nexcited the girl\xe2\x80\x99s alarm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sick?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked of Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou seem so restless, and you look\r\npale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not at all well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris; and it occurred to him that, if he\r\ncould only make her pity him enough, he might get off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you are overworked; you oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to work so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must do that.\xe2\x80\x9d  And then he added, with a sort of calculated\r\nbrutality, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to owe you everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, how can you say that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am too proud,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94you are too proud!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you must take me as I am,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can never change me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to change you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gently.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will take you as you\r\nare!\xe2\x80\x9d  And she stood looking at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know people talk tremendously about a man\xe2\x80\x99s marrying a rich girl,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMorris remarked.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s excessively disagreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am not rich?\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are rich enough to make me talked about!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you are talked about.  It\xe2\x80\x99s an honour!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an honour I could easily dispense with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was on the point of asking him whether it were not a compensation for\r\nthis annoyance that the poor girl who had the misfortune to bring it upon\r\nhim, loved him so dearly and believed in him so truly; but she hesitated,\r\nthinking that this would perhaps seem an exacting speech, and while she\r\nhesitated, he suddenly left her.\r\n\r\nThe next time he came, however, she brought it out, and she told him\r\nagain that he was too proud.  He repeated that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t change, and\r\nthis time she felt the impulse to say that with a little effort he might\r\nchange.\r\n\r\nSometimes he thought that if he could only make a quarrel with her it\r\nmight help him; but the question was how to quarrel with a young woman\r\nwho had such treasures of concession.  \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you think the effort is\r\nall on your side!\xe2\x80\x9d he was reduced to exclaiming.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you believe that\r\nI have my own effort to make?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all yours now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy effort is finished and done with!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, mine is not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must bear things together,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what we ought to\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris attempted a natural smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere are some things which we can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nvery well bear together\xe2\x80\x94for instance, separation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you speak of separation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you don\xe2\x80\x99t like it; I knew you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going, Morris?\xe2\x80\x9d she suddenly asked.\r\n\r\nHe fixed his eye on her for a moment, and for a part of that moment she\r\nwas afraid of it.  \xe2\x80\x9cWill you promise not to make a scene?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA scene!\xe2\x80\x94do I make scenes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll women do!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, with the tone of large experience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t.  Where are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I should say I was going away on business, should you think it very\r\nstrange?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe wondered a moment, gazing at him.  \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94no.  Not if you will take me\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake you with me\xe2\x80\x94on business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your business?  Your business is to be with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t earn my living with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cOr rather,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried\r\nwith a sudden inspiration, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s just what I do\xe2\x80\x94or what the world says\r\nI do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis ought perhaps to have been a great stroke, but it miscarried.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine simply repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo New Orleans.  About buying some cotton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am perfectly willing to go to New Orleans.\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I would take you to a nest of yellow fever?\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nMorris.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I would expose you at such a time as this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there is yellow fever, why should you go?  Morris, you must not go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is to make six thousand dollars,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you grudge me\r\nthat satisfaction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have no need of six thousand dollars.  You think too much about\r\nmoney!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can afford to say that?  This is a great chance; we heard of it last\r\nnight.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he explained to her in what the chance consisted; and told\r\nher a long story, going over more than once several of the details, about\r\nthe remarkable stroke of business which he and his partner had planned\r\nbetween them.\r\n\r\nBut Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s imagination, for reasons best known to herself,\r\nabsolutely refused to be fired.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you can go to New Orleans, I can\r\ngo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you catch yellow fever quite as easily as\r\nI?  I am every bit as strong as you, and not in the least afraid of any\r\nfever.  When we were in Europe, we were in very unhealthy places; my\r\nfather used to make me take some pills.  I never caught anything, and I\r\nnever was nervous.  What will be the use of six thousand dollars if you\r\ndie of a fever?  When persons are going to be married they oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to\r\nthink so much about business.  You shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think about cotton, you\r\nshould think about me.  You can go to New Orleans some other time\xe2\x80\x94there\r\nwill always be plenty of cotton.  It isn\xe2\x80\x99t the moment to choose\xe2\x80\x94we have\r\nwaited too long already.\xe2\x80\x9d  She spoke more forcibly and volubly than he\r\nhad ever heard her, and she held his arm in her two hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make a scene!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cI call this a\r\nscene.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s you that are making it!  I have never asked you anything before.\r\nWe have waited too long already.\xe2\x80\x9d  And it was a comfort to her to think\r\nthat she had hitherto asked so little; it seemed to make her right to\r\ninsist the greater now.\r\n\r\nMorris bethought himself a little.  \xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then; we won\xe2\x80\x99t talk about\r\nit any more.  I will transact my business by letter.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he began to\r\nsmooth his hat, as if to take leave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t go?\xe2\x80\x9d  And she stood looking up at him.\r\n\r\nHe could not give up his idea of provoking a quarrel; it was so much the\r\nsimplest way!  He bent his eyes on her upturned face, with the darkest\r\nfrown he could achieve.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not discreet.  You mustn\xe2\x80\x99t bully me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, as usual, she conceded everything.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not discreet; I know I\r\nam too pressing.  But isn\xe2\x80\x99t it natural?  It is only for a moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a moment you may do a great deal of harm.  Try and be calmer the next\r\ntime I come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will you come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to make conditions?\xe2\x80\x9d Morris asked.  \xe2\x80\x9cI will come next\r\nSaturday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine begged; \xe2\x80\x9cI want you to come to-morrow.  I\r\nwill be very quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d she added; and her agitation had by this time become\r\nso great that the assurance was not becoming.  A sudden fear had come\r\nover her; it was like the solid conjunction of a dozen disembodied\r\ndoubts, and her imagination, at a single bound, had traversed an enormous\r\ndistance.  All her being, for the moment, centred in the wish to keep him\r\nin the room.\r\n\r\nMorris bent his head and kissed her forehead.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you are quiet, you\r\nare perfection,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut when you are violent, you are not in\r\ncharacter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s wish that there should be no violence about her save\r\nthe beating of her heart, which she could not help; and she went on, as\r\ngently as possible, \xe2\x80\x9cWill you promise to come to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said Saturday!\xe2\x80\x9d Morris answered, smiling.  He tried a frown at one\r\nmoment, a smile at another; he was at his wit\xe2\x80\x99s end.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Saturday too,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, trying to smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut to-morrow\r\nfirst.\xe2\x80\x9d  He was going to the door, and she went with him quickly.  She\r\nleaned her shoulder against it; it seemed to her that she would do\r\nanything to keep him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am prevented from coming to-morrow, you will say I have deceived\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you be prevented?  You can come if you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a busy man\xe2\x80\x94I am not a dangler!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris sternly.\r\n\r\nHis voice was so hard and unnatural that, with a helpless look at him,\r\nshe turned away; and then he quickly laid his hand on the door-knob.  He\r\nfelt as if he were absolutely running away from her.  But in an instant\r\nshe was close to him again, and murmuring in a tone none the less\r\npenetrating for being low, \xe2\x80\x9cMorris, you are going to leave me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, for a little while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor how long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill you are reasonable again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never be reasonable in that way!\xe2\x80\x9d  And she tried to keep him\r\nlonger; it was almost a struggle.  \xe2\x80\x9cThink of what I have done!\xe2\x80\x9d she broke\r\nout.  \xe2\x80\x9cMorris, I have given up everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall have everything back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say that if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean something.  What is it?\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nhas happened?\xe2\x80\x94what have I done?\xe2\x80\x94what has changed you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will write to you\xe2\x80\x94that is better,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you won\xe2\x80\x99t come back!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, bursting into tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t believe that I promise you that you\r\nshall see me again!\xe2\x80\x9d  And he managed to get away and to close the door\r\nbehind him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXX\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was almost her last outbreak of passive grief; at least, she never\r\nindulged in another that the world knew anything about.  But this one was\r\nlong and terrible; she flung herself on the sofa and gave herself up to\r\nher misery.  She hardly knew what had happened; ostensibly she had only\r\nhad a difference with her lover, as other girls had had before, and the\r\nthing was not only not a rupture, but she was under no obligation to\r\nregard it even as a menace.  Nevertheless, she felt a wound, even if he\r\nhad not dealt it; it seemed to her that a mask had suddenly fallen from\r\nhis face.  He had wished to get away from her; he had been angry and\r\ncruel, and said strange things, with strange looks.  She was smothered\r\nand stunned; she buried her head in the cushions, sobbing and talking to\r\nherself.  But at last she raised herself, with the fear that either her\r\nfather or Mrs. Penniman would come in; and then she sat there, staring\r\nbefore her, while the room grew darker.  She said to herself that perhaps\r\nhe would come back to tell her he had not meant what he said; and she\r\nlistened for his ring at the door, trying to believe that this was\r\nprobable.  A long time passed, but Morris remained absent; the shadows\r\ngathered; the evening settled down on the meagre elegance of the light,\r\nclear-coloured room; the fire went out.  When it had grown dark,\r\nCatherine went to the window and looked out; she stood there for half an\r\nhour, on the mere chance that he would come up the steps.  At last she\r\nturned away, for she saw her father come in.  He had seen her at the\r\nwindow looking out, and he stopped a moment at the bottom of the white\r\nsteps, and gravely, with an air of exaggerated courtesy, lifted his hat\r\nto her.  The gesture was so incongruous to the condition she was in, this\r\nstately tribute of respect to a poor girl despised and forsaken was so\r\nout of place, that the thing gave her a kind of horror, and she hurried\r\naway to her room.  It seemed to her that she had given Morris up.\r\n\r\nShe had to show herself half an hour later, and she was sustained at\r\ntable by the immensity of her desire that her father should not perceive\r\nthat anything had happened.  This was a great help to her afterwards, and\r\nit served her (though never as much as she supposed) from the first.  On\r\nthis occasion Dr. Sloper was rather talkative.  He told a great many\r\nstories about a wonderful poodle that he had seen at the house of an old\r\nlady whom he visited professionally.  Catherine not only tried to appear\r\nto listen to the anecdotes of the poodle, but she endeavoured to interest\r\nherself in them, so as not to think of her scene with Morris.  That\r\nperhaps was an hallucination; he was mistaken, she was jealous; people\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t change like that from one day to another.  Then she knew that she\r\nhad had doubts before\xe2\x80\x94strange suspicions, that were at once vague and\r\nacute\xe2\x80\x94and that he had been different ever since her return from Europe:\r\nwhereupon she tried again to listen to her father, who told a story so\r\nremarkably well.  Afterwards she went straight to her own room; it was\r\nbeyond her strength to undertake to spend the evening with her aunt.  All\r\nthe evening, alone, she questioned herself.  Her trouble was terrible;\r\nbut was it a thing of her imagination, engendered by an extravagant\r\nsensibility, or did it represent a clear-cut reality, and had the worst\r\nthat was possible actually come to pass?  Mrs. Penniman, with a degree of\r\ntact that was as unusual as it was commendable, took the line of leaving\r\nher alone.  The truth is, that her suspicions having been aroused, she\r\nindulged a desire, natural to a timid person, that the explosion should\r\nbe localised.  So long as the air still vibrated she kept out of the way.\r\n\r\nShe passed and repassed Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s door several times in the course of\r\nthe evening, as if she expected to hear a plaintive moan behind it.  But\r\nthe room remained perfectly still; and accordingly, the last thing before\r\nretiring to her own couch, she applied for admittance.  Catherine was\r\nsitting up, and had a book that she pretended to be reading.  She had no\r\nwish to go to bed, for she had no expectation of sleeping.  After Mrs.\r\nPenniman had left her she sat up half the night, and she offered her\r\nvisitor no inducement to remain.  Her aunt came stealing in very gently,\r\nand approached her with great solemnity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you are in trouble, my dear.  Can I do anything to help\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not in any trouble whatever, and do not need any help,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nCatherine, fibbing roundly, and proving thereby that not only our faults,\r\nbut our most involuntary misfortunes, tend to corrupt our morals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas nothing happened to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing whatever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you very sure, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd can I really do nothing for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, aunt, but kindly leave me alone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, though she had been afraid of too warm a welcome before,\r\nwas now disappointed at so cold a one; and in relating afterwards, as she\r\ndid to many persons, and with considerable variations of detail, the\r\nhistory of the termination of her niece\xe2\x80\x99s engagement, she was usually\r\ncareful to mention that the young lady, on a certain occasion, had\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chustled\xe2\x80\x9d her out of the room.  It was characteristic of Mrs. Penniman\r\nthat she related this fact, not in the least out of malignity to\r\nCatherine, whom she very sufficiently pitied, but simply from a natural\r\ndisposition to embellish any subject that she touched.\r\n\r\nCatherine, as I have said, sat up half the night, as if she still\r\nexpected to hear Morris Townsend ring at the door.  On the morrow this\r\nexpectation was less unreasonable; but it was not gratified by the\r\nreappearance of the young man.  Neither had he written; there was not a\r\nword of explanation or reassurance.  Fortunately for Catherine she could\r\ntake refuge from her excitement, which had now become intense, in her\r\ndetermination that her father should see nothing of it.  How well she\r\ndeceived her father we shall have occasion to learn; but her innocent\r\narts were of little avail before a person of the rare perspicacity of\r\nMrs. Penniman.  This lady easily saw that she was agitated, and if there\r\nwas any agitation going forward, Mrs. Penniman was not a person to\r\nforfeit her natural share in it.  She returned to the charge the next\r\nevening, and requested her niece to lean upon her\xe2\x80\x94to unburden her heart.\r\nPerhaps she should be able to explain certain things that now seemed\r\ndark, and that she knew more about than Catherine supposed.  If Catherine\r\nhad been frigid the night before, to-day she was haughty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are completely mistaken, and I have not the least idea what you\r\nmean.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you are trying to fasten on me, and I have never\r\nhad less need of any one\xe2\x80\x99s explanations in my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn this way the girl delivered herself, and from hour to hour kept her\r\naunt at bay.  From hour to hour Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity grew.  She\r\nwould have given her little finger to know what Morris had said and done,\r\nwhat tone he had taken, what pretext he had found.  She wrote to him,\r\nnaturally, to request an interview; but she received, as naturally, no\r\nanswer to her petition.  Morris was not in a writing mood; for Catherine\r\nhad addressed him two short notes which met with no acknowledgment.\r\nThese notes were so brief that I may give them entire.  \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you give\r\nme some sign that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be so cruel as you seemed on\r\nTuesday?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that was the first; the other was a little longer.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf I was\r\nunreasonable or suspicious on Tuesday\xe2\x80\x94if I annoyed you or troubled you in\r\nany way\xe2\x80\x94I beg your forgiveness, and I promise never again to be so\r\nfoolish.  I am punished enough, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.  Dear Morris, you\r\nare killing me!\xe2\x80\x9d  These notes were despatched on the Friday and Saturday;\r\nbut Saturday and Sunday passed without bringing the poor girl the\r\nsatisfaction she desired.  Her punishment accumulated; she continued to\r\nbear it, however, with a good deal of superficial fortitude.  On Saturday\r\nmorning the Doctor, who had been watching in silence, spoke to his sister\r\nLavinia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe thing has happened\xe2\x80\x94the scoundrel has backed out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Penniman, who had bethought herself what she should\r\nsay to Catherine, but was not provided with a line of defence against her\r\nbrother, so that indignant negation was the only weapon in her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has begged for a reprieve, then, if you like that better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to make you very happy that your daughter\xe2\x80\x99s affections have\r\nbeen trifled with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor; \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cfor I had foretold it!  It\xe2\x80\x99s a great\r\npleasure to be in the right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour pleasures make one shudder!\xe2\x80\x9d his sister exclaimed.\r\n\r\nCatherine went rigidly through her usual occupations; that is, up to the\r\npoint of going with her aunt to church on Sunday morning.  She generally\r\nwent to afternoon service as well; but on this occasion her courage\r\nfaltered, and she begged of Mrs. Penniman to go without her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you have a secret,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with great\r\nsignificance, looking at her rather grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have, I shall keep it!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered, turning away.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman started for church; but before she had arrived, she stopped\r\nand turned back, and before twenty minutes had elapsed she re-entered the\r\nhouse, looked into the empty parlours, and then went upstairs and knocked\r\nat Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s door.  She got no answer; Catherine was not in her room,\r\nand Mrs. Penniman presently ascertained that she was not in the house.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone to him, she has fled!\xe2\x80\x9d Lavinia cried, clasping her hands\r\nwith admiration and envy.  But she soon perceived that Catherine had\r\ntaken nothing with her\xe2\x80\x94all her personal property in her room was\r\nintact\xe2\x80\x94and then she jumped at the hypothesis that the girl had gone\r\nforth, not in tenderness, but in resentment.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe has followed him to\r\nhis own door\xe2\x80\x94she has burst upon him in his own apartment!\xe2\x80\x9d  It was in\r\nthese terms that Mrs. Penniman depicted to herself her niece\xe2\x80\x99s errand,\r\nwhich, viewed in this light, gratified her sense of the picturesque only\r\na shade less strongly than the idea of a clandestine marriage.  To visit\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s lover, with tears and reproaches, at his own residence, was an\r\nimage so agreeable to Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s mind that she felt a sort of\r\n\xc3\xa6sthetic disappointment at its lacking, in this case, the harmonious\r\naccompaniments of darkness and storm.  A quiet Sunday afternoon appeared\r\nan inadequate setting for it; and, indeed, Mrs. Penniman was quite out of\r\nhumour with the conditions of the time, which passed very slowly as she\r\nsat in the front parlour in her bonnet and her cashmere shawl, awaiting\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s return.\r\n\r\nThis event at last took place.  She saw her\xe2\x80\x94at the window\xe2\x80\x94mount the\r\nsteps, and she went to await her in the hall, where she pounced upon her\r\nas soon as she had entered the house, and drew her into the parlour,\r\nclosing the door with solemnity.  Catherine was flushed, and her eye was\r\nbright.  Mrs. Penniman hardly knew what to think.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I venture to ask where you have been?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been to take a walk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had gone to\r\nchurch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did go to church; but the service was shorter than usual.  And pray,\r\nwhere did you walk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour ignorance is most extraordinary!  Dear Catherine, you can trust\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to trust you with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith your secret\xe2\x80\x94your sorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no sorrow!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine fiercely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor child,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman insisted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t deceive me.  I know\r\neverything.  I have been requested to\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94to converse with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to converse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will relieve you.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know Shakespeare\xe2\x80\x99s lines?\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98the grief\r\nthat does not speak!\xe2\x80\x99  My dear girl, it is better as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is better?\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine asked.\r\n\r\nShe was really too perverse.  A certain amount of perversity was to be\r\nallowed for in a young lady whose lover had thrown her over; but not such\r\nan amount as would prove inconvenient to his apologists.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat you\r\nshould be reasonable,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, with some sternness.  \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nyou should take counsel of worldly prudence, and submit to practical\r\nconsiderations.  That you should agree to\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94separate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine had been ice up to this moment, but at this word she flamed up.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeparate?  What do you know about our separating?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman shook her head with a sadness in which there was almost a\r\nsense of injury.  \xe2\x80\x9cYour pride is my pride, and your susceptibilities are\r\nmine.  I see your side perfectly, but I also\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and she smiled with\r\nmelancholy suggestiveness\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI also see the situation as a whole!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis suggestiveness was lost upon Catherine, who repeated her violent\r\ninquiry.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you talk about separation; what do you know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must study resignation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman, hesitating, but\r\nsententious at a venture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cResignation to what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo a change of\xe2\x80\x94of our plans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy plans have not changed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, with a little laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but Mr. Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s have,\xe2\x80\x9d her aunt answered very gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was an imperious brevity in the tone of this inquiry, against which\r\nMrs. Penniman felt bound to protest; the information with which she had\r\nundertaken to supply her niece was, after all, a favour.  She had tried\r\nsharpness, and she had tried sternness: but neither would do; she was\r\nshocked at the girl\xe2\x80\x99s obstinacy.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, well,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cif he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntold you! . . . \xe2\x80\x9d and she turned away.\r\n\r\nCatherine watched her a moment in silence; then she hurried after her,\r\nstopping her before she reached the door.  \xe2\x80\x9cTold me what?  What do you\r\nmean?  What are you hinting at and threatening me with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it broken off?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy engagement?  Not in the least!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon in that case.  I have spoken too soon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo soon!  Soon or late,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cyou speak foolishly and\r\ncruelly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened between you, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her aunt, struck by the\r\nsincerity of this cry.  \xe2\x80\x9cFor something certainly has happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing has happened but that I love him more and more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was silent an instant.  \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that\xe2\x80\x99s the reason you\r\nwent to see him this afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine flushed as if she had been struck.  \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I did go to see him!\r\nBut that\xe2\x80\x99s my own business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then; we won\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it.\xe2\x80\x9d  And Mrs. Penniman moved\r\ntowards the door again.  But she was stopped by a sudden imploring cry\r\nfrom the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Lavinia, _where_ has he gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you admit, then, that he has gone away?  Didn\xe2\x80\x99t they know at his\r\nhouse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey said he had left town.  I asked no more questions; I was ashamed,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Catherine, simply enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t have taken so compromising a step if you had had a little\r\nmore confidence in me,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Penniman observed, with a good deal of\r\ngrandeur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it to New Orleans?\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine went on irrelevantly.\r\n\r\nIt was the first time Mrs. Penniman had heard of New Orleans in this\r\nconnexion; but she was averse to letting Catherine know that she was in\r\nthe dark.  She attempted to strike an illumination from the instructions\r\nshe had received from Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen a\r\nseparation has been agreed upon, the farther he goes away the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgreed upon?  Has he agreed upon it with you?\xe2\x80\x9d  A consummate sense of\r\nher aunt\xe2\x80\x99s meddlesome folly had come over her during the last five\r\nminutes, and she was sickened at the thought that Mrs. Penniman had been\r\nlet loose, as it were, upon her happiness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe certainly has sometimes advised with me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it you, then, that have changed him and made him so unnatural?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nCatherine cried.  \xe2\x80\x9cIs it you that have worked on him and taken him from\r\nme?  He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to you, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how you have anything to\r\ndo with what is between us!  Is it you that have made this plot and told\r\nhim to leave me?  How could you be so wicked, so cruel?  What have I ever\r\ndone to you; why can\xe2\x80\x99t you leave me alone?  I was afraid you would spoil\r\neverything; for you _do_ spoil everything you touch; I was afraid of you\r\nall the time we were abroad; I had no rest when I thought that you were\r\nalways talking to him.\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine went on with growing vehemence,\r\npouring out in her bitterness and in the clairvoyance of her passion\r\n(which suddenly, jumping all processes, made her judge her aunt finally\r\nand without appeal) the uneasiness which had lain for so many months upon\r\nher heart.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman was scared and bewildered; she saw no prospect of\r\nintroducing her little account of the purity of Morris\xe2\x80\x99s motives.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare a most ungrateful girl!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you scold me for talking\r\nwith him?  I am sure we never talked of anything but you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and that was the way you worried him; you made him tired of my very\r\nname!  I wish you had never spoken of me to him; I never asked your\r\nhelp!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for me he would never have come to the\r\nhouse, and you would never have known what he thought of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nPenniman rejoined, with a good deal of justice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish he never had come to the house, and that I never had known it!\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s better than this,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a very ungrateful girl,\xe2\x80\x9d Aunt Lavinia repeated.\r\n\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s outbreak of anger and the sense of wrong gave her, while they\r\nlasted, the satisfaction that comes from all assertion of force; they\r\nhurried her along, and there is always a sort of pleasure in cleaving the\r\nair.  But at the bottom she hated to be violent, and she was conscious of\r\nno aptitude for organised resentment.  She calmed herself with a great\r\neffort, but with great rapidity, and walked about the room a few moments,\r\ntrying to say to herself that her aunt had meant everything for the best.\r\nShe did not succeed in saying it with much conviction, but after a little\r\nshe was able to speak quietly enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not ungrateful, but I am very unhappy.  It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to be grateful\r\nfor that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cWill you please tell me where he is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea; I am not in secret correspondence with him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd Mrs. Penniman wished indeed that she were, so that she might let him\r\nknow how Catherine abused her, after all she had done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it a plan of his, then, to break off\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d  By this time Catherine had\r\nbecome completely quiet.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman began again to have a glimpse of her chance for explaining.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shrank\xe2\x80\x94he shrank,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe lacked courage, but it was the\r\ncourage to injure you!  He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to bring down on you your\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s curse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine listened to this with her eyes fixed upon her aunt, and\r\ncontinued to gaze at her for some time afterwards.  \xe2\x80\x9cDid he tell you to\r\nsay that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe told me to say many things\xe2\x80\x94all so delicate, so discriminating.  And\r\nhe told me to tell you he hoped you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t despise him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  And then she added: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd will he stay away\r\nfor ever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for ever is a long time.  Your father, perhaps, won\xe2\x80\x99t live for\r\never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you appreciate\xe2\x80\x94you understand\xe2\x80\x94even though your heart bleeds,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou doubtless think him too scrupulous.  So do I,\r\nbut I respect his scruples.  What he asks of you is that you should do\r\nthe same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine was still gazing at her aunt, but she spoke at last, as if she\r\nhad not heard or not understood her.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt has been a regular plan, then.\r\nHe has broken it off deliberately; he has given me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the present, dear Catherine.  He has put it off only.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has left me alone,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you _me_?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Penniman, with much expression.\r\n\r\nCatherine shook her head slowly.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it!\xe2\x80\x9d and she left the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXI\r\n\r\n\r\nTHOUGH she had forced herself to be calm, she preferred practising this\r\nvirtue in private, and she forbore to show herself at tea\xe2\x80\x94a repast which,\r\non Sundays, at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, took the place of dinner.  Dr. Sloper and his\r\nsister sat face to face, but Mrs. Penniman never met her brother\xe2\x80\x99s eye.\r\nLate in the evening she went with him, but without Catherine, to their\r\nsister Almond\xe2\x80\x99s, where, between the two ladies, Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s unhappy\r\nsituation was discussed with a frankness that was conditioned by a good\r\ndeal of mysterious reticence on Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am delighted he is not to marry her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond, \xe2\x80\x9cbut he ought\r\nto be horsewhipped all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, who was shocked at her sister\xe2\x80\x99s coarseness, replied that\r\nhe had been actuated by the noblest of motives\xe2\x80\x94the desire not to\r\nimpoverish Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very happy that Catherine is not to be impoverished\xe2\x80\x94but I hope he\r\nmay never have a penny too much!  And what does the poor girl say to\r\n_you_?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Almond asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe says I have a genius for consolation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\nThis was the account of the matter that she gave to her sister, and it\r\nwas perhaps with the consciousness of genius that, on her return that\r\nevening to Washington Square, she again presented herself for admittance\r\nat Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s door.  Catherine came and opened it; she was apparently\r\nvery quiet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only want to give you a little word of advice,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf your\r\nfather asks you, say that everything is going on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine stood there, with her hand on the knob looking at her aunt, but\r\nnot asking her to come in.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he will ask me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure he will.  He asked me just now, on our way home from your Aunt\r\nElizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s.  I explained the whole thing to your Aunt Elizabeth.  I said\r\nto your father I know nothing about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he will ask me when he sees\xe2\x80\x94when he sees\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d  But here\r\nCatherine stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more he sees the more disagreeable he will be,\xe2\x80\x9d said her aunt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shall see as little as possible!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him you are to be married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine softly; and she closed the door upon her aunt.\r\n\r\nShe could not have said this two days later\xe2\x80\x94for instance, on Tuesday,\r\nwhen she at last received a letter from Morris Townsend.  It was an\r\nepistle of considerable length, measuring five large square pages, and\r\nwritten at Philadelphia.  It was an explanatory document, and it\r\nexplained a great many things, chief among which were the considerations\r\nthat had led the writer to take advantage of an urgent \xe2\x80\x9cprofessional\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nabsence to try and banish from his mind the image of one whose path he\r\nhad crossed only to scatter it with ruins.  He ventured to expect but\r\npartial success in this attempt, but he could promise her that, whatever\r\nhis failure, he would never again interpose between her generous heart\r\nand her brilliant prospects and filial duties.  He closed with an\r\nintimation that his professional pursuits might compel him to travel for\r\nsome months, and with the hope that when they should each have\r\naccommodated themselves to what was sternly involved in their respective\r\npositions\xe2\x80\x94even should this result not be reached for years\xe2\x80\x94they should\r\nmeet as friends, as fellow-sufferers, as innocent but philosophic victims\r\nof a great social law.  That her life should be peaceful and happy was\r\nthe dearest wish of him who ventured still to subscribe himself her most\r\nobedient servant.  The letter was beautifully written, and Catherine, who\r\nkept it for many years after this, was able, when her sense of the\r\nbitterness of its meaning and the hollowness of its tone had grown less\r\nacute, to admire its grace of expression.  At present, for a long time\r\nafter she received it, all she had to help her was the determination,\r\ndaily more rigid, to make no appeal to the compassion of her father.\r\n\r\nHe suffered a week to elapse, and then one day, in the morning, at an\r\nhour at which she rarely saw him, he strolled into the back parlour.  He\r\nhad watched his time, and he found her alone.  She was sitting with some\r\nwork, and he came and stood in front of her.  He was going out, he had on\r\nhis hat and was drawing on his gloves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to me that you are treating me just now with all the\r\nconsideration I deserve,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I have done,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered, with her eyes on her\r\nwork.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have apparently quite banished from your mind the request I made you\r\nat Liverpool, before we sailed; the request that you would notify me in\r\nadvance before leaving my house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not left your house!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you intend to leave it, and by what you gave me to understand, your\r\ndeparture must be impending.  In fact, though you are still here in body,\r\nyou are already absent in spirit.  Your mind has taken up its residence\r\nwith your prospective husband, and you might quite as well be lodged\r\nunder the conjugal roof, for all the benefit we get from your society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try and be more cheerful!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly ought to be cheerful, you ask a great deal if you are not.\r\nTo the pleasure of marrying a brilliant young man, you add that of having\r\nyour own way; you strike me as a very lucky young lady!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine got up; she was suffocating.  But she folded her work,\r\ndeliberately and correctly, bending her burning face upon it.  Her father\r\nstood where he had planted himself; she hoped he would go, but he\r\nsmoothed and buttoned his gloves, and then he rested his hands upon his\r\nhips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a convenience to me to know when I may expect to have an\r\nempty house,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you go, your aunt marches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him at last, with a long silent gaze, which, in spite of\r\nher pride and her resolution, uttered part of the appeal she had tried\r\nnot to make.  Her father\xe2\x80\x99s cold grey eye sounded her own, and he insisted\r\non his point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it to-morrow?  Is it next week, or the week after?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not go away!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nThe Doctor raised his eyebrows.  \xe2\x80\x9cHas he backed out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have broken off my engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBroken it off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have asked him to leave New York, and he has gone away for a long\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor was both puzzled and disappointed, but he solved his\r\nperplexity by saying to himself that his daughter simply\r\nmisrepresented\xe2\x80\x94justifiably, if one would? but nevertheless\r\nmisrepresented\xe2\x80\x94the facts; and he eased off his disappointment, which was\r\nthat of a man losing a chance for a little triumph that he had rather\r\ncounted on, by a few words that he uttered aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does he take his dismissal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, less ingeniously than she had hitherto\r\nspoken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean you don\xe2\x80\x99t care?  You are rather cruel, after encouraging him\r\nand playing with him for so long!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor had his revenge, after all.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXII\r\n\r\n\r\nOUR story has hitherto moved with very short steps, but as it approaches\r\nits termination it must take a long stride.  As time went on, it might\r\nhave appeared to the Doctor that his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s account of her rupture\r\nwith Morris Townsend, mere bravado as he had deemed it, was in some\r\ndegree justified by the sequel.  Morris remained as rigidly and\r\nunremittingly absent as if he had died of a broken heart, and Catherine\r\nhad apparently buried the memory of this fruitless episode as deep as if\r\nit had terminated by her own choice.  We know that she had been deeply\r\nand incurably wounded, but the Doctor had no means of knowing it.  He was\r\ncertainly curious about it, and would have given a good deal to discover\r\nthe exact truth; but it was his punishment that he never knew\xe2\x80\x94his\r\npunishment, I mean, for the abuse of sarcasm in his relations with his\r\ndaughter.  There was a good deal of effective sarcasm in her keeping him\r\nin the dark, and the rest of the world conspired with her, in this sense,\r\nto be sarcastic.  Mrs. Penniman told him nothing, partly because he never\r\nquestioned her\xe2\x80\x94he made too light of Mrs. Penniman for that\xe2\x80\x94and partly\r\nbecause she flattered herself that a tormenting reserve, and a serene\r\nprofession of ignorance, would avenge her for his theory that she had\r\nmeddled in the matter.  He went two or three times to see Mrs.\r\nMontgomery, but Mrs. Montgomery had nothing to impart.  She simply knew\r\nthat her brother\xe2\x80\x99s engagement was broken off, and now that Miss Sloper\r\nwas out of danger she preferred not to bear witness in any way against\r\nMorris.  She had done so before\xe2\x80\x94however unwillingly\xe2\x80\x94because she was sorry\r\nfor Miss Sloper; but she was not sorry for Miss Sloper now\xe2\x80\x94not at all\r\nsorry.  Morris had told her nothing about his relations with Miss Sloper\r\nat the time, and he had told her nothing since.  He was always away, and\r\nhe very seldom wrote to her; she believed he had gone to California.\r\nMrs. Almond had, in her sister\xe2\x80\x99s phrase, \xe2\x80\x9ctaken up\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine violently\r\nsince the recent catastrophe; but though the girl was very grateful to\r\nher for her kindness, she revealed no secrets, and the good lady could\r\ngive the Doctor no satisfaction.  Even, however, had she been able to\r\nnarrate to him the private history of his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s unhappy love affair,\r\nit would have given her a certain comfort to leave him in ignorance; for\r\nMrs. Almond was at this time not altogether in sympathy with her brother.\r\nShe had guessed for herself that Catherine had been cruelly jilted\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nknew nothing from Mrs. Penniman, for Mrs. Penniman had not ventured to\r\nlay the famous explanation of Morris\xe2\x80\x99s motives before Mrs. Almond, though\r\nshe had thought it good enough for Catherine\xe2\x80\x94and she pronounced her\r\nbrother too consistently indifferent to what the poor creature must have\r\nsuffered and must still be suffering.  Dr. Sloper had his theory, and he\r\nrarely altered his theories.  The marriage would have been an abominable\r\none, and the girl had had a blessed escape.  She was not to be pitied for\r\nthat, and to pretend to condole with her would have been to make\r\nconcessions to the idea that she had ever had a right to think of Morris.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put my foot on this idea from the first, and I keep it there now,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Doctor.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see anything cruel in that; one can\xe2\x80\x99t keep it\r\nthere too long.\xe2\x80\x9d  To this Mrs. Almond more than once replied that if\r\nCatherine had got rid of her incongruous lover, she deserved the credit\r\nof it, and that to bring herself to her father\xe2\x80\x99s enlightened view of the\r\nmatter must have cost her an effort that he was bound to appreciate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am by no means sure she has got rid of him,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor said.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nis not the smallest probability that, after having been as obstinate as a\r\nmule for two years, she suddenly became amenable to reason.  It is\r\ninfinitely more probable that he got rid of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more reason you should be gentle with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _am_ gentle with her.  But I can\xe2\x80\x99t do the pathetic; I can\xe2\x80\x99t pump up\r\ntears, to look graceful, over the most fortunate thing that ever happened\r\nto her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have no sympathy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond; \xe2\x80\x9cthat was never your strong\r\npoint.  You have only to look at her to see that, right or wrong, and\r\nwhether the rupture came from herself or from him, her poor little heart\r\nis grievously bruised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHandling bruises\xe2\x80\x94and even dropping tears on them\xe2\x80\x94doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make them any\r\nbetter!  My business is to see she gets no more knocks, and that I shall\r\ncarefully attend to.  But I don\xe2\x80\x99t at all recognise your description of\r\nCatherine.  She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t strike me in the least as a young woman going\r\nabout in search of a moral poultice.  In fact, she seems to me much\r\nbetter than while the fellow was hanging about.  She is perfectly\r\ncomfortable and blooming; she eats and sleeps, takes her usual exercise,\r\nand overloads herself, as usual, with finery.  She is always knitting\r\nsome purse or embroidering some handkerchief, and it seems to me she\r\nturns these articles out about as fast as ever.  She hasn\xe2\x80\x99t much to say;\r\nbut when had she anything to say?  She had her little dance, and now she\r\nis sitting down to rest.  I suspect that, on the whole, she enjoys it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe enjoys it as people enjoy getting rid of a leg that has been\r\ncrushed.  The state of mind after amputation is doubtless one of\r\ncomparative repose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf your leg is a metaphor for young Townsend, I can assure you he has\r\nnever been crushed.  Crushed?  Not he!  He is alive and perfectly intact,\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x99s why I am not satisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you have liked to kill him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very much.  I think it is quite possible that it is all a blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA blind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn arrangement between them.  _Il fait le mort_, as they say in France;\r\nbut he is looking out of the corner of his eye.  You can depend upon it\r\nhe has not burned his ships; he has kept one to come back in.  When I am\r\ndead, he will set sail again, and then she will marry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is interesting to know that you accuse your only daughter of being\r\nthe vilest of hypocrites,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Almond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see what difference her being my only daughter makes.  It is\r\nbetter to accuse one than a dozen.  But I don\xe2\x80\x99t accuse any one.  There is\r\nnot the smallest hypocrisy about Catherine, and I deny that she even\r\npretends to be miserable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s idea that the thing was a \xe2\x80\x9cblind\xe2\x80\x9d had its intermissions and\r\nrevivals; but it may be said on the whole to have increased as he grew\r\nolder; together with his impression of Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s blooming and\r\ncomfortable condition.  Naturally, if he had not found grounds for\r\nviewing her as a lovelorn maiden during the year or two that followed her\r\ngreat trouble, he found none at a time when she had completely recovered\r\nher self-possession.  He was obliged to recognise the fact that if the\r\ntwo young people were waiting for him to get out of the way, they were at\r\nleast waiting very patiently.  He had heard from time to time that Morris\r\nwas in New York; but he never remained there long, and, to the best of\r\nthe Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s belief, had no communication with Catherine.  He was sure\r\nthey never met, and he had reason to suspect that Morris never wrote to\r\nher.  After the letter that has been mentioned, she heard from him twice\r\nagain, at considerable intervals; but on none of these occasions did she\r\nwrite herself.  On the other hand, as the Doctor observed, she averted\r\nherself rigidly from the idea of marrying other people.  Her\r\nopportunities for doing so were not numerous, but they occurred often\r\nenough to test her disposition.  She refused a widower, a man with a\r\ngenial temperament, a handsome fortune, and three little girls (he had\r\nheard that she was very fond of children, and he pointed to his own with\r\nsome confidence); and she turned a deaf ear to the solicitations of a\r\nclever young lawyer, who, with the prospect of a great practice, and the\r\nreputation of a most agreeable man, had had the shrewdness, when he came\r\nto look about him for a wife, to believe that she would suit him better\r\nthan several younger and prettier girls.  Mr. Macalister, the widower,\r\nhad desired to make a marriage of reason, and had chosen Catherine for\r\nwhat he supposed to be her latent matronly qualities; but John Ludlow,\r\nwho was a year the girl\xe2\x80\x99s junior, and spoken of always as a young man who\r\nmight have his \xe2\x80\x9cpick,\xe2\x80\x9d was seriously in love with her.  Catherine,\r\nhowever, would never look at him; she made it plain to him that she\r\nthought he came to see her too often.  He afterwards consoled himself,\r\nand married a very different person, little Miss Sturtevant, whose\r\nattractions were obvious to the dullest comprehension.  Catherine, at the\r\ntime of these events, had left her thirtieth year well behind her, and\r\nhad quite taken her place as an old maid.  Her father would have\r\npreferred she should marry, and he once told her that he hoped she would\r\nnot be too fastidious.  \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see you an honest man\xe2\x80\x99s wife\r\nbefore I die,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  This was after John Ludlow had been compelled to\r\ngive it up, though the Doctor had advised him to persevere.  The Doctor\r\nexercised no further pressure, and had the credit of not \xe2\x80\x9cworrying\xe2\x80\x9d at\r\nall over his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s singleness.  In fact he worried rather more than\r\nappeared, and there were considerable periods during which he felt sure\r\nthat Morris Townsend was hidden behind some door.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf he is not, why\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t she marry?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked himself.  \xe2\x80\x9cLimited as her intelligence may\r\nbe, she must understand perfectly well that she is made to do the usual\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine, however, became an admirable old maid.  She formed\r\nhabits, regulated her days upon a system of her own, interested herself\r\nin charitable institutions, asylums, hospitals, and aid societies; and\r\nwent generally, with an even and noiseless step, about the rigid business\r\nof her life.  This life had, however, a secret history as well as a\r\npublic one\xe2\x80\x94if I may talk of the public history of a mature and diffident\r\nspinster for whom publicity had always a combination of terrors.  From\r\nher own point of view the great facts of her career were that Morris\r\nTownsend had trifled with her affection, and that her father had broken\r\nits spring.  Nothing could ever alter these facts; they were always\r\nthere, like her name, her age, her plain face.  Nothing could ever undo\r\nthe wrong or cure the pain that Morris had inflicted on her, and nothing\r\ncould ever make her feel towards her father as she felt in her younger\r\nyears.  There was something dead in her life, and her duty was to try and\r\nfill the void.  Catherine recognised this duty to the utmost; she had a\r\ngreat disapproval of brooding and moping.  She had, of course, no faculty\r\nfor quenching memory in dissipation; but she mingled freely in the usual\r\ngaieties of the town, and she became at last an inevitable figure at all\r\nrespectable entertainments.  She was greatly liked, and as time went on\r\nshe grew to be a sort of kindly maiden aunt to the younger portion of\r\nsociety.  Young girls were apt to confide to her their love affairs\r\n(which they never did to Mrs. Penniman), and young men to be fond of her\r\nwithout knowing why.  She developed a few harmless eccentricities; her\r\nhabits, once formed, were rather stiffly maintained; her opinions, on all\r\nmoral and social matters, were extremely conservative; and before she was\r\nforty she was regarded as an old-fashioned person, and an authority on\r\ncustoms that had passed away.  Mrs. Penniman, in comparison, was quite a\r\ngirlish figure; she grew younger as she advanced in life.  She lost none\r\nof her relish for beauty and mystery, but she had little opportunity to\r\nexercise it.  With Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s later wooers she failed to establish\r\nrelations as intimate as those which had given her so many interesting\r\nhours in the society of Morris Townsend.  These gentlemen had an\r\nindefinable mistrust of her good offices, and they never talked to her\r\nabout Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s charms.  Her ringlets, her buckles and bangles,\r\nglistened more brightly with each succeeding year, and she remained quite\r\nthe same officious and imaginative Mrs. Penniman, and the odd mixture of\r\nimpetuosity and circumspection, that we have hitherto known.  As regards\r\none point, however, her circumspection prevailed, and she must be given\r\ndue credit for it.  For upwards of seventeen years she never mentioned\r\nMorris Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s name to her niece.  Catherine was grateful to her, but\r\nthis consistent silence, so little in accord with her aunt\xe2\x80\x99s character,\r\ngave her a certain alarm, and she could never wholly rid herself of a\r\nsuspicion that Mrs. Penniman sometimes had news of him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nLITTLE by little Dr. Sloper had retired from his profession; he visited\r\nonly those patients in whose symptoms he recognised a certain\r\noriginality.  He went again to Europe, and remained two years; Catherine\r\nwent with him, and on this occasion Mrs. Penniman was of the party.\r\nEurope apparently had few surprises for Mrs. Penniman, who frequently\r\nremarked, in the most romantic sites\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cYou know I am very familiar with\r\nall this.\xe2\x80\x9d  It should be added that such remarks were usually not\r\naddressed to her brother, or yet to her niece, but to fellow-tourists who\r\nhappened to be at hand, or even to the cicerone or the goat-herd in the\r\nforeground.\r\n\r\nOne day, after his return from Europe, the Doctor said something to his\r\ndaughter that made her start\xe2\x80\x94it seemed to come from so far out of the\r\npast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like you to promise me something before I die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you talk about your dying?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I am sixty-eight years old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will live a long time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I shall!  But some day I shall take a bad cold, and then it will\r\nnot matter much what any one hopes.  That will be the manner of my exit,\r\nand when it takes place, remember I told you so.  Promise me not to marry\r\nMorris Townsend after I am gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was what made Catherine start, as I have said; but her start was a\r\nsilent one, and for some moments she said nothing.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you speak of\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou challenge everything I say.  I speak of him because he\xe2\x80\x99s a topic,\r\nlike any other.  He\xe2\x80\x99s to be seen, like any one else, and he is still\r\nlooking for a wife\xe2\x80\x94having had one and got rid of her, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know by\r\nwhat means.  He has lately been in New York, and at your cousin Marian\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse; your Aunt Elizabeth saw him there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey neither of them told me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s their merit; it\xe2\x80\x99s not yours.  He has grown fat and bald, and he\r\nhas not made his fortune.
