But I can\xe2\x80\x99t trust those facts alone to steel\r\nyour heart against him, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I ask you to promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFat and bald\xe2\x80\x9d: these words presented a strange image to Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind, out of which the memory of the most beautiful young man in the\r\nworld had never faded.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you understand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nvery seldom think of Mr. Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be very easy for you to go on, then.  Promise me, after my\r\ndeath, to do the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain, for some moments, Catherine was silent; her father\xe2\x80\x99s request\r\ndeeply amazed her; it opened an old wound and made it ache afresh.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think I can promise that,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a great satisfaction,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t promise that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Doctor was silent a minute.  \xe2\x80\x9cI ask you for a particular reason.  I\r\nam altering my will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis reason failed to strike Catherine; and indeed she scarcely\r\nunderstood it.  All her feelings were merged in the sense that he was\r\ntrying to treat her as he had treated her years before.  She had suffered\r\nfrom it then; and now all her experience, all her acquired tranquillity\r\nand rigidity, protested.  She had been so humble in her youth that she\r\ncould now afford to have a little pride, and there was something in this\r\nrequest, and in her father\xe2\x80\x99s thinking himself so free to make it, that\r\nseemed an injury to her dignity.  Poor Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s dignity was not\r\naggressive; it never sat in state; but if you pushed far enough you could\r\nfind it.  Her father had pushed very far.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t promise,\xe2\x80\x9d she simply repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very obstinate,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease explain, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t explain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I can\xe2\x80\x99t promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word,\xe2\x80\x9d her father explained, \xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea how obstinate you\r\nare!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe knew herself that she was obstinate, and it gave her a certain joy.\r\nShe was now a middle-aged woman.\r\n\r\nAbout a year after this, the accident that the Doctor had spoken of\r\noccurred; he took a violent cold.  Driving out to Bloomingdale one April\r\nday to see a patient of unsound mind, who was confined in a private\r\nasylum for the insane, and whose family greatly desired a medical opinion\r\nfrom an eminent source, he was caught in a spring shower, and being in a\r\nbuggy, without a hood, he found himself soaked to the skin.  He came home\r\nwith an ominous chill, and on the morrow he was seriously ill.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\ncongestion of the lungs,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cI shall need very good\r\nnursing.  It will make no difference, for I shall not recover; but I wish\r\neverything to be done, to the smallest detail, as if I should.  I hate an\r\nill-conducted sick-room; and you will be so good as to nurse me on the\r\nhypothesis that I shall get well.\xe2\x80\x9d  He told her which of his\r\nfellow-physicians to send for, and gave her a multitude of minute\r\ndirections; it was quite on the optimistic hypothesis that she nursed\r\nhim.  But he had never been wrong in his life, and he was not wrong now.\r\nHe was touching his seventieth year, and though he had a very\r\nwell-tempered constitution, his hold upon life had lost its firmness.  He\r\ndied after three weeks\xe2\x80\x99 illness, during which Mrs. Penniman, as well as\r\nhis daughter, had been assiduous at his bedside.\r\n\r\nOn his will being opened after a decent interval, it was found to consist\r\nof two portions.  The first of these dated from ten years back, and\r\nconsisted of a series of dispositions by which he left the great mass of\r\nproperty to his daughter, with becoming legacies to his two sisters.  The\r\nsecond was a codicil, of recent origin, maintaining the annuities to Mrs.\r\nPenniman and Mrs. Almond, but reducing Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s share to a fifth of\r\nwhat he had first bequeathed her.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe is amply provided for from her\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s side,\xe2\x80\x9d the document ran, \xe2\x80\x9cnever having spent more than a\r\nfraction of her income from this source; so that her fortune is already\r\nmore than sufficient to attract those unscrupulous adventurers whom she\r\nhas given me reason to believe that she persists in regarding as an\r\ninteresting class.\xe2\x80\x9d  The large remainder of his property, therefore, Dr.\r\nSloper had divided into seven unequal parts, which he left, as\r\nendowments, to as many different hospitals and schools of medicine, in\r\nvarious cities of the Union.\r\n\r\nTo Mrs. Penniman it seemed monstrous that a man should play such tricks\r\nwith other people\xe2\x80\x99s money; for after his death, of course, as she said,\r\nit was other people\xe2\x80\x99s.  \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, you will dispute the will,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nremarked, fatuously, to Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI like it very much.  Only I wish it had\r\nbeen expressed a little differently!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was her habit to remain in town very late in the summer; she preferred\r\nthe house in Washington Square to any other habitation whatever, and it\r\nwas under protest that she used to go to the seaside for the month of\r\nAugust.  At the sea she spent her month at an hotel.  The year that her\r\nfather died she intermitted this custom altogether, not thinking it\r\nconsistent with deep mourning; and the year after that she put off her\r\ndeparture till so late that the middle of August found her still in the\r\nheated solitude of Washington Square.  Mrs. Penniman, who was fond of a\r\nchange, was usually eager for a visit to the country; but this year she\r\nappeared quite content with such rural impressions as she could gather,\r\nat the parlour window, from the ailantus-trees behind the wooden paling.\r\nThe peculiar fragrance of this vegetation used to diffuse itself in the\r\nevening air, and Mrs. Penniman, on the warm nights of July, often sat at\r\nthe open window and inhaled it.  This was a happy moment for Mrs.\r\nPenniman; after the death of her brother she felt more free to obey her\r\nimpulses.  A vague oppression had disappeared from her life, and she\r\nenjoyed a sense of freedom of which she had not been conscious since the\r\nmemorable time, so long ago, when the Doctor went abroad with Catherine\r\nand left her at home to entertain Morris Townsend.  The year that had\r\nelapsed since her brother\xe2\x80\x99s death reminded her\xe2\x80\x94of that happy time,\r\nbecause, although Catherine, in growing older, had become a person to be\r\nreckoned with, yet her society was a very different thing, as Mrs.\r\nPenniman said, from that of a tank of cold water.  The elder lady hardly\r\nknew what use to make of this larger margin of her life; she sat and\r\nlooked at it very much as she had often sat, with her poised needle in\r\nher hand, before her tapestry frame.  She had a confident hope, however,\r\nthat her rich impulses, her talent for embroidery, would still find their\r\napplication, and this confidence was justified before many months had\r\nelapsed.\r\n\r\nCatherine continued to live in her father\xe2\x80\x99s house in spite of its being\r\nrepresented to her that a maiden lady of quiet habits might find a more\r\nconvenient abode in one of the smaller dwellings, with brown stone\r\nfronts, which had at this time begun to adorn the transverse\r\nthoroughfares in the upper part of the town.  She liked the earlier\r\nstructure\xe2\x80\x94it had begun by this time to be called an \xe2\x80\x9cold\xe2\x80\x9d house\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nproposed to herself to end her days in it.  If it was too large for a\r\npair of unpretending gentlewomen, this was better than the opposite\r\nfault; for Catherine had no desire to find herself in closer quarters\r\nwith her aunt.  She expected to spend the rest of her life in Washington\r\nSquare, and to enjoy Mrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s society for the whole of this\r\nperiod; as she had a conviction that, long as she might live, her aunt\r\nwould live at least as long, and always retain her brilliancy and\r\nactivity.  Mrs. Penniman suggested to her the idea of a rich vitality.\r\n\r\nOn one of those warm evenings in July of which mention has been made, the\r\ntwo ladies sat together at an open window, looking out on the quiet\r\nSquare.  It was too hot for lighted lamps, for reading, or for work; it\r\nmight have appeared too hot even for conversation, Mrs. Penniman having\r\nlong been speechless.  She sat forward in the window, half on the\r\nbalcony, humming a little song.  Catherine was within the room, in a low\r\nrocking-chair, dressed in white, and slowly using a large palmetto fan.\r\nIt was in this way, at this season, that the aunt and niece, after they\r\nhad had tea, habitually spent their evenings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman at last, \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to say something\r\nthat will surprise you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray do,\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine answered; \xe2\x80\x9cI like surprises.  And it is so quiet\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I have seen Morris Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Catherine was surprised, she checked the expression of it; she gave\r\nneither a start nor an exclamation.  She remained, indeed, for some\r\nmoments intensely still, and this may very well have been a symptom of\r\nemotion.  \xe2\x80\x9cI hope he was well,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; he is a great deal changed.  He would like very much to\r\nsee you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather not see him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid you would say that.  But you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am\xe2\x80\x94very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI met him at Marian\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe goes to Marian\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nthey are so afraid you will meet him there.  It\xe2\x80\x99s my belief that that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhy he goes.  He wants so much to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine made no response\r\nto this, and Mrs. Penniman went on.  \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know him at first; he is\r\nso remarkably changed.  But he knew me in a minute.  He says I am not in\r\nthe least changed.  You know how polite he always was.  He was coming\r\naway when I came, and we walked a little distance together.  He is still\r\nvery handsome, only, of course, he looks older, and he is not so\xe2\x80\x94so\r\nanimated as he used to be.  There was a touch of sadness about him; but\r\nthere was a touch of sadness about him before\xe2\x80\x94especially when he went\r\naway.  I am afraid he has not been very successful\xe2\x80\x94that he has never got\r\nthoroughly established.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose he is sufficiently plodding, and\r\nthat, after all, is what succeeds in this world.\xe2\x80\x9d  Mrs. Penniman had not\r\nmentioned Morris Townsend\xe2\x80\x99s name to her niece for upwards of the fifth of\r\na century; but now that she had broken the spell, she seemed to wish to\r\nmake up for lost time, as if there had been a sort of exhilaration in\r\nhearing herself talk of him.  She proceeded, however, with considerable\r\ncaution, pausing occasionally to let Catherine give some sign.  Catherine\r\ngave no other sign than to stop the rocking of her chair and the swaying\r\nof her fan; she sat motionless and silent.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt was on Tuesday last,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mrs. Penniman, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have been hesitating ever since about telling\r\nyou.  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how you might like it.  At last I thought that it was\r\nso long ago that you would probably not have any particular feeling.  I\r\nsaw him again, after meeting him at Marian\xe2\x80\x99s.  I met him in the street,\r\nand he went a few steps with me.  The first thing he said was about you;\r\nhe asked ever so many questions.  Marian didn\xe2\x80\x99t want me to speak to you;\r\nshe didn\xe2\x80\x99t want you to know that they receive him.  I told him I was sure\r\nthat after all these years you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have any feeling about that; you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t grudge him the hospitality of his own cousin\xe2\x80\x99s house.  I said\r\nyou would be bitter indeed if you did that.  Marian has the most\r\nextraordinary ideas about what happened between you; she seems to think\r\nhe behaved in some very unusual manner.  I took the liberty of reminding\r\nher of the real facts, and placing the story in its true light.  _He_ has\r\nno bitterness, Catherine, I can assure you; and he might be excused for\r\nit, for things have not gone well with him.  He has been all over the\r\nworld, and tried to establish himself everywhere; but his evil star was\r\nagainst him.  It is most interesting to hear him talk of his evil star.\r\nEverything failed; everything but his\xe2\x80\x94you know, you remember\xe2\x80\x94his proud,\r\nhigh spirit.  I believe he married some lady somewhere in Europe.  You\r\nknow they marry in such a peculiar matter-of-course way in Europe; a\r\nmarriage of reason they call it.  She died soon afterwards; as he said to\r\nme, she only flitted across his life.  He has not been in New York for\r\nten years; he came back a few days ago.  The first thing he did was to\r\nask me about you.  He had heard you had never married; he seemed very\r\nmuch interested about that.  He said you had been the real romance of his\r\nlife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine had suffered her companion to proceed from point to point, and\r\npause to pause, without interrupting her; she fixed her eyes on the\r\nground and listened.  But the last phrase I have quoted was followed by a\r\npause of peculiar significance, and then, at last, Catherine spoke.  It\r\nwill be observed that before doing so she had received a good deal of\r\ninformation about Morris Townsend.  \xe2\x80\x9cPlease say no more; please don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfollow up that subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t it interest you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Penniman, with a certain timorous\r\narchness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt pains me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid you would say that.  But don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you could get used\r\nto it?  He wants so much to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t, Aunt Lavinia,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine, getting up from her seat.\r\nShe moved quickly away, and went to the other window, which stood open to\r\nthe balcony; and here, in the embrasure, concealed from her aunt by the\r\nwhite curtains, she remained a long time, looking out into the warm\r\ndarkness.  She had had a great shock; it was as if the gulf of the past\r\nhad suddenly opened, and a spectral figure had risen out of it.  There\r\nwere some things she believed she had got over, some feelings that she\r\nhad thought of as dead; but apparently there was a certain vitality in\r\nthem still.  Mrs. Penniman had made them stir themselves.  It was but a\r\nmomentary agitation, Catherine said to herself; it would presently pass\r\naway.  She was trembling, and her heart was beating so that she could\r\nfeel it; but this also would subside.  Then, suddenly, while she waited\r\nfor a return of her calmness, she burst into tears.  But her tears flowed\r\nvery silently, so that Mrs. Penniman had no observation of them.  It was\r\nperhaps, however, because Mrs. Penniman suspected them that she said no\r\nmore that evening about Morris Townsend.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXV\r\n\r\n\r\nHER refreshed attention to this gentleman had not those limits of which\r\nCatherine desired, for herself, to be conscious; it lasted long enough to\r\nenable her to wait another week before speaking of him again.  It was\r\nunder the same circumstances that she once more attacked the subject.\r\nShe had been sitting with her niece in the evening; only on this\r\noccasion, as the night was not so warm, the lamp had been lighted, and\r\nCatherine had placed herself near it with a morsel of fancy-work.  Mrs.\r\nPenniman went and sat alone for half an hour on the balcony; then she\r\ncame in, moving vaguely about the room.  At last she sank into a seat\r\nnear Catherine, with clasped hands, and a little look of excitement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall you be angry if I speak to you again about _him_?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nCatherine looked up at her quietly.  \xe2\x80\x9cWho is _he_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe whom you once loved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not be angry, but I shall not like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe sent you a message,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Penniman.  \xe2\x80\x9cI promised him to deliver\r\nit, and I must keep my promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn all these years Catherine had had time to forget how little she had to\r\nthank her aunt for in the season of her misery; she had long ago forgiven\r\nMrs. Penniman for taking too much upon herself.  But for a moment this\r\nattitude of interposition and disinterestedness, this carrying of\r\nmessages and redeeming of promises, brought back the sense that her\r\ncompanion was a dangerous woman.  She had said she would not be angry;\r\nbut for an instant she felt sore.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care what you do with your\r\npromise!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\nMrs. Penniman, however, with her high conception of the sanctity of\r\npledges, carried her point.  \xe2\x80\x9cI have gone too far to retreat,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nthough precisely what this meant she was not at pains to explain.  \xe2\x80\x9cMr.\r\nTownsend wishes most particularly to see you, Catherine; he believes that\r\nif you knew how much, and why, he wishes it, you would consent to do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere can be no reason,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine; \xe2\x80\x9cno good reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis happiness depends upon it.  Is not that a good reason?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs.\r\nPenniman impressively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for me.  My happiness does not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you will be happier after you have seen him.  He is going away\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94going to resume his wanderings.  It is a very lonely, restless,\r\njoyless life.  Before he goes he wishes to speak to you; it is a fixed\r\nidea with him\xe2\x80\x94he is always thinking of it.  He has something very\r\nimportant to say to you.  He believes that you never understood him\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nyou never judged him rightly, and the belief has always weighed upon him\r\nterribly.  He wishes to justify himself; he believes that in a very few\r\nwords he could do so.  He wishes to meet you as a friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine listened to this wonderful speech without pausing in her work;\r\nshe had now had several days to accustom herself to think of Morris\r\nTownsend again as an actuality.  When it was over she said simply,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease say to Mr. Townsend that I wish he would leave me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had hardly spoken when a sharp, firm ring at the door vibrated\r\nthrough the summer night.  Catherine looked up at the clock; it marked a\r\nquarter-past nine\xe2\x80\x94a very late hour for visitors, especially in the empty\r\ncondition of the town.  Mrs. Penniman at the same moment gave a little\r\nstart, and then Catherine\xe2\x80\x99s eyes turned quickly to her aunt.  They met\r\nMrs. Penniman\xe2\x80\x99s and sounded them for a moment, sharply.  Mrs. Penniman\r\nwas blushing; her look was a conscious one; it seemed to confess\r\nsomething.  Catherine guessed its meaning, and rose quickly from her\r\nchair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Penniman,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a tone that scared her companion, \xe2\x80\x9chave you\r\ntaken the _liberty_ . . . ?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest Catherine,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Mrs. Penniman, \xe2\x80\x9cjust wait till you see\r\nhim!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCatherine had frightened her aunt, but she was also frightened herself;\r\nshe was on the point of rushing to give orders to the servant, who was\r\npassing to the door, to admit no one; but the fear of meeting her visitor\r\nchecked her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Morris Townsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was what she heard, vaguely but recognisably articulated by the\r\ndomestic, while she hesitated.  She had her back turned to the door of\r\nthe parlour, and for some moments she kept it turned, feeling that he had\r\ncome in.  He had not spoken, however, and at last she faced about.  Then\r\nshe saw a gentleman standing in the middle of the room, from which her\r\naunt had discreetly retired.\r\n\r\nShe would never have known him.  He was forty-five years old, and his\r\nfigure was not that of the straight, slim young man she remembered.  But\r\nit was a very fine person, and a fair and lustrous beard, spreading\r\nitself upon a well-presented chest, contributed to its effect.  After a\r\nmoment Catherine recognised the upper half of the face, which, though her\r\nvisitor\xe2\x80\x99s clustering locks had grown thin, was still remarkably handsome.\r\nHe stood in a deeply deferential attitude, with his eyes on her face.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave ventured\xe2\x80\x94I have ventured,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; and then he paused, looking\r\nabout him, as if he expected her to ask him to sit down.  It was the old\r\nvoice, but it had not the old charm.  Catherine, for a minute, was\r\nconscious of a distinct determination not to invite him to take a seat.\r\nWhy had he come?  It was wrong for him to come.  Morris was embarrassed,\r\nbut Catherine gave him no help.  It was not that she was glad of his\r\nembarrassment; on the contrary, it excited all her own liabilities of\r\nthis kind, and gave her great pain.  But how could she welcome him when\r\nshe felt so vividly that he ought not to have come?  \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted so much\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nwas determined,\xe2\x80\x9d Morris went on.  But he stopped again; it was not easy.\r\nCatherine still said nothing, and he may well have recalled with\r\napprehension her ancient faculty of silence.  She continued to look at\r\nhim, however, and as she did so she made the strangest observation.  It\r\nseemed to be he, and yet not he; it was the man who had been everything,\r\nand yet this person was nothing.  How long ago it was\xe2\x80\x94how old she had\r\ngrown\xe2\x80\x94how much she had lived!  She had lived on something that was\r\nconnected with _him_, and she had consumed it in doing so.  This person\r\ndid not look unhappy.  He was fair and well-preserved, perfectly dressed,\r\nmature and complete.  As Catherine looked at him, the story of his life\r\ndefined itself in his eyes; he had made himself comfortable, and he had\r\nnever been caught.  But even while her perception opened itself to this,\r\nshe had no desire to catch him; his presence was painful to her, and she\r\nonly wished he would go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you not sit down?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we had better not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI offend you by coming?\xe2\x80\x9d  He was very grave; he spoke in a tone of the\r\nrichest respect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you ought to have come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid not Mrs. Penniman tell you\xe2\x80\x94did she not give you my message?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe told me something, but I did not understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would let _me_ tell you\xe2\x80\x94let me speak for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for you, perhaps, but for me.  It would be a great satisfaction\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nI have not many.\xe2\x80\x9d  He seemed to be coming nearer; Catherine turned away.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan we not be friends again?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not enemies,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cI have none but friendly feelings\r\nto you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I wonder whether you know the happiness it gives me to hear you say\r\nthat!\xe2\x80\x9d  Catherine uttered no intimation that she measured the influence\r\nof her words; and he presently went on, \xe2\x80\x9cYou have not changed\xe2\x80\x94the years\r\nhave passed happily for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have passed very quietly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have left no marks; you are admirably young.\xe2\x80\x9d  This time he\r\nsucceeded in coming nearer\xe2\x80\x94he was close to her; she saw his glossy\r\nperfumed beard, and his eyes above it looking strange and hard.  It was\r\nvery different from his old\xe2\x80\x94from his young\xe2\x80\x94face.  If she had first seen\r\nhim this way she would not have liked him.  It seemed to her that he was\r\nsmiling, or trying to smile.  \xe2\x80\x9cCatherine,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, lowering his voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never ceased to think of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say those things,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you hate me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nSomething in her tone discouraged him, but in a moment he recovered\r\nhimself.  \xe2\x80\x9cHave you still some kindness for me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why you have come here to ask me such things!\xe2\x80\x9d Catherine\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause for many years it has been the desire of my life that we should\r\nbe friends again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?  Not if you will allow it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not allow it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her again in silence.  \xe2\x80\x9cI see; my presence troubles you and\r\npains you.  I will go away; but you must give me leave to come again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t come again,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever?\xe2\x80\x94never?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made a great effort; she wished to say something that would make it\r\nimpossible he should ever again cross her threshold.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is wrong of\r\nyou.  There is no propriety in it\xe2\x80\x94no reason for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, dearest lady, you do me injustice!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris Townsend.  \xe2\x80\x9cWe have\r\nonly waited, and now we are free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou treated me badly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if you think of it rightly.  You had your quiet life with your\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x94which was just what I could not make up my mind to rob you of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I had that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris felt it to be a considerable damage to his cause that he could not\r\nadd that she had had something more besides; for it is needless to say\r\nthat he had learnt the contents of Dr. Sloper\xe2\x80\x99s will.  He was\r\nnevertheless not at a loss.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere are worse fates than that!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed, with expression; and he might have been supposed to refer to\r\nhis own unprotected situation.  Then he added, with a deeper tenderness,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatherine, have you never forgiven me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgave you years ago, but it is useless for us to attempt to be\r\nfriends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if we forget the past.  We have still a future, thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t forget\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t forget,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou treated me too\r\nbadly.  I felt it very much; I felt it for years.\xe2\x80\x9d  And then she went on,\r\nwith her wish to show him that he must not come to her this way, \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbegin again\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t take it up.  Everything is dead and buried.  It was\r\ntoo serious; it made a great change in my life.  I never expected to see\r\nyou here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are angry!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Morris, who wished immensely that he could\r\nextort some flash of passion from her mildness.  In that case he might\r\nhope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not angry.  Anger does not last, that way, for years.  But\r\nthere are other things.  Impressions last, when they have been strong.\r\nBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMorris stood stroking his beard, with a clouded eye.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you never\r\nmarried?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked abruptly.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou have had opportunities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you are rich, you are free; you had nothing to gain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had nothing to gain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Catherine.\r\n\r\nMorris looked vaguely round him, and gave a deep sigh.  \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I was in\r\nhopes that we might still have been friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI meant to tell you, by my aunt, in answer to your message\xe2\x80\x94if you had\r\nwaited for an answer\xe2\x80\x94that it was unnecessary for you to come in that\r\nhope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris.  \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse my indiscretion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bowed, and she turned away\xe2\x80\x94standing there, averted, with her eyes on\r\nthe ground, for some moments after she had heard him close the door of\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\nIn the hall he found Mrs. Penniman, fluttered and eager; she appeared to\r\nhave been hovering there under the irreconcilable promptings of her\r\ncuriosity and her dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a precious plan of yours!\xe2\x80\x9d said Morris, clapping on his hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she so hard?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Penniman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care a button for me\xe2\x80\x94with her confounded little dry manner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it very dry?\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mrs. Penniman, with solicitude.\r\n\r\nMorris took no notice of her question; he stood musing an instant, with\r\nhis hat on.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut why the deuce, then, would she never marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94why indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Penniman.  And then, as if from a sense of\r\nthe inadequacy of this explanation, \xe2\x80\x9cBut you will not despair\xe2\x80\x94you will\r\ncome back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome back?  Damnation!\xe2\x80\x9d  And Morris Townsend strode out of the house,\r\nleaving Mrs. Penniman staring.\r\n\r\nCatherine, meanwhile, in the parlour, picking up her morsel of fancy\r\nwork, had seated herself with it again\xe2\x80\x94for life, as it were.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n***END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK WASHINGTON SQUARE***\r\n\r\n\r\n******* This file should be named 2870-0.txt or 2870-0.zip *******\r\n\r\n\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\nhttp://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/2/8/7/2870\r\n\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United\r\nStates without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
